Contrapositive of Affirmative Propositions
Author states the general rule for contrapositive of affirmative categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule for contrapositive of universal affirmative propositions and identify the required modal conditions.

Core Idea
Contraposition flips the rule: affirmatives become like negatives in simple conversion.

This lesson unpacks the counterintuitive rule for contrapositive of affirmative propositions. Unlike simple conversion, where affirmatives weaken, contrapositive treats affirmatives like negatives, requiring universal and modalized originals. We'll walk through the logical shift and its concrete application.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conversion
Simple conversion (al-'aks al-mustawī) swaps subject and predicate without negation. Contraposition ('aks al-naqīḍ) swaps and negates both terms. The author stresses they follow opposite rules.
For affirmatives, simple conversion yields a weaker form (e.g., Every A is B becomes Some B is A). Contraposition preserves universality but requires modal conditions.
2
Compare the rules for affirmatives in simple conversion vs contraposition
In simple conversion, affirmatives convert to a particular. In contraposition, affirmatives convert to a universal affirmative, but only if the original is universal and modalized (necessary, perpetual, etc.). This mirrors the rule for negatives in simple conversion.
The author says: 'the judgment in affirmatives here is like the judgment in negatives there'.
3
Understand the modal conditions
The universal affirmative original must be necessary (ḍarūriyya), perpetual (dā'ima), general conditional (mashrūṭa 'āmma), or general conventional ('urfiyya 'āmma). These ensure the contrapositive holds.
These modes correspond to the conditions under which a universal negative converts in simple conversion.
4
Test with a concrete Islamic example
Take the universal affirmative 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (necessary in Sharī'a). Its contrapositive is 'Everything not forbidden is not an intoxicant'. Check that it is universal affirmative and the original is necessary.
This example illustrates the rule and the modal requirement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في الموجبات من الحمليات والمشهور في عكس النقيض عند الجمهور وهو أن الحكم فيه على خلاف الحكم في العكس المستوي؛ وذلك لأن الحكم في الموجبات هنا نحو الحكم في السوالب هناك، وفي السوالب نحو الموجبات أيضًا بحسب ما يكون في الحقيقة وبحسب ما يكون في الخارج كذلك. فالموجبات إذن بحسب ما يكون في الخارج مثلا لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية، والكلية لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها كما وجهة إلا وأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو مشروطة عامة أو عرفية عامة كما في السوالب في العكس المستوي.
Chapter: On the affirmatives from the categoricals. The well-known (view) on contraposition according to the majority is that the judgment in it is opposite to the judgment in simple conversion; because the judgment in affirmatives here is like the judgment in negatives there, and in negatives like affirmatives as well, according to what is in reality and according to what is in the exterior as such. So the affirmatives, according to what is in the exterior for example, are not converted unless they are universal, and the universal is not converted as itself in respect of mode unless it is necessary or perpetual or general conditional or general conventional, just as in the negatives in simple conversion.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage opens a chapter on the contrapositive of affirmative categorical propositions. The author explains that, according to the majority of logicians, the rule for contraposition (عكس النقيض) is opposite to that of simple conversion (العكس المستوي). In simple conversion, affirmatives convert to a weaker form, while negatives convert to a universal negative. In contraposition, the roles are reversed: the affirmative here behaves like the negative in simple conversion, and the negative behaves like the affirmative. Specifically, a universal affirmative proposition (e.g., 'Every J is B') when contraposed yields a universal affirmative with negated terms: 'Every not-B is not-J', but only if the original is necessary, perpetual, or conditioned by time or common convention. This mirrors the rule for negatives in simple conversion, where only certain modalized negatives convert. The author thereby sets the stage for the detailed proofs of each modal type.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
العكس المستوي
موجبات
ضرورية
دائمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, the contrapositive of a universal affirmative proposition is a proposition that is:
A) A universal affirmative with negated terms
B) A universal negative with the same terms
C) A particular affirmative
D) A necessary proposition

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of a universal affirmative is a universal affirmative with negated terms, but only if the original is necessary, perpetual, or conditioned by time or convention.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the universal affirmative proposition from fiqh: 'Every sale of defective goods without disclosure is invalid' (assuming it is a necessary ruling). Write its contrapositive and verify that it meets the modal condition.
?
Given the proposition 'Every act of worship requiring intention is valid only if intended' (a perpetual ruling), derive its contrapositive and explain how it helps in evaluating acts of worship without a stated intention.

Proof of Contrapositive for Necessary Universal Affirmative
Author proves contrapositive rule for necessary affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction for the contrapositive of a necessary universal affirmative proposition.

Core Idea
The contrapositive of a necessary proposition is itself necessary and affirmative, despite negated terms.

This passage demonstrates that for a necessary universal affirmative like 'Every J is B necessarily', the contrapositive 'Every not-B is not-J necessarily' follows. The proof uses a contradiction from assuming the converse is false, converting a particular affirmative, and showing it conflicts with the original necessity. This reinforces the logical principle of transposition and highlights an important nuance about affirmative propositions with negated terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition and its contrapositive
The original is 'بالضرورة كل ج ب' and the claimed contrapositive is 'بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس ب ليس ج'. Note that the latter is affirmative in quality despite the negated terms.
The contrapositive is an immediate inference, not a syllogism.
2
Assume the contrapositive is false
The argument begins 'وإلا لصدق نقيضه' which yields 'ليس كل ما هو ليس ب ليس ج بالإمكان العام' i.e., some not-B is J by general possibility.
The contradictory of the universal necessary is a particular possible.
3
Apply simple conversion to the false assumption
The particular 'بعض ما ليس ب ج بالإمكان العام' converts to 'بعض ما هو ليس ج ليس ب بالإمكان العام' as per the rule for simple conversion of particulars.
Conversion preserves truth and modality for particular affirmatives.
4
Recognize the contradiction
This converted particular contradicts the original universal necessary 'كل ج ب بالضرورة', because it asserts that some not-B is J while the original says all J are B.
The contradiction proves the contrapositive must be true.
5
Reflect on the nature of the contrapositive
The author clarifies that it is an affirmative with privative terms (موجبة معدولة), not a negative, matching the original's quality.
Do not confuse term negation with propositional quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإمكان العام أي بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» بالإمكان العام فينعكس بعض ما هو ليس «ج» ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام كما مرَّ في العكس المستوي؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، هذا خلف ولا يظن أن العكس وهو قولنا بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» هو السالبة؛ إذ هو موجبة معدولة موافقة للأصل. واللفظ مطابق للموجبة فيما مرَّ فلا يمكن أن يكون سالبة. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس، فإن الموضوع في الأصل بدون المحمول لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا، أو لا يكون. فإن كان ممكنا فلا مجال للصدق في الأصل، وإن لم يكن ممكنا البتة فلا مجال لانتفاء الصدق في العكس وإلا لا يمكن وجود ما ليس «ب» بدون ما ليس «ج» فيمكن وجود «ج» بدون «ب» مع أنه لا يمكن البتة، هذا خلف. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الحملية في قوة الشرطية كما مر، والشرطية لا يمكن تحققها إلا ولا يمكن وضع المقدم دون وضع التالي، ولا رفع التالي دون رفع المقدم فإنه إذا أمكن فلا يكون المقدم في نفس الأمر مقدما، ولا التالي تاليا كذلك، وذلك محال.
[1] As for the necessary (proposition): it is said necessarily: every J is B, necessarily: every not-B is not-J. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'Not every not-B is not-J' by general possibility, i.e., some not-B is J by general possibility. Then there would convert 'Some not-J is not-B' by general possibility, as previously in simple conversion; while it was that 'Every J is B' necessarily. This is a contradiction. And do not think that the converse, which is our statement 'necessarily every not-B is not-J', is a negative; for it is an affirmative with privative terms (ma'dūla) agreeing with the original. The wording matches the affirmative as previously, so it cannot be a negative. It is also said that truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse, because the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not. If it is possible, then there is no room for truth in the original; if it is absolutely impossible, then there is no room for the falsity of the converse. Otherwise, it would be impossible for something that is not-B to exist without being not-J, thus J could exist without B, while it is absolutely impossible – a contradiction. It is also said that the categorical is equivalent to a conditional, as previously, and the conditional cannot be realized unless the antecedent cannot be posited without the consequent, nor the consequent denied without denying the antecedent. For if it is possible, then the antecedent would not be truly antecedent, nor the consequent truly consequent, which is absurd.
Source pages p. 489

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed proof for the contrapositive of a necessary universal affirmative proposition. The author uses a proof by contradiction: assume the contrapositive is false, which yields a particular affirmative that then converts to another particular, contradicting the original necessary universal. He then clarifies that the contrapositive is an affirmative with privative terms, not a negative. Two additional arguments are given: one based on the modality of the subject-predicate relation (if the subject can exist without the predicate, the original cannot be necessary; if it cannot, then the converse must be true), and another based on the equivalence of categorical and conditional propositions. In the conditional form, the contrapositive rule corresponds to the logical principle of transposition: if P implies Q, then not-Q implies not-P. The author notes that the antecedent and consequent must be strictly linked, otherwise the conditional would not hold.

Key Terms
ضرورية
الإمكان العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof for the necessary proposition, what is the result of assuming the contrapositive is false?
A) A particular affirmative that contradicts the original
B) A universal negative that implies the original is false
C) An impossible conditional
D) The subject becomes empty

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of a necessary affirmative is also necessary, and is an affirmative proposition with negated terms, proven by contradiction and conversion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the necessary proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' (by consensus). Construct its contrapositive and check if it maintains necessity. Then test the proof by contradiction using a different example: 'Every human is mortal' (necessary). Assume the contrapositive false and derive the contradiction.

Distinction: Affirmative with Privative Terms vs. Negative
Author clarifies affirmative nature and conditional equivalence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a negative proposition and an affirmative with privative terms in modal logic, and explain the conditional form of the contrapositive rule.

Core Idea
The contrapositive is not a negative but an affirmative with privative terms, equivalent to a conditional's transposition.

The author warns readers not to mistake the contrapositive 'every not-B is not-J' for a negative proposition. It is an affirmative (موجبة معدولة) because its quality is affirmative, even though its terms are negated. He then reframes the categorical proposition as a conditional to show the logical necessity of the contrapositive: if the antecedent implies the consequent, then denying the consequent implies denying the antecedent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the distinction between quality and term negation
In logic, an affirmative proposition is one where the predicate is affirmed of the subject, even if the terms are negated. 'Every not-B is not-J' affirms not-J of not-B, so it is affirmative. The author says 'اللفظ مطابق للموجبة فيما مر'.
Quality is about the copula, not about negation in the terms.
2
Identify the common error
Students often see 'not-B' and 'not-J' and assume it is negative. The author explicitly says 'لا يظن أن العكس ... هو السالبة'.
The words 'ليس' as term negations do not make the proposition negative.
3
Translate the categorical into a conditional
The author states 'الحملية في قوة الشرطية' meaning the categorical 'Every J is B' is equivalent to the conditional 'If something is J, then it is B'.
This equivalence holds for necessary propositions as well.
4
Apply the rule of transposition
The conditional form makes clear that the contrapositive is 'If something is not-B, then it is not-J', which is the logical transposition. For necessity, this transposition must hold necessarily.
Transposition is logically equivalent to the original conditional.
5
Check the modal entailment
The author argues that if the antecedent can be true without the consequent, then the original necessary universal fails. So the contrapositive must be true.
This argument reinforces the equivalence between categorical and conditional forms in modal logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[١] أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإمكان العام أي بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» بالإمكان العام فينعكس بعض ما هو ليس «ج» ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام كما مرَّ في العكس المستوي؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة، هذا خلف ولا يظن أن العكس وهو قولنا بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» هو السالبة؛ إذ هو موجبة معدولة موافقة للأصل. واللفظ مطابق للموجبة فيما مرَّ فلا يمكن أن يكون سالبة. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس، فإن الموضوع في الأصل بدون المحمول لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا، أو لا يكون. فإن كان ممكنا فلا مجال للصدق في الأصل، وإن لم يكن ممكنا البتة فلا مجال لانتفاء الصدق في العكس وإلا لا يمكن وجود ما ليس «ب» بدون ما ليس «ج» فيمكن وجود «ج» بدون «ب» مع أنه لا يمكن البتة، هذا خلف. وقد يقال أيضًا إن الحملية في قوة الشرطية كما مر، والشرطية لا يمكن تحققها إلا ولا يمكن وضع المقدم دون وضع التالي، ولا رفع التالي دون رفع المقدم فإنه إذا أمكن فلا يكون المقدم في نفس الأمر مقدما، ولا التالي تاليا كذلك، وذلك محال.
[1] As for the necessary (proposition): it is said necessarily: every J is B, necessarily: every not-B is not-J. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'Not every not-B is not-J' by general possibility, i.e., some not-B is J by general possibility. Then there would convert 'Some not-J is not-B' by general possibility, as previously in simple conversion; while it was that 'Every J is B' necessarily. This is a contradiction. And do not think that the converse, which is our statement 'necessarily every not-B is not-J', is a negative; for it is an affirmative with privative terms (ma'dūla) agreeing with the original. The wording matches the affirmative as previously, so it cannot be a negative. It is also said that truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse, because the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not. If it is possible, then there is no room for truth in the original; if it is absolutely impossible, then there is no room for the falsity of the converse. Otherwise, it would be impossible for something that is not-B to exist without being not-J, thus J could exist without B, while it is absolutely impossible – a contradiction. It is also said that the categorical is equivalent to a conditional, as previously, and the conditional cannot be realized unless the antecedent cannot be posited without the consequent, nor the consequent denied without denying the antecedent. For if it is possible, then the antecedent would not be truly antecedent, nor the consequent truly consequent, which is absurd.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed proof for the contrapositive of a necessary universal affirmative proposition. The author uses a proof by contradiction: assume the contrapositive is false, which yields a particular affirmative that then converts to another particular, contradicting the original necessary universal. Key Takeaway
Recognizing the quality of a proposition requires looking at the copula, not just the presence of negative particles in the terms.

Proving Contrapositive of Perpetual Affirmative
Author proves the contrapositive rule for perpetual universal affirmative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to prove the contrapositive of a perpetual universal affirmative proposition using reductio and absolute generality.

Core Idea
Contrapositive of a perpetual affirmative is an affirmative with privative terms, not a negative.

In this lesson, we examine the proof for the contrapositive of a perpetual universal affirmative proposition. The author demonstrates that if 'Every J is B perpetually' is true, then 'Every not-B is not-J perpetually' must also be true, using a reductio ad absurdum argument. We will walk through the steps of this proof and clarify the modal operator used.

Guided Path
1
State the original and its contrapositive
The original is 'Every J is B perpetually' (دائما كل ج ب). The claimed contrapositive is 'Every not-B is not-J perpetually' (دائما كل ما ليس ب ليس ج). Note the modal 'perpetually' applies to both.
Perpetual means true at all times, not necessarily.
2
Assume the contrapositive false
Assume 'Every not-B is not-J perpetually' is false. Then its contradictory is true: 'Some not-B is J' by absolute generality (بالإطلاق العام). This is the only way to contradict a perpetual universal affirmative.
Absolute generality (الإطلاق العام) is the modal for perpetual propositions.
3
Convert the contradictory
From 'Some not-B is J' by absolute generality, convert (by conversion per accidens) to 'Some J is not-B' by absolute generality. The conversion is valid because the proposition is affirmative particular.
Conversion preserves the modal of absolute generality.
4
Derive contradiction
Now we have 'Some J is not-B' by absolute generality, but the original says 'Every J is B perpetually'. Perpetual implies at all times, so no J is not-B at any time. Hence we have a contradiction.
The conclusion contradicts the original, so the assumption is false.
5
State the contrapositive and its form
Therefore the contrapositive 'Every not-B is not-J perpetually' must be true. Note it is an affirmative with privative terms (موجبة معدولة), not a negative. This corrects a common mistake.
Contrapositive of a perpetual affirmative remains affirmative with negated terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] وأما الدائمة فيقال دائما كل «ج» «ب»، دائما كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإطلاق العام أي بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» بالإطلاق العام فينعكس بعض ما هو «ج» ليس «ب» بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» دائما، هذا خلف. ثمَّ الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس لما مرَّ من قبل أن الموضوع في الأصل بدون المحمول لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا أو لا يكون إلى آخر ما مر، والعكس في الدائمة هو الموجبة المعدولة كذلك لا السالبة كما هي في اعتقاد البعض منهم.
[2] As for the perpetual (proposition): it is said perpetually: every J is B, perpetually: every not-B is not-J. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is 'Not every not-B is not-J' by absolute generality, i.e., some not-B is J by absolute generality. Then there would convert 'Some J is not-B' by absolute generality; while it was that 'Every J is B' perpetually. This is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse, as previously stated, because the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not, etc. as previously. And the converse in the perpetual is the affirmative with privative terms as well, not the negative, as some believe.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the contrapositive of a perpetual universal affirmative (e.g., 'Every J is B always'). The argument parallels the necessary case but uses 'absolute generality' (الإطلاق العام) instead of 'general possibility'. The perpetual proposition is one that holds at all times but not necessarily; it is a temporal universality. The proof goes: assume the contrapositive 'perpetually every not-B is not-J' is false. Then its contradictory 'some not-B is J' is true by absolute generality. This converts to 'some J is not-B' by absolute generality, contradicting the original universal perpetual. The author then reiterates the argument that truth in the original requires truth in the converse, based on the possibility or impossibility of the subject existing without the predicate. He also notes that the contrapositive is an affirmative with privative terms, not a negative, correcting a mistaken belief.

Key Terms
دائمة
الإطلاق العام
موجبة معدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proof of contrapositive for a perpetual universal affirmative, what modal operator is used for the contradictory of the assumption?
A) General possibility (الإمكان العام)
B) Absolute generality (الإطلاق العام)
C) Necessity (الضرورة)
D) Conventional (المتعارفة)

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive of a perpetual universal affirmative is itself a perpetual affirmative with negated terms, proven by reductio using absolute generality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Contrapositive of General Conditional Affirmative
Proof of contrapositive rule for general conditional universal affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to derive the contrapositive of a general conditional universal affirmative proposition and prove its validity using contradiction.

Core Idea
Contrapositive preserves truth: if every J is B as long as it is J, then every not-B is not-J as long as it is not-B.

This lesson demonstrates how the contrapositive of a general conditional proposition is proven valid. The author uses a reductio argument: assume the contrapositive false, derive a contradiction with the original. Mastering this technique enables you to transform conditionally quantified statements in Islamic logical arguments—for example, in kalam proofs or fiqh maxims—without losing certainty.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The proposition is a general conditional universal affirmative: 'Every J is B, as long as it is J.' Here 'as long as it is J' is the condition restricting the necessity.
2
State the target contrapositive
The contrapositive you aim to prove is: 'Every not-B is not-J, as long as it is not-B.' Note that the condition changes from 'as long as it is J' to 'as long as it is not-B'.
3
Assume the contrapositive false
If the contrapositive were false, its contradictory would be true: 'Some not-B is not not-J when it is not-B', i.e., 'Some not-B is J when it is not-B' (by general possibility).
The contradictory of a universal affirmative is a particular negative, but here the author uses 'not some not-B is not-J' which simplifies to 'some not-B is J'.
4
Apply conversion to the assumed false
From 'Some not-B is J when it is not-B', by conversion of a particular affirmative, we get 'Some J is not-B when it is not-B' (also by general possibility).
Conversion preserves truth for particular affirmatives.
5
Derive contradiction with original
The original says 'Every J is B necessarily as long as it is J'. The converted proposition says 'Some J is not-B when it is not-B', which contradicts the original because it asserts that a J exists that is not-B under a condition that is a subset of the original condition.
The contradiction shows the contrapositive cannot be false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٣] وأما المشروطة العامة فيقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» ما دام «ج»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس «ب» ليس «ج» ما دام ليس «ب» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» حين هو ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام أي بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» حين هو ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام. فينعكس بعض ما هو «ج» ليس «ب» بالإمكان العام؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» بالضرورة هذا خلف. ثمَّ الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس لما مرَّ وهو أن الموضوع بالوصف الذي مرَّ ذكره لا يخلو من أن يكون ممكنا بدون المحمول أو لا يكون إلى آخر ما مر، والعكس هو الموجبة أيضًا لا السالبة.
[3] As for the general conditional (proposition): it is said necessarily: every J is B as long as it is J, necessarily: every not-B is not-J as long as it is not-B. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement: 'Not some not-B is not-J when it is not-B' by general possibility, i.e., some not-B is J when it is not-B by general possibility. Then there would convert 'Some J is not-B' by general possibility; while it was that 'Every J is B' necessarily. This is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse, as previously mentioned, which is that the subject with the description mentioned earlier is either possible without the predicate or not, etc. as previously. And the converse is the affirmative as well, not the negative.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage provides the proof for the contrapositive of a general conditional universal affirmative proposition. The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) is a proposition that holds necessarily with a condition: 'Every J is B necessarily as long as it is J'. The contrapositive is 'Every not-B is not-J necessarily as long as it is not-B'. The proof follows the same pattern as before: assume the contrapositive is false, leading to a conversion that contradicts the original. The author uses 'general possibility' (الإمكان العام) for the contradictory, and the conversion yields a particular proposition that conflicts with the original universal conditional. The author again reiterates the argument about the possibility of the subject without the predicate, and confirms that the contrapositive is affirmative, not negative.

Contraposition of the General Conventional Affirmative
Author begins the contrapositive rule for general conventional affirmative propositions, text incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and construct the contrapositive of a general conventional universal affirmative proposition and verify its validity using the pattern of proof by contradiction.

Core Idea
The general conventional proposition's contrapositive mirrors its meaning under the same condition.

In this lesson, we examine the contrapositive (عكس النقيض) rule for the general conventional ('urfiyya 'āmma) universal affirmative proposition. This type of proposition is used in Islamic jurisprudence and theology when a ruling is tied to a specific description or condition based on convention, e.g., 'every scholar is knowledgeable as long as they are a scholar'. The text begins the proof that its contrapositive follows necessarily, but the proof is incomplete; we will complete it using the established pattern from previous lessons.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
The original proposition is of the form: 'Every J is B as long as it is J' (كل ج ب ما دام ج). This is a universal affirmative with a temporal condition ('as long as'). Note that it is not a categorical unconditional proposition; it holds only while the subject possesses the description J.
The condition 'ما دام' (as long as) is crucial; it limits the scope of the judgment.
2
Formulate the contrapositive
The contrapositive is formed by swapping the subject and predicate, negating both, and retaining the condition: 'Every not-B is not-J as long as it is not-B' (كل ما هو ليس ب ليس ج ما دام ليس ب). This statement should be equivalent to the original.
The condition now applies to the new subject (not-B) in the same manner.
3
Test for equivalence via contradiction
To prove the contrapositive follows, assume the opposite: the contrapositive is false by taking its contradictory. The contradictory of 'every not-B is not-J as long as it is not-B' is 'some not-B is J as long as it is not-B' (or as long as it is not-B). This statement converts to 'some J is not-B as long as it is J' (by conversion of the temporal proposition), which contradicts the original 'every J is B as long as it is J'.
This pattern mirrors the proof for categorical and conditional propositions; the key is handling the condition consistently.
4
Verify the result
Since assuming the contrapositive false leads to a contradiction, the contrapositive must be true whenever the original is true. Thus, the contrapositive rule holds for general conventional affirmatives.
The text cuts off at 'ليس بعض', but the rest of the proof can be reconstructed following this pattern.

[4] As for the general conventional (proposition): it is said: every J is B as long as it is J, every not-B is not-J as long as it is not-B. Otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Not some...'

Conversion of Perpetual Propositions
Author explains conversion of perpetual proposition with proof by contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a perpetual proposition (دائمة) into its converse correctly, distinguishing between the correct affirmative-with-negated-predicate form and the mistaken negative form.

Core Idea
Perpetual propositions convert to affirmatives with negated predicates under the absolute modality.

In the previous lesson, we learned the general principle of conversion for categorical propositions. Now we turn to modal propositions, beginning with the perpetual (دائمة). The author explains that a perpetual universal affirmative 'Always every J is B' converts not to a universal negative but to a universal affirmative with a negated predicate under the absolute modality. This is a subtle but crucial point for modal syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the perpetual proposition form
The proposition is of the form 'دائما كل ج ب' (Always every J is B). Observe the quantifier (كل) and the modality (دائما).
The term 'دائما' means 'always' or 'perpetual'.
2
State the converse according to the passage
The author states the converse as 'دائما كل ما ليس ب ليس ج' (Always everything that is not B is not J). This is an affirmative proposition with a negated predicate.
Some mistakenly think the converse is a negative, but the author corrects this.
3
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory is true: 'Not every not-B is not J' under the absolute modality, which implies 'Some not-B is J'. By simple conversion, this becomes 'Some J is not B', contradicting the original that every J is B always.
The contradiction shows that the converse must be true.
4
Note the correct converse form
The converse is an affirmative with a negated predicate, not a negative proposition. This is the key point.
The modality remains perpetual (دائما).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

ليس «ج» وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) بالإطلاق العام أي بعض ما ليس (ب) (ج) بالإطلاق العام فينعكس بعض ما هو «ج» ليس «ب» بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان كل «ج» «ب» دائما، هذا خلف. ثمَّ الصدق في كل ما هو ليس ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس (ب) وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» حين هو ليس «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وإنه يفضي إلى المحال لما مر. ولأن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس بالوجه الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس أصلا عند بعضهم لما أن عكسه يختلف باختلاف المواد فلا يمكن أن يكون عكسه هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس إلى العامة كما في العكس المستوي، وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» ب» لا دائما بل ما دام «ج»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس (ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض لما مر. فلا تنعكس إذن كنفسها ولا كعامتها بل في البعض كالنفس وفي البعض كالعامة. وأما العرفية الخاصة فالكلام فيها نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة، والمثال المذكور في المشروطة الخاصة وعكسها فذلك بدون لفظة الضرورة المثال للعرفية الخاصة. وأما القضايا الباقية من الموجبات فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية الخاصة، فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل ما ليس «ب» ليس (ج) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض ما هو ليس «ب» هو «ج» بالضرورة، ولو أخذ نقيض المحمول موضوعا موقتاً فذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الموضوع في عكس النقيض بعض المحمول. وذلك لا يكون نقيض المحمول؛ إذ المحمول هو الباء مطلقاً لا الباء المقيد بقيد كونه في ذلك الوقت. وكذلك إذا كان العكس ضروريًا في الوضع أو دائما كما يقال كل ما هو ليس «ب» بالضرورة أو دائما
Positing the antecedent without positing the consequent, and not lifting the consequent without lifting the antecedent, because if it were possible, then the antecedent would not in itself be antecedent, nor the consequent consequent, and that is impossible. As for the perpetual [proposition], it is said: Always every J is B, always everything that is not B is not J; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not every not-B is not J' under the absolute modality, i.e., some not-B is J under the absolute modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the absolute modality, whereas it was always every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not, etc. And the converse in the perpetual is the affirmative with a negated predicate, not the negative, as some believe. As for the general conditional [proposition], it is said: Necessarily every J is B as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the general possible modality, i.e., some not-B is J at the time it is not B under the general possible modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the general possible modality, whereas it was necessarily every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject under the description mentioned is either possible without the predicate or not, etc. And the converse is also the affirmative, not the negative. As for the general customary [proposition], it is said: Every J is B as long as it is J; everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the absolute modality, and that leads to impossibility for what was previously mentioned. And because truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse in the manner previously mentioned. As for the particular conditional [proposition], it does not convert at all according to some, because its converse varies according to the materials, so it cannot be that its converse is this and not that, and vice versa. According to some, it converts to the general [proposition] as in simple conversion. According to some, it always converts partially but not always partially, as is said: Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B, always partially not always partially, for what was previously mentioned. So it does not convert as itself nor as its general, but partially as itself and partially as the general. As for the particular customary [proposition], the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the particular conditional, and the example mentioned for the particular conditional and its converse is without the word 'necessity' as an example for the particular customary. As for the remaining affirmative propositions, they are not convertible because conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, namely the particular temporal [proposition]. For it is true to say 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time', but it is not true to say 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at such a time', because it is possible that some not-B is J by necessity. And if the negated predicate were taken as a subject temporally restricted, that would not be conversion of the contradictory, because it is necessary that the subject in the conversion of the contradictory be part of the predicate. And that is not the contradictory of the predicate, since the predicate is B absolutely, not B restricted by the condition of being at that time. Similarly, if the converse is necessary in the straightforward sense or perpetual, as is said 'Everything that is not B is necessarily or always'.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules of conversion (عكس) for various types of quantified and modal propositions in Aristotelian logic. The author first states a general principle about antecedent-consequent relationships, then proceeds to discuss each type. The perpetual proposition (دائمة) converts to an affirmative with a negated predicate under the absolute modality, not to a negative. The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) converts similarly but under the general possible modality. The general customary (عرفية عامة) converts under the absolute modality. For the particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة), there is disagreement: some say it does not convert at all; some say it converts to the general; some say it partially converts as both itself and the general. The particular customary (عرفية خاصة) is similar. Finally, the remaining affirmatives, specifically the particular temporal (وقتية خاصة), do not convert because the temporal restriction on the subject cannot be preserved in the converse. The author explains that if you try to convert 'Necessarily every J is B at time t', the converse 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J at time t' is false because it is possible that some not-B is necessarily J at that time. The argument uses the notion that the predicate in the original is unrestricted, so the converse would require a different temporal condition. This lesson is essential for understanding how to correctly perform immediate inference with modal propositions, a key skill in constructing valid syllogisms in modal logic.

Key Terms
دائمة
عكس
الإطلاق العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) ليس دائما كل حكم شرعي موضوع
D) بعض ما ليس بموضوع ليس حكما شرعيا بالإطلاق العام

Key Takeaway
The perpetual proposition converts to an affirmative with a negated predicate under the same perpetual modality.

Conversion of General Conditional and Customary
Author explains conversion of general conditional and general customary with their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert general conditional (مشروطة عامة) and general customary (عرفية عامة) propositions, noting the change in modality from necessary to possible absolute for conditional, and customary to absolute for customary.

Core Idea
General conditional and customary propositions convert to partial affirmatives under a weaker modality.

Having understood perpetual conversion, we now examine two types of conditional propositions: the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) which carries necessity during the time of being J, and the general customary (عرفية عامة) which carries mere customary truth. The author shows that both convert to a partial affirmative under a more general modality, not a universal proposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general conditional form
The proposition is 'بالضرورة كل ج ب ما دام ج' (Necessarily every J is B as long as it is J). The condition 'ما دام ج' restricts the time to the duration of J.
2
Understand the proof by contradiction
Assume the converse is false. Then its contradictory is true: 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the general possible modality, which implies 'Some not-B is J when it is not B'. This converts to 'Some J is not B' under general possible, contradicting the original universal necessary.
The modality changes from necessary (ضروري) to possible (إمكان عام).
3
Note the converse form for conditional
The converse is 'بعض ما هو ج ليس ب بالإمكان العام' (Some J is not B under general possible). It is a particular affirmative, not a universal.
The author emphasizes it is affirmative, not negative.
4
Apply the same reasoning to general customary
For 'كل ج ب ما دام ج' (without necessity), the converse is 'بعض ما هو ج ليس ب بالإطلاق العام' (Some J is not B under absolute modality). The proof is analogous.
The modality becomes absolute (إطلاق عام), which is the weakest.

كل ما هو ليس ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس (ب) وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» حين هو ليس «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وإنه يفضي إلى المحال لما مر. ولأن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس بالوجه الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس أصلا عند بعضهم لما أن عكسه يختلف باختلاف المواد فلا يمكن أن يكون عكسه هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس إلى العامة كما في العكس المستوي، وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» ب» لا دائما بل ما دام «ج»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس (ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض لما مر. فلا تنعكس إذن كنفسها ولا كعامتها بل في البعض كالنفس وفي البعض كالعامة. وأما العرفية الخاصة فالكلام فيها نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة، والمثال المذكور في المشروطة الخاصة وعكسها فذلك بدون لفظة الضرورة المثال للعرفية الخاصة. وأما القضايا الباقية من الموجبات فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية الخاصة، فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل ما ليس «ب» ليس (ج) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض ما هو ليس «ب» هو «ج» بالضرورة، ولو أخذ نقيض المحمول موضوعا موقتاً فذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الموضوع في عكس النقيض بعض المحمول. وذلك لا يكون نقيض المحمول؛ إذ المحمول هو الباء مطلقاً لا الباء المقيد بقيد كونه في ذلك الوقت. وكذلك إذا كان العكس ضروريًا في الوضع أو دائما كما يقال كل ما هو ليس «ب» بالضرورة أو دائما
Positing the antecedent without positing the consequent, and not lifting the consequent without lifting the antecedent, because if it were possible, then the antecedent would not in itself be antecedent, nor the consequent consequent, and that is impossible. As for the perpetual [proposition], it is said: Always every J is B, always everything that is not B is not J; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not every not-B is not J' under the absolute modality, i.e., some not-B is J under the absolute modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the absolute modality, whereas it was always every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not, etc. And the converse in the perpetual is the affirmative with a negated predicate, not the negative, as some believe. As for the general conditional [proposition], it is said: Necessarily every J is B as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the general possible modality, i.e., some not-B is J at the time it is not B under the general possible modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the general possible modality, whereas it was necessarily every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject under the description mentioned is either possible without the predicate or not, etc. And the converse is also the affirmative, not the negative. As for the general customary [proposition], it is said: Every J is B as long as it is J; everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the absolute modality, and that leads to impossibility for what was previously mentioned. And because truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse in the manner previously mentioned. As for the particular conditional [proposition], it does not convert at all according to some, because its converse varies according to the materials, so it cannot be that its converse is this and not that, and vice versa. According to some, it converts to the general [proposition] as in simple conversion. According to some, it always converts partially but not always partially, as is said: Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B, always partially not always partially, for what was previously mentioned. So it does not convert as itself nor as its general, but partially as itself and partially as the general. As for the particular customary [proposition], the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the particular conditional, and the example mentioned for the particular conditional and its converse is without the word 'necessity' as an example for the particular customary. As for the remaining affirmative propositions, they are not convertible because conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, namely the particular temporal [proposition]. For it is true to say 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time', but it is not true to say 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at such a time', because it is possible that some not-B is J by necessity. And if the negated predicate were taken as a subject temporally restricted, that would not be conversion of the contradictory, because it is necessary that the subject in the conversion of the contradictory be part of the predicate. And that is not the contradictory of the predicate, since the predicate is B absolutely, not B restricted by the condition of being at that time. Similarly, if the converse is necessary in the straightforward sense or perpetual, as is said 'Everything that is not B is necessarily or always'.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules of conversion (عكس) for various types of quantified and modal propositions in Aristotelian logic. The author first states a general principle about antecedent-consequent relationships, then proceeds to discuss each type. The perpetual proposition (دائمة) converts to an affirmative with a negated predicate under the absolute modality, not to a negative. The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) converts similarly but under the general possible modality. The general customary (عرفية عامة) converts under the absolute modality. For the particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة), there is disagreement: some say it does not convert at all; some say it converts to the general; some say it partially converts as both itself and the general. The particular customary (عرفية خاصة) is similar. Finally, the remaining affirmatives, specifically the particular temporal (وقتية خاصة), do not convert because the temporal restriction on the subject cannot be preserved in the converse. The author explains that if you try to convert 'Necessarily every J is B at time t', the converse 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J at time t' is false because it is possible that some not-B is necessarily J at that time. The argument uses the notion that the predicate in the original is unrestricted, so the converse would require a different temporal condition. This lesson is essential for understanding how to correctly perform immediate inference with modal propositions, a key skill in constructing valid syllogisms in modal logic.

Key Terms
مشروطة عامة
عرفية عامة
الإمكان العام
الإطلاق العام

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) A universal customary proposition
D) A particular necessary proposition

Key Takeaway
General conditional and customary propositions convert to partial affirmatives under a weaker (absolute or possible) modality.

Conversion of Particular Conditional and Customary
Author discusses three views on conversion of particular conditional and customary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the disagreement about the conversion of particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة) and particular customary (عرفية خاصة) propositions, and identify the three views: no conversion, conversion to general, or partial conversion.

Core Idea
The conversion of particular conditional propositions is disputed; some deny it entirely, others allow partial or complete conversion.

The previous lessons covered perpetual and general conditionals, which have fixed conversion rules. Now we encounter a more complex case: the particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة), which adds the condition 'not always but as long as it is J'. The author reports three opinions on its conversion, indicating the difficulty. The same applies to the particular customary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular conditional form
The proposition is 'بالضرورة كل ج ب لا دائما بل ما دام ج' (Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as it is J). The 'not always' part makes it particular.
2
Understand the three views
First view: it does not convert at all because the converse varies with the material. Second view: it converts to the general conditional (مشروطة عامة) as in simple conversion. Third view: it converts partially, sometimes as itself, sometimes as the general.
The author mentions these views without endorsing any.
3
Examine the example for the third view
The example: original 'بالضرورة كل ج ب لا دائما بل ما دام ج', converse 'بالضرورة كل ما ليس ب ليس ج ما دام ليس ب، دائمًا في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض' (always partially, not always partially).
This shows the converse is not uniform.
4
Apply to particular customary
The same discussion applies, but without the word 'necessity'. The example is analogous.

Why Particular Temporal Propositions Do Not Convert
Author explains impossibility of conversion for particular temporal and remaining affirmatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why particular temporal (وقتية خاصة) and other remaining affirmative propositions do not convert.

Core Idea
Particular temporal propositions do not convert because the temporal restriction cannot be preserved in the converse.

Finally, the author addresses the remaining affirmative modal propositions, specifically the particular temporal (وقتية خاصة). Unlike the previous types, these do not convert at all. The reason is structural: the temporal condition on the subject cannot be transferred to the predicate in the converse.

Guided Path
1
Identify the particular temporal form
The proposition is 'بالضرورة كل ج ب لا دائما بل وقت كذا' (Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time). The time is specified.
2
Attempt the natural converse
One might guess the converse is 'بالضرورة كل ما ليس ب ليس ج لا دائما بل وقت كذا' (Necessarily everything that is not B is not J at that time). But the author says this is false.
3
Understand the impossibility
It is possible that some not-B is necessarily J at that time. For example, if B = 'temporal' and J = 'event', then some non-temporal (God) is not event at that time, but the original might still be true. The converse fails.
The predicate in the original is absolute, so its negation is also absolute, not temporally restricted.
4
Note the reason about the predicate
The author explains that if we take the negated predicate as a subject with a temporal restriction, that is not the conversion of the contradictory. The predicate in the original is B absolutely, not B restricted to that time.
Hence the converse cannot be valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

كل ما هو ليس ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس (ب) وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» حين هو ليس «ب» بالإطلاق العام، وإنه يفضي إلى المحال لما مر. ولأن الصدق في الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون بدون الصدق في العكس بالوجه الذي مرَّ ذكره. وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس أصلا عند بعضهم لما أن عكسه يختلف باختلاف المواد فلا يمكن أن يكون عكسه هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس إلى العامة كما في العكس المستوي، وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» ب» لا دائما بل ما دام «ج»، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس (ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض لما مر. فلا تنعكس إذن كنفسها ولا كعامتها بل في البعض كالنفس وفي البعض كالعامة. وأما العرفية الخاصة فالكلام فيها نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة، والمثال المذكور في المشروطة الخاصة وعكسها فذلك بدون لفظة الضرورة المثال للعرفية الخاصة. وأما القضايا الباقية من الموجبات فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية الخاصة، فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل ما ليس «ب» ليس (ج) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض ما هو ليس «ب» هو «ج» بالضرورة، ولو أخذ نقيض المحمول موضوعا موقتاً فذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الموضوع في عكس النقيض بعض المحمول. وذلك لا يكون نقيض المحمول؛ إذ المحمول هو الباء مطلقاً لا الباء المقيد بقيد كونه في ذلك الوقت. وكذلك إذا كان العكس ضروريًا في الوضع أو دائما كما يقال كل ما هو ليس «ب» بالضرورة أو دائما
Positing the antecedent without positing the consequent, and not lifting the consequent without lifting the antecedent, because if it were possible, then the antecedent would not in itself be antecedent, nor the consequent consequent, and that is impossible. As for the perpetual [proposition], it is said: Always every J is B, always everything that is not B is not J; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not every not-B is not J' under the absolute modality, i.e., some not-B is J under the absolute modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the absolute modality, whereas it was always every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject in the original without the predicate is either possible or not, etc. And the converse in the perpetual is the affirmative with a negated predicate, not the negative, as some believe. As for the general conditional [proposition], it is said: Necessarily every J is B as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the general possible modality, i.e., some not-B is J at the time it is not B under the general possible modality. Then it converts to 'Some J is not B' under the general possible modality, whereas it was necessarily every J is B; this is a contradiction. Then truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse for what was previously mentioned: that the subject under the description mentioned is either possible without the predicate or not, etc. And the converse is also the affirmative, not the negative. As for the general customary [proposition], it is said: Every J is B as long as it is J; everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Not some not-B is not J at the time it is not B' under the absolute modality, and that leads to impossibility for what was previously mentioned. And because truth in the original cannot be without truth in the converse in the manner previously mentioned. As for the particular conditional [proposition], it does not convert at all according to some, because its converse varies according to the materials, so it cannot be that its converse is this and not that, and vice versa. According to some, it converts to the general [proposition] as in simple conversion. According to some, it always converts partially but not always partially, as is said: Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as it is J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J as long as it is not B, always partially not always partially, for what was previously mentioned. So it does not convert as itself nor as its general, but partially as itself and partially as the general. As for the particular customary [proposition], the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the particular conditional, and the example mentioned for the particular conditional and its converse is without the word 'necessity' as an example for the particular customary. As for the remaining affirmative propositions, they are not convertible because conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, namely the particular temporal [proposition]. For it is true to say 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time', but it is not true to say 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at such a time', because it is possible that some not-B is J by necessity. And if the negated predicate were taken as a subject temporally restricted, that would not be conversion of the contradictory, because it is necessary that the subject in the conversion of the contradictory be part of the predicate. And that is not the contradictory of the predicate, since the predicate is B absolutely, not B restricted by the condition of being at that time. Similarly, if the converse is necessary in the straightforward sense or perpetual, as is said 'Everything that is not B is necessarily or always'.
Source pages p. 490

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules of conversion (عكس) for various types of quantified and modal propositions in Aristotelian logic. The author first states a general principle about antecedent-consequent relationships, then proceeds to discuss each type. The perpetual proposition (دائمة) converts to an affirmative with a negated predicate under the absolute modality, not to a negative. The general conditional (مشروطة عامة) converts similarly but under the general possible modality. The general customary (عرفية عامة) converts under the absolute modality. For the particular conditional (مشروطة خاصة), there is disagreement: some say it does not convert at all; some say it converts to the general; some say it partially converts as both itself and the general. The particular customary (عرفية خاصة) is similar. Finally, the remaining affirmatives, specifically the particular temporal (وقتية خاصة), do not convert because the temporal restriction on the subject cannot be preserved in the converse. The author explains that if you try to convert 'Necessarily every J is B at time t', the converse 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J at time t' is false because it is possible that some not-B is necessarily J at that time. The argument uses the notion that the predicate in the original is unrestricted, so the converse would require a different temporal condition. This lesson is essential for understanding how to correctly perform immediate inference with modal propositions, a key skill in constructing valid syllogisms in modal logic.

Key Terms
وقتية خاصة
نقيض المحمول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the particular temporal proposition not convert?
A) Because the mode is too weak
B) Because the predicate is absolute but the subject in the converse would need to be restricted
C) Because the original is always false
D) Because conversion only works for universals

Key Takeaway
Particular temporal propositions do not convert because the temporal condition on the subject cannot be transferred to the predicate in the converse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Why does the particular temporal proposition 'بالضرورة كل حدث محدث لا دائما بل وقت حدوثه' (Necessarily every event is temporally originated, not always but at the time of its occurrence) not convert? Explain using the author's argument about absolute vs restricted predicate.

Conversion of Special Conditional and Customary Propositions
Author explains conversion of special conditional and customary propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a special conditional or special customary proposition converts, and identify the correct form of its converse based on the author's discussion.

Core Idea
Modal conversions vary; some restrict conversion to a qualified particular.

The author presents three opinions on the conversion of special conditional propositions. Understanding these opinions helps you see how medieval Islamic logicians handled modal propositions with conditions. We will walk through the reasoning and apply it to Islamic examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Recognize that a special conditional (mashruta khassa) is of the form 'Necessarily every J is B as long as J exists'. It is a universal affirmative modal proposition with a condition of persistence.
2
Review the three opinions
Note the three views: (1) No conversion at all, (2) Converts to a general universal affirmative (like simple conversion), (3) Converts to a qualified particular: 'Always in some, not always in some' – i.e., a particular affirmative with a temporal qualifier.
The author sides with the third view but notes it is mixed.
3
Apply to special customary
The special customary ('urfiyya khassa) is similar but without necessity. The same analysis applies, just remove the necessity operator.
Example: 'Every J is B as long as J exists' (non-necessary) converts similarly.

[۸] وعلى هذا في المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا دائمة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص من المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة كما مر . [٩] ثمَّ العكس في الممكنات إنما يمتنع إذا كان الإمكان جهة، فأما إذا كان محمولا فلا يمتنع بل الحكم فيها نحو الحكم في الغير من الفعليات. واعلم بأن القضايا التي لا تنعكس عكس النقيض يمكن أن يلزمها ما يتوهم أن يكون عکس النقيض لكنه لا يكون عكس النقيض، فإن عكس النقيض وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم أن يكون ما يكون من اللوازم فهو عكس النقيض ومن زعم أن ذلك عكس النقيض فعليه أن يعرف عكس النقيض بتعريف يوافق ذلك اللازم وذلك باطل يعرف بالتأمل في حقيقة العكس. وقد مر من قبل أن عكس الشيء هو التصرف في جميع أجزاء ذلك الشيء، والتصرف في الأجزاء لا يكون إلا وأن تكون الأجزاء في العكس هي الأجزاء التي في الأصل. [١٠] وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض لما أنه يمكن أن يحمل الأعم على عدم الأخص، ولا يمكن أن يحمل الأخص على عدم الأعم أصلًا فيقال مثلا بعض الإنسان حيوان، ولا يقال بعض ما ليس بحيوان ليس بلا إنسان أي بعض ما ليس بحيوان إنسان فإن اللا إنسان من لوازم اللا حيوان وإلا لا يلزم أن يكون الإنسان حيوانا. ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول إذا كان الحيوان من لوازم اللا إنسان كان من اللوازم أن يكون الإنسان من لوازم اللاحيوان وإلا لا يلزم من نفي اللازم نفي الملزوم. لكنا نقول الحيوان الذي هو المحمول في قولنا بعض اللا إنسان حيوان، هو الحيوان مطلقًا لا الحيوان الذي هو اللازم. ولو كان كذلك لكان نقيض المحمول من حيث هو نقيض المحمول غير
[5] As for the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), some say it does not convert at all because its converse varies according to the matter, so it is impossible that its converse is this without that, and vice versa. According to some, it converts to the general (al-‘amma) like simple conversion. According to some, it converts as 'always in some not always in some' as is said: 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J, as long as it is not B, always in some not always in some' for what has passed. So it does not convert as itself nor as its general, but in some as itself and in some as the general. [6] As for the special customary (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa), the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the special conditional, and the example mentioned for the special conditional and its converse is without the word 'necessity' as the example for the special customary. [7] As for the remaining affirmative propositions, they are not converted because conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, namely the special temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa). For it is true to say 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time', but it is not true to say 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at such a time', because it is possible that some of what is not B is necessarily J. And if one takes the contradictory of the predicate as a temporally restricted subject, that is not the contrapositive (naqid al-‘aks), for it is necessary that the subject in the contrapositive is some of the predicate. That is not the contradictory of the predicate, since the predicate is B absolutely, not B restricted by the condition of being at that time. Likewise, if the conversion is necessary in position or always, as is said 'everything that is not B necessarily or always is not J', then it is not the contrapositive. [8] And similarly for the pervasive (al-muntashira) which is more specific than the existential non-permanent, which is more specific than the existential non-necessary, which is more specific than the special possible, which is more specific than the absolute general, which is more specific than the general possible, as has passed. [9] Then conversion in possibles is only impossible if possibility is a modality; but if it is a predicate, it is not impossible, rather the judgment about them is like the judgment about others among actualities. Know that propositions that do not convert by contrapositive may have a consequence that is imagined to be the contrapositive but is not the contrapositive. For the contrapositive, even if it is among the consequences, it does not follow that whatever is among the consequences is the contrapositive. Whoever claims that that is the contrapositive must define the contrapositive with a definition that agrees with that consequence, and that is false, known by reflection on the reality of conversion. It has passed earlier that conversion of a thing is handling all the parts of that thing, and handling the parts is only when the parts in the converse are the parts that are in the original. [10] As for the partial affirmative (al-mujiba al-juz’iyya), it does not convert by contrapositive because it is possible to predicate the more general of the absence of the more specific, and it is not possible to predicate the more specific of the absence of the more general at all. For example, 'Some human is animal' is said, but 'Some of what is not animal is not non-human' – i.e., some of what is not animal is human – is not said. For non-human is among the necessary consequences of non-animal, otherwise it would not follow that human is animal. Then someone may say: if animal is among the consequences of non-human, then it is necessary that human is among the consequences of non-animal, otherwise the negation of the consequent does not imply the negation of the antecedent. But we say: animal which is the predicate in our statement 'some non-human is animal' is animal absolutely, not animal which is the consequent. And if it were so, then the contradictory of the predicate insofar as it is the contradictory of the predicate would be...

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion on the conversion (عكس) of various types of modal and quantified propositions, specifically focusing on when conversion is possible and when it is not, and clarifying misconceptions about contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author proceeds by examining each type in turn: 

Firstly, the special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) – a proposition like 'necessarily every J is B, as long as J exists' (i.e., per se in the first mode). Three opinions are given: (1) Some say it cannot convert at all because its converse varies with the matter; (2) Some say it converts to the general (i.e., the absolute universal affirmative) via simple conversion; (3) Some say it converts in a qualified manner: 'always in some, not always in some,' meaning the converse is a particular affirmative with a temporal qualifier. The author notes that it does not convert identically nor as its general form, but sometimes as itself and sometimes as the general. 

Secondly, the special customary (العرفية الخاصة) – similar to the special conditional but without the necessity operator, so the discussion parallels. 

Thirdly, the remaining affirmative propositions, specifically the special temporal (الوقتية الخاصة) – e.g., 'necessarily every J is B, not always but at time T'. Conversion is impossible because the converse would require 'necessarily every non-B is non-J at that time', but it is possible that some non-B is necessarily J at that time (since J may be necessarily something else). The author stresses that taking the contradictory of the predicate as a temporally restricted subject does not yield a valid contrapositive (عكس النقيض) because the predicate in the original is absolute, not restricted. Similarly, if the converse is necessary in position or always, it still is not contrapositive. 

Then, a hierarchy of proposition types is given: pervasive (منتشرة) < existential non-permanent < existential non-necessary < special possible < absolute general < general possible, each being more specific than the next. 

Regarding possibles (الممكنات), conversion is impossible only when possibility is a modality (i.e., a modal operator), but if 'possible' is a predicate (e.g., 'J is possible'), then conversion is allowed like other actual propositions. 

The author warns against mistakenly identifying a logical consequence as contrapositive. Contrapositive (عكس النقيض) is a specific operation: it must involve handling all parts of the original proposition such that the subject and predicate are swapped and negated. Not every necessary consequence qualifies. 

Finally, the partial affirmative (الموجبة الجزئية) does not have a contrapositive. For example, 'Some human is animal' is true, but its supposed contrapositive 'Some non-animal is non-human' would mean 'Some non-animal is human', which is false because if non-human were a necessary consequence of non-animal, then human would be a consequence of animal? The author addresses an objection: if animal is a consequence of non-human, then human should be a consequence of non-animal (by contrapositive of the implication). But the predicate 'animal' in the conversion is absolute, not the animal that is a consequent of non-human. The contradictory of the predicate (i.e., 'non-animal') is different. The text cuts off but the point is that the partial affirmative does not convert via contrapositive.

Key Terms
المشروطة الخاصة
العرفية الخاصة
عكس مستوي
مادة

The conversion of modal propositions like special conditional is contentious but the accepted view holds that they convert to qualified particular affirmatives.
