Why Special Temporal Propositions Do Not Convert via Contrapositive
Author argues special temporal propositions cannot be converted via contrapositive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the logical reason why special temporal propositions cannot be converted via contrapositive.

Core Idea
Temporal restrictions on the predicate prevent valid contraposition.

The author examines the special temporal proposition and shows that its assumed contrapositive fails because the predicate in the original is absolute, not temporally restricted. We will focus on the logical reasoning behind this impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special temporal form
Recognize that a special temporal (waqtiyya khassa) is: 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at a specific time T'.
Example: 'Necessarily every human is sleeping, not always but at night?' Not accurate, but the form is clear.
2
Attempt the contrapositive
The putative contrapositive would be: 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at time T'.
3
Find the counterexample
It is possible that some non-B is necessarily J at time T, making the contrapositive false. For instance, J might have a necessary property not at time T.
The author says: 'من الممكن أن يكون بعض ما هو ليس «ب» هو «ج» بالضرورة' - some non-B is necessarily J.
4
Understand why the predicate is absolute
The predicate B in the original is absolute, not restricted to time T. In the contrapositive, the subject would be the contradictory of the predicate, but that contradictory is absolute ('not B' absolutely), whereas the original premise involves B only at time T.
Thus the terms do not match; contrapositive requires identical subject and predicate terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٥] وأما المشروطة الخاصة فإنها لا تنعكس أصلا عند بعضهم لما أن عكسه يختلف باختلاف المواد فلا يمكن أن يكون عكسه هذا دون ذلك، وبالعكس. وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس إلى العامة كما في العكس المستوي، وعند بعضهم أنها تنعكس دائمًا في البعض لا دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» ب» لا دائما بل ما دام «ج» ، بالضرورة كل ما هو ليس (ب) ليس (ج) ما دام ليس «ب» دائمًا في البعض لا دائمًا في البعض لما مر . فلا تنعكس إذن كنفسها ولا كعامتها بل في البعض كالنفس وفي البعض كالعامة. [٦] وأما العرفية الخاصة فالكلام فيها نحو الكلام في المشروطة الخاصة، والمثال المذكور في المشروطة الخاصة وعكسها فذلك بدون لفظة الضرورة المثال للعرفية الخاصة. [۷] وأما القضايا الباقية من الموجبات فإنها لا تكون منعكسة لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية الخاصة، فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل «ج» «ب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا، ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كل ما ليس «ب» ليس (ج) لا دائما بل وقت كذا، فإن من الممكن أن يكون بعض ما هو ليس «ب» هو «ج» بالضرورة، ولو أخذ نقيض المحمول موضوعا موقتاً فذلك لا يكون عكس النقيض فإن من اللوازم أن يكون الموضوع في عكس النقيض بعض المحمول. وذلك لا يكون نقيض المحمول؛ إذ المحمول هو الباء مطلقاً لا الباء المقيد بقيد كونه في ذلك الوقت. وكذلك إذا كان العكس ضروريًا في الوضع أو دائما كما يقال كل ما هو ليس «ب» بالضرورة أو دائما ليس بج»، فإنه لا يكون عكس النقيض. [۸] وعلى هذا في المنتشرة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا دائمة التي هي أخص من الوجودية اللا ضرورية التي هي أخص من الممكنة الخاصة التي هي أخص من المطلقة العامة التي هي أخص من الممكنة العامة كما مر . [٩] ثمَّ العكس في الممكنات إنما يمتنع إذا كان الإمكان جهة، فأما إذا كان محمولا فلا يمتنع بل الحكم فيها نحو الحكم في الغير من الفعليات. واعلم بأن القضايا التي لا تنعكس عكس النقيض يمكن أن يلزمها ما يتوهم أن يكون عکس النقيض لكنه لا يكون عكس النقيض، فإن عكس النقيض وإن كان من اللوازم فلا يلزم أن يكون ما يكون من اللوازم فهو عكس النقيض ومن زعم أن ذلك عكس النقيض فعليه أن يعرف عكس النقيض بتعريف يوافق ذلك اللازم وذلك باطل يعرف بالتأمل في حقيقة العكس. وقد مر من قبل أن عكس الشيء هو التصرف في جميع أجزاء ذلك الشيء، والتصرف في الأجزاء لا يكون إلا وأن تكون الأجزاء في العكس هي الأجزاء التي في الأصل. [١٠] وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض لما أنه يمكن أن يحمل الأعم على عدم الأخص، ولا يمكن أن يحمل الأخص على عدم الأعم أصلًا فيقال مثلا بعض الإنسان حيوان، ولا يقال بعض ما ليس بحيوان ليس بلا إنسان أي بعض ما ليس بحيوان إنسان فإن اللا إنسان من لوازم اللا حيوان وإلا لا يلزم أن يكون الإنسان حيوانا. ثمَّ لقائل أن يقول إذا كان الحيوان من لوازم اللا إنسان كان من اللوازم أن يكون الإنسان من لوازم اللاحيوان وإلا لا يلزم من نفي اللازم نفي الملزوم. لكنا نقول الحيوان الذي هو المحمول في قولنا بعض اللا إنسان حيوان، هو الحيوان مطلقًا لا الحيوان الذي هو اللازم. ولو كان كذلك لكان نقيض المحمول من حيث هو نقيض المحمول غير
[5] As for the special conditional (al-mashruta al-khassa), some say it does not convert at all because its converse varies according to the matter, so it is impossible that its converse is this without that, and vice versa. According to some, it converts to the general (al-‘amma) like simple conversion. According to some, it converts as 'always in some not always in some' as is said: 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but as long as J; necessarily everything that is not B is not J, as long as it is not B, always in some not always in some' for what has passed. So it does not convert as itself nor as its general, but in some as itself and in some as the general. [6] As for the special customary (al-‘urfiyya al-khassa), the discussion about it is similar to the discussion about the special conditional, and the example mentioned for the special conditional and its converse is without the word 'necessity' as the example for the special customary. [7] As for the remaining affirmative propositions, they are not converted because conversion is impossible in the most specific of them, namely the special temporal (al-waqtiyya al-khassa). For it is true to say 'Necessarily every J is B, not always but at such a time', but it is not true to say 'Necessarily everything that is not B is not J, not always but at such a time', because it is possible that some of what is not B is necessarily J. And if one takes the contradictory of the predicate as a temporally restricted subject, that is not the contrapositive (naqid al-‘aks), for it is necessary that the subject in the contrapositive is some of the predicate. That is not the contradictory of the predicate, since the predicate is B absolutely, not B restricted by the condition of being at that time. Likewise, if the conversion is necessary in position or always, as is said 'everything that is not B necessarily or always is not J', then it is not the contrapositive. [8] And similarly for the pervasive (al-muntashira) which is more specific than the existential non-permanent, which is more specific than the existential non-necessary, which is more specific than the special possible, which is more specific than the absolute general, which is more specific than the general possible, as has passed. [9] Then conversion in possibles is only impossible if possibility is a modality; but if it is a predicate, it is not impossible, rather the judgment about them is like the judgment about others among actualities. Know that propositions that do not convert by contrapositive may have a consequence that is imagined to be the contrapositive but is not the contrapositive. For the contrapositive, even if it is among the consequences, it does not follow that whatever is among the consequences is the contrapositive. Whoever claims that that is the contrapositive must define the contrapositive with a definition that agrees with that consequence, and that is false, known by reflection on the reality of conversion. It has passed earlier that conversion of a thing is handling all the parts of that thing, and handling the parts is only when the parts in the converse are the parts that are in the original. [10] As for the partial affirmative (al-mujiba al-juz’iyya), it does not convert by contrapositive because it is possible to predicate the more general of the absence of the more specific, and it is not possible to predicate the more specific of the absence of the more general at all. For example, 'Some human is animal' is said, but 'Some of what is not animal is not non-human' – i.e., some of what is not animal is human – is not said. For non-human is among the necessary consequences of non-animal, otherwise it would not follow that human is animal. Then someone may say: if animal is among the consequences of non-human, then it is necessary that human is among the consequences of non-animal, otherwise the negation of the consequent does not imply the negation of the antecedent. But we say: animal which is the predicate in our statement 'some non-human is animal' is animal absolutely, not animal which is the consequent. And if it were so, then the contradictory of the predicate insofar as it is the contradictory of the predicate would be...
Source pages p. 491

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion on the conversion (عكس) of various types of modal and quantified propositions, specifically focusing on when conversion is possible and when it is not, and clarifying misconceptions about contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author proceeds by examining each type in turn: 

Firstly, the special conditional (المشروطة الخاصة) – a proposition like 'necessarily every J is B, as long as J exists' (i.e., per se in the first mode). Three opinions are given: (1) Some say it cannot convert at all because its converse varies with the matter; (2) Some say it converts to the general (i.e., the absolute universal affirmative) via simple conversion; (3) Some say it converts in a qualified manner: 'always in some, not always in some,' meaning the converse is a particular affirmative with a temporal qualifier. The author notes that it does not convert identically nor as its general form, but sometimes as itself and sometimes as the general. 

Secondly, the special customary (العرفية الخاصة) – similar to the special conditional but without the necessity operator, so the discussion parallels. 

Thirdly, the remaining affirmative propositions, specifically the special temporal (الوقتية الخاصة) – e.g., 'necessarily every J is B, not always but at time T'. Conversion is impossible because the converse would require 'necessarily every non-B is non-J at that time', but it is possible that some non-B is necessarily J at that time (since J may be necessarily something else). The author stresses that taking the contradictory of the predicate as a temporally restricted subject does not yield a valid contrapositive (عكس النقيض) because the predicate in the original is absolute, not restricted. Similarly, if the converse is necessary in position or always, it still is not contrapositive. 

Then, a hierarchy of proposition types is given: pervasive (منتشرة) < existential non-permanent < existential non-necessary < special possible < absolute general < general possible, each being more specific than the next. 

Regarding possibles (الممكنات), conversion is impossible only when possibility is a modality (i.e., a modal operator), but if 'possible' is a predicate (e.g., 'J is possible'), then conversion is allowed like other actual propositions. 

The author warns against mistakenly identifying a logical consequence as contrapositive. Contrapositive (عكس النقيض) is a specific operation: it must involve handling all parts of the original proposition such that the subject and predicate are swapped and negated. Not every necessary consequence qualifies. 

Finally, the partial affirmative (الموجبة الجزئية) does not have a contrapositive. For example, 'Some human is animal' is true, but its supposed contrapositive 'Some non-animal is non-human' would mean 'Some non-animal is human', which is false because if non-human were a necessary consequence of non-animal, then human would be a consequence of animal? The author addresses an objection: if animal is a consequence of non-human, then human should be a consequence of non-animal (by contrapositive of the implication). But the predicate 'animal' in the conversion is absolute, not the animal that is a consequent of non-human. The contradictory of the predicate (i.e., 'non-animal') is different. The text cuts off but the point is that the partial affirmative does not convert via contrapositive.

Key Terms
الوقتية الخاصة
عكس النقيض
محمول مطلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the special temporal proposition not have a valid contrapositive?
A) Because the contrapositive would be a universal negative, which is not equivalent.
B) Because the predicate in the original is absolute, not time-restricted, and the subject in the contrapositive would not match.
C) Because the proposition is not actually true.
D) Because conversion is only allowed for universal affirmatives.

Key Takeaway
Special temporal propositions resist contrapositive conversion because the predicate's absolute nature and the temporal restriction on the subject prevent term matching.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition: 'Necessarily every contingent being is created, not always but at the time of its existence'. Explain why its contrapositive is invalid, using the author's reasoning about the absolute predicate.

Immediate Inference

12 min
The Nature of Contrapositive and the Partial Affirmative
Author clarifies true contrapositive and explains why partial affirmative lacks one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a true contrapositive from a mere logical consequence, and explain why partial affirmatives lack a valid contrapositive.

Core Idea
Not every logical consequence is a contrapositive; the contrapositive must preserve the exact original terms.

The author warns against confusing any necessary consequence with a true contrapositive. He then examines the partial affirmative proposition and explains why it cannot have a contrapositive. We will dissect the reasoning carefully.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of contrapositive
The contrapositive (عكس النقيض) is formed by swapping subject and predicate and negating both, requiring that the parts of the original are preserved.
The author says: 'عكس الشيء هو التصرف في جميع أجزاء ذلك الشيء' - conversion of a thing is handling all its parts.
2
Apply to partial affirmative
Take 'Some human is animal' (Some J is B). The supposed contrapositive would be 'Some non-animal is non-human' (Some non-B is non-J).
But 'Some non-animal is non-human' would mean 'Some non-animal is human', which is false because non-animal includes things like stones that are not human.
3
Understand the impossibility
It is possible to predicate the more general (animal) of the more specific (human) in the original, but in the contrapositive, the more specific would have to be predicated of the more general's negation, which fails.
Formally: from Some J is B, we cannot infer Some non-B is non-J because non-B is more general than non-J, and the inference would require the opposite.
4
Address an objection
An objector might say: if animal is a consequence of non-human, then human should be a consequence of non-animal (by contrapositive of implication). But the author clarifies that the predicate 'animal' in 'Some non-human is animal' is absolute, not the animal that is a consequence.
Thus the terms differ; the contrapositive would require the predicate to be the contradictory of the original predicate, which is 'non-animal', but that doesn't yield the needed converse.

Why does the partial affirmative (e.g., Some human is animal) not have a valid contrapositive according to the passage?
A) Because the proposition is particular, and contrapositive only works for universals.

Why the Partial Affirmative Does Not Contraposit
Author explains why partial affirmative cannot undergo contraposition and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given partial affirmative proposition undergoes contraposition by testing the predicability of the specific on the negation of the general.

Core Idea
The partial affirmative fails contraposition because the more specific cannot be predicated of the negation of the more general.

In this passage, the author explains a crucial restriction on contraposition: while universal propositions can be contraposed, the partial affirmative cannot. He provides a logical argument based on the five universals (specific/general) and refutes an objection from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa. Understanding this helps you avoid a common fallacy in immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Confirm that the proposition is a partial affirmative (e.g., 'Some human is animal'). It has a subject term, a predicate term, and is particular in quantity.
Partial affirmative = al-mūjaba al-juz'iyya.
2
Recall the definition of contraposition
Contraposition ('aks al-naqid) transforms a proposition into another where the subject becomes the negation of the original predicate and the predicate becomes the negation of the original subject, while preserving truth.
For example, from 'All A is B' we get 'All non-B is non-A'.
3
Test the predicability condition
Check if the more specific term (original subject) can be predicated of the negation of the more general term (original predicate). For 'Some human is animal', the more specific is 'human' and the more general is 'animal'. The contrapositive would require 'Some non-animal is non-human', i.e., 'Some non-animal is human' (since double negation cancels). But 'human' (specific) cannot be predicated of 'non-animal' (negation of a more general). Therefore, the contrapositive is invalid.
The author states: 'لا يمكن أن يحمل الأخص على عدم الأعم أصلًا' (it is not possible at all that the more specific be predicated of the negation of the more general).
4
Analyze a purported counterexample
Ibn Sina claimed that from 'Some J is B' it follows that 'Some non-B is non-J'. The author argues that this is not a universal rule but applies only in special cases where both subject and predicate can be negated in some manner.
The author says: 'ذلك القول لا يكون قولاً كلياً يشمل لجميع الجزئيات بل يكون مخصوصاً بالبعض منها'.
5
Reconstruct the author's refutation
The author distinguishes between 'animal' as an absolute predicate and 'animal' as a necessary consequent of 'non-human'. In the supposed contrapositive 'Some non-human is animal', the predicate is absolute, not the necessary consequent, so negating it does not negate the necessary consequent, and the inference fails.
The author says: 'الحيوان الذي هو المحمول... هو الحيوان مطلقاً لا الحيوان الذي هو اللازم'.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed clarification of why the partial affirmative proposition (الموجبة الجزئية) does not undergo contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author begins by establishing that contraposition involves a transformation of all parts of the proposition, requiring that the parts in the converse be the same as in the original. The partial affirmative fails because its converse would require predicating the more specific (الأخص) of the negation of the more general (الأعم), which is impossible. For example, from 'Some human is animal' we cannot validly infer 'Some non-animal is non-human' (i.e., 'Some non-animal is human'), because non-human is a necessary consequence of non-animal, but the converse does not hold logically. The author then addresses a potential objection: if animal is a necessary consequent of non-human, then the contrapositive should hold. He clarifies that the predicate in the supposed converse ('Some non-human is animal') is 'animal' in an absolute sense, not the animal that is a necessary consequent of non-human. Therefore, negating that predicate does not negate the necessary consequent, so the inference fails. He also refutes an argument from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa that appears to support contraposition of the partial affirmative. The author argues that Ibn Sina's statement is not universal but applies only to cases where both subject and predicate can be negated in some manner; it does not establish the general rule. The author emphasizes that if a proposition is not convertible in actual fact, local instances of valid inference do not make it convertible.

عكس النقيض
الموجبة الجزئية

Key Takeaway
The partial affirmative cannot undergo contraposition because the more specific cannot be predicated of the negation of the more general.

Contrapositive of Particular Affirmatives: Critique and the External/Essential Distinction
Author refutes the claim that particular affirmatives convert by contrapositive and distinguishes external from essential propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the claim that particular affirmatives convert by contrapositive and distinguish between external and essential propositions in this context.

Core Idea
Not all particular affirmatives convert by contrapositive; the rule depends on the nature of the subject and predicate.

In this passage, the author addresses a common logical claim: that a particular affirmative proposition like "Some J is B" can be converted by contrapositive to "Some not-B is not-J". He argues that this is not a universal rule, but only applies under certain conditions. He also introduces the important distinction between external propositions (about actual existents) and essential propositions (about natures), showing that logical rules must be sensitive to the type of proposition.

Guided Path
1
Understand the principle of contrapositive
Recall that contrapositive is an immediate inference: if 'A implies B', then 'not-B implies not-A'. This is valid for universal propositions but not always for particulars.
The author first states this principle: from the contradictory of the consequent, the contradictory of the antecedent follows.
2
Identify the specific claim under scrutiny
The claim is that 'Some J is B' converts by contrapositive to 'Some not-B is not-J'. This is attributed to Avicenna in the Shifa'.
The author quotes Avicenna's example: if we say 'Some J is B', then 'Some not-B is not-J' follows because there are existents outside both J and B.
3
Examine the author's objection
The author argues that the inference is valid only when there exist things that are neither J nor B (i.e., the subject and predicate do not exhaust the universe). If J and B cover everything, then 'Some not-B is not-J' is false.
The author states that Avicenna's statement is not universal; it is specific to some particulars where both subject and predicate can be negated.
4
Apply the distinction between external and essential propositions
The author concludes that the majority view is that contrapositive for affirmatives works when the propositions are external (kharijiyya) under certain conditions. For essential (haqiqiyya) propositions, the rule may differ because the subject includes possible existents.
This distinction is crucial for understanding when immediate inferences hold in modal contexts.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقيض اللازم من حيث هو نقيض اللازم. فإذن يلزم من نقيض اللازم نقيض الملزوم لامتناع تحقق الملزوم دون اللازم لكن لا يلزم من نقيض المحمول نقيض الموضوع، وقد كان من الممكن أن يتحقق الموضوع دون المحمول كما أن اللا إنسان الذي هو الموضوع يمكن أن يكون، ولاحيوان. ولا يظن أن الموضوع هو اللا إنسان الذي يلزمه أن يكون حيوانا؛ إذ الموضوع هو اللا إنسان من حيث هو اللا إنسان. ولقائل أن يقول أيضًا الموجبة الجزئية تنعكس عكس النقيض، وكيف لا يقال وقد قال الشيخ في الشفاء إذا قلنا بعض «ج» «ب» لزم بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج»، فإنه توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن «ج» و «ب» معا فيكون بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج». لكن نقول: ذلك القول لا يكون قولا كليا يشمل لجميع الجزئيات بل يكون مخصوصا بالبعض منها وهي التي يمكن ارتفاع الموضوع فيها، والمحمول كذلك في الجملة؛ ولهذا علله بذلك التعليل. ولما كان مخصوصا بالبعض منها فلا يلزم منه أن تكون الموجبة الجزئية منعكسة ولا أن يكون ذلك اللازم عكس النقيض، وإن كانت موجبة بل من اللوازم أن لا يكون عكس النقيض إذا كانت سالبة، وقد كان من الشرائط أن يكون السلب والإيجاب بحاله. ولا يقال «ه» «ب» أنه كذلك لكن قوله لا يكون كما ينبغي فإن صدق قوله بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» لا يتعلق بصدق الأصل في هذه الصورة أصلا. ولو كان كذلك فلا يكون صدقه من لوازم صدق الأصل؛ إذ صدقه من اللوازم فيما ذكرناه من الصورة وإلا لا يكون هذا ذلك في مثل تلك الصورة. وقول من قال «إنها تنعكس عكس النقيض إذا كانت بهذه الجهة أو في تلك المادة أو كذا وكذا»؛ فذلك باطل إذا لم تكن منعكسة في نفس الأمر. فإنه لا يلزم من كونها منعكسة في البعض من الصور بحسب ما يكون مختصا بذلك البعض أن تكون منعكسة في سائر الصور، وإذا لم تكن منعكسة في البعض من الصور فلا تكون منعكسة في نفس الأمر، هذا هو المشهور عند الجمهور في عكس النقيض إذا كانت الموجبات خارجية، وبهذا يظهر حكمه إذا كانت حقيقية.
The contradictory of the consequent insofar as it is the contradictory of the consequent. So then from the contradictory of the consequent follows the contradictory of the antecedent because it is impossible for the antecedent to be realized without the consequent. But from the contradictory of the predicate does not follow the contradictory of the subject, for it is possible for the subject to be realized without the predicate, as in the non-human which is the subject can be, and non-animal. And one should not think that the subject is the non-human which requires it to be an animal; for the subject is the non-human insofar as it is non-human. And since it is specific to some of them, it does not entail that the particular affirmative is convertible, nor that that consequent is the contrapositive, even if it is affirmative; rather, from the necessary consequences it is that there is no contrapositive if it is negative, and the condition is that negation and affirmation remain as they are. And if it were so, then its truth would not be a necessary consequence of the truth of the original; for its truth is a necessary consequence in the form we mentioned, otherwise it would not be that in such a form. And the statement of one who says 'it converts by contrapositive if it is in this manner or in that matter or such and such' is false if it is not convertible in itself. For from its being convertible in some forms according to what is specific to that part, it does not follow that it is convertible in all forms; and if it is not convertible in some forms, then it is not convertible in itself. This is the well-known view among the majority regarding contrapositive when the affirmatives are external, and by this its ruling becomes clear when they are essential.
Source pages p. 493

Commentary
This passage refutes a common claim about contraposition. The author begins by stating a general logical principle: from the contradictory of the consequent follows the contradictory of the antecedent (if A implies B, then not-B implies not-A). However, he clarifies that this does not hold for the predicate alone: from the contradictory of the predicate, the contradictory of the subject does not necessarily follow, because the subject could exist without the predicate. The example given is 'non-human' which can be 'non-animal' — showing that the subject term 'non-human' does not entail 'animal'. The author then addresses a specific objection: that the particular affirmative proposition (Some J is B) converts by contrapositive to (Some not-B is not-J). He cites Avicenna's example from the Shifa' where this is claimed. The author argues that Avicenna's statement is not universal; it only applies to some cases where both subject and predicate can be negated. Therefore, it does not prove that all particular affirmatives are convertible by contrapositive. The passage emphasizes that a rule must hold in all cases, not just some. It concludes with a distinction between 'external' (kharijiyya) and 'essential' (haqiqiyya) propositions: the majority view is that contrapositive works for external affirmatives, and by analogy the rule for essential propositions can be derived. The author warns that partial convertibility does not imply universal convertibility.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
الموجبة الجزئية
خارجية
حقيقية

A) Because Avicenna's statement in the Shifa' is false.
B) Because it only applies to cases where there exist things outside both J and B.
C) Because the contrapositive of a particular affirmative is always a universal negative.
D) Because the subject J cannot be negated.

Key Takeaway
The conversion by contrapositive of particular affirmatives is not a universal logical law; it depends on the material and type of proposition.

Contrapositive of Affirmative Modal Universals
Enumeration of views on contrapositive of modal affirmatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the contrapositive of any affirmative modal universal proposition (necessary, perpetual, conditional, customary) and recognize that a proposition has exactly one contrapositive.

Core Idea
Each modal affirmative universal has a unique contrapositive that preserves its mode.

In this lesson, we examine the views of early logicians on the contrapositive (عكس النقيض) of affirmative modal universal propositions. The passage presents the positions of a group of early scholars, as well as the view of al-Shaykh (Avicenna), on how necessary, perpetual, conditional, and customary propositions convert. You will learn to identify the correct contrapositive for each type and understand why the contrapositive is always unique.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of modal proposition
Determine whether the proposition is necessary, perpetual, conditional (general or special), or customary (general or special). Also note if it is external (kharijiyya) or essential (haqiqiyya), as this affects the result.
External concerns actual existents; essential concerns the nature.
2
Recall the contrapositive rule
The contrapositive of 'Every A is B' (with modality M) is 'Every not-B is not-A' with a derived modality. The passage specifies the exact modality for each type.
The general rule is that the contrapositive must preserve the truth of the original.
3
Apply the specific ruling from the text
For necessary external: perpetual universal negative. For necessary essential: necessary universal negative. For perpetual (external or essential): perpetual universal negative. For general conditional and general customary: universal negative. For special conditional and special customary: like the general (universal negative).
These are the views of a group of early scholars as cited in the text.
4
Confirm uniqueness
The author emphasizes that each proposition has exactly one contrapositive. Do not confuse other necessary consequences with the contrapositive.
Avicenna also notes that for affirmative modal propositions, the contrapositive resembles the original in modality.

فصل: [ في الأقوال المختلفة في عكس النقيض ] وأما الأقوال المختلفة في عكس النقيض فإنها متعددة منها ما يكون في الموجبات على خلاف ما ذهب إليه الجمهور ، ومنها ما يكون في السوالب. أما في الموجبات فقوم من المتقدمين زعموا [1] في الكلية الضرورية أنها إذا كانت خارجية تنعكس إلى سالبة دائمة كلية لما مر ، وإن كانت حقيقية فإلى سالبة كلية ضرورية؛ [ب] وفي الدائمة أنها تنعكس إلى سالبة كلية دائمة خارجية كانت أو حقيقية [ت] وفي المشروطة العامة أنها تنعكس سالبتها كلية؛ [ث] وكذلك في العرفية العامة؛ [ج] وفي المشروطة الخاصة أنها تنعكس انعكاس العامة خارجية كانت أو حقيقية؛ [ح] وكذلك في العرفية الخاصة. ولا يمتنع أن يستدل بالدلائل التي مرَّ ذكرها لكنه لا يكون عكس النقيض في صورة من هذه الصور على مذهب الجمهور منهم بل هو من اللوازم عندهم [١٤٢ظ واللوازم لعكس النقيض / بل للأصل كثيرة يستدل عليها بما يستدل به على عكس النقيض. ولا يظن أن جميع ما يلزم من هذا أو من ذلك فهو عكس النقيض فإن عكس النقيض للقضية الواحدة واحد ليس إلا، ومن زعم أنها تنعكس إلى الزائد على الواحد فزعمه باطل. وعن الشيخ أنه قال في هذه الستة إذا كانت موجبة أنها تنعكس كنفسها كما وجهةً فإنه إذا صدق قولنا كل (ج) ب)) دائمًا صدق قولنا كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) دائمًا وإلا نقيضه وهو
Section: On the different opinions regarding contrapositive. As for the different opinions regarding contrapositive, they are numerous. Some concern affirmatives contrary to what the majority held, and some concern negatives. Regarding affirmatives, a group of the early scholars claimed: [1] For the necessary universal, if it is external, it converts to a perpetual universal negative as previously mentioned; if it is essential, then to a necessary universal negative. [B] For the perpetual, it converts to a perpetual universal negative, whether external or essential. [T] For the general conditional, its negative converts to a universal. [Th] Likewise for the general customary. [J] For the special conditional, it converts by the conversion of the general, whether external or essential. [H] Likewise for the special customary. And it is not impossible to use the proofs that were mentioned, but it is not contrapositive in any of these forms according to the majority view; rather, it is among the necessary consequences for them. And the necessary consequences for the contrapositive / rather for the original are many, and one can use the same proofs used for contrapositive. And one should not think that everything that follows from this or that is contrapositive; for the contrapositive of a single proposition is only one. Whoever claims that it converts to more than one is mistaken. And from the Shaykh (Avicenna) it is reported that he said concerning these six, when they are affirmative, that they convert like themselves in a certain way: for if 'Every J is B' is always true, then 'Every not-B is not-J' is always true, otherwise its contradictory...
Source pages p. 494

Commentary
This passage enumerates the views of early scholars on the contrapositives of various modal affirmative propositions. The author explicitly notes that the opinions differ from the majority view. The enumerations are as follows: (1) For the necessary universal (e.g., 'Every A is necessarily B'), if it is external (i.e., about actual existents), its contrapositive is a perpetual universal negative (i.e., 'Every not-B is not-A' perpetually). If it is essential (about the nature), the contrapositive is a necessary universal negative. (2) For the perpetual universal (e.g., 'Every A is always B'), its contrapositive is a perpetual universal negative, whether external or essential. (3) For the general conditional (mashruta 'amma, e.g., 'Every A is B as long as it exists'), its contrapositive is a universal negative. (4) Similarly for the general customary (urfiyya 'amma). (5) For the special conditional (mashruta khassa), its contrapositive is like that of the general, whether external or essential. (6) Likewise for the special customary. The author then warns that although many consequences follow from a proposition, the contrapositive is unique for each proposition. He cites Avicenna's view that for six affirmative modal propositions, their contrapositives are of the same type (e.g., necessary to necessary, perpetual to perpetual). The passage ends abruptly, but the argument is clear: each proposition has exactly one contrapositive, and one should not conflate other necessary consequences with the contrapositive.

Conversion of Conditional and Conventional Propositions
Author explains conversion of specific propositions and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which proposition types do not convert as themselves and reconstruct the logical refutation of an objection against conversion entailment.

Core Idea
Conversion preserves modality, not logical form; contradictions refute pseudo-generality.

This passage tackles a subtle point: the conversion of conditional and conventional propositions. The author clarifies that these propositions do not convert 'as themselves' – they preserve their modal character (necessity, perpetuity, etc.) but not the same propositional type. He then refutes an objection claiming that the contradictory of a universal negative is too general to entail a particular affirmative. By using the law of contradiction, he shows the entailment must hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two proposition types
Find the terms 'شرطيتين' (conditional) and 'عرفيتين' (conventional) in the passage. These are the types that do not convert as themselves.
2
Understand 'convert as themselves'
The author explains that conversion preserves modality: if the original is necessary (ضرورية), the converse is necessary; if perpetual (دائمة), perpetual; if conditional, conditional; if conventional, conventional. This applies whether the proposition is particular or universal.
Modality is preserved, not the logical form (e.g., a conditional does not become a categorical).
3
Trace the objector's argument
The objector claims that the contradictory of 'Every not-B is not-J' is 'Not every not-B is not-J'. He argues that this negative statement is more general than the positive 'Some not-B is J', so it cannot entail it.
The objector thinks entailment fails due to generality mismatch.
4
Follow the author's refutation
The author denies the generality claim. He asserts it is impossible for 'Not every not-B is not-J' to be true while 'Some not-B is J' is false, because they are logically equivalent through contradiction.
The key move: 'It is J' and 'It is not J' are contradictories; negating one affirms the other.
5
Apply the law of contradiction
Recognize that if 'Not every not-B is not-J' is true, then 'Every not-B is not-J' is false. Since 'Some not-B is J' is the contradictory of 'Every not-B is not-J', it must be true. This shows the entailment holds.
This refutes the objector's claim of generality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قولنا بعض ما ليس ب ج بالإطلاق العام؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف. وهذا على وفق مذهب الجمهور إلا في الخاصتين من الشرطيتين والعرفيتين: فإنهما لا تنعكسان كنفسهما لما مرَّ من قبل. والظاهر أن المراد من قوله كنفسها أنها إذا كانت ضرورية فضرورية، وإذا كانت دائمة فدائمة، وإذا كانت شرطية فشرطية، وإذا كانت عرفية فعرفية خاصة كانت كل واحدة منهما أو عامة. ومنهم من طعن في قوله وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج» ؛ إذ النقيض لقولنا كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» موجبا قولنا ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» سالبا ، وإنه أعم من الموجب المحصل وهو بعض ما ليس بج» فيمتنع استلزامه إياه لكن العمومية في حيز المنع. فإن من المحال أن يصدق قولنا ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج»؛ ولا يصدق قولنا بعض ما ليس «ب» «ج». وقد كان قولنا إنه «ج» يناقض قولنا إنه ليس «ج»، وبالعكس؛ فيلزم من نفي أحدهما إثبات الآخر وإنه مما ينافي العمومية.
Our statement 'Some of what is not B is J' (with absolute generality); and that it leads to impossibility as known. This is in accordance with the majority view except for the two specific ones among conditionals and conventional propositions: for they do not convert as themselves, as has passed earlier. The apparent meaning of 'as themselves' is that if they are necessary then necessary, if perpetual then perpetual, if conditional then conditional, if conventional then conventional, whether each of them is particular or universal. Among them are those who objected to his statement: 'otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our statement 'Some of what is not B is J''; for the contradictory of our statement 'Everything that is not B is not J' in positive form is our statement 'Not everything that is not B is not J' in negative form, and it is more general than the positive statement which is 'Some of what is not B is J'. So it is impossible for it to entail it. But the generality is subject to denial. For it is impossible that our statement 'Not everything that is not B is not J' is true while our statement 'Some of what is not B is J' is not true. And our statement that it is J contradicts our statement that it is not J, and vice versa; thus from the negation of one of them the affirmation of the other follows, and this is something that contradicts generality.
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a critical discussion of the conversion rules for certain types of propositions, specifically conditional and conventional propositions. The author begins by referencing a previous claim that stating 'Some of what is not B is J' under absolute generality leads to impossibility. He notes that this view aligns with the majority (جمهور) except for two specific types: conditional propositions (شرطيتين) and conventional propositions (عرفيتين). These two types do not convert as themselves, meaning that their modality (necessity, perpetuity, conditionality, conventionality) is preserved when converting—if the original is necessary, the converse is necessary; if perpetual, perpetual; etc., whether the proposition is particular or universal. The author then reports an objection from some critics: they argue that if the original statement 'Everything that is not B is not J' is true, then its contradictory ('Not everything that is not B is not J') would be false, and so the statement 'Some of what is not B is J' would be false; but the objector claims that this implication does not hold because the negative statement is more general than the positive particular. The author refutes this by asserting that the claim of generality is invalid: it is impossible for the negative statement to be true without the positive particular being true, because the negation of one contradictory implies the affirmation of the other. This impossibility shows that the objector's argument fails. The lesson illustrates the precision required in logical conversion and the importance of understanding contradictions.

Three Proofs for Conversion by Contraposition of Modal Propositions
Author presents three proofs for conversion by contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct and explain the three proofs for conversion by contraposition of positive temporal, existential, and possible propositions.

Core Idea
Conversion by contraposition for modal propositions requires multiple logical steps and remains contested.

In this lesson, we examine three proofs advanced by Imam Zain al-Din al-Kasi to show that positive temporal, existential, and possible propositions convert by contraposition into particular positives. These proofs rely upon reductio ad absurdum, relations between subject and predicate, and the existence of external individuals. Understanding these proofs is essential for mastering immediate inferences in modal logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim to be proved
The claim is that any positive temporal, existential, or possible proposition converts by contraposition to a particular positive proposition. For example, 'Every human is a breather' should yield 'Some non-breather is not human'.
The original proposition is assumed true.
2
Analyze the first proof (reductio)
Assume the converse by contraposition is false. Then its contradictory (a universal negative with negated subject and predicate) is true, which leads to a universal positive that predicates a more specific term on all individuals of a more general term—an impossibility.
Example: 'Every human is a breather' leads to 'Every non-breather is human', which is absurd because 'human' is more specific than 'non-breather'.
3
Analyze the second proof (subject-predicate relation)
The subject of the original proposition is either more specific than or disparate from the contradictory of the predicate. This prevents the subject from being true of all individuals of some part of the predicate, leading to the desired converse.
The proof relies on a logical relation between subject and negated predicate.
4
Analyze the third proof (external individuals)
There must exist individuals (existents or non-existents) that are neither the subject nor the predicate. For such individuals, 'non-predicate' and 'non-subject' apply, yielding the conversion.
This proof requires the existence of individuals outside the subject and predicate extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما في القضايا الباقية نحو الوقتيتين والوجوديتين والممكنتين وغير ذلك فعن الإمام زين الدين الكسي أنها إذا كانت موجبة تنعكس عكس النقيض إلى موجبة جزئية، واحتج عليه بوجوه : [1] منها أنها إذا لم تكن صادقة عند صدق الأصل فقد صدق نقيضها وهو السالبة الكلية الدائمة المعدولة الموضوع والمحمول المستلزمة للموجبة الكلية الدائمة المحصلة المحمول: وحينئذ يلزم حمل الأخص على كل فرد من أفراد الأعم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام وجب أن يصدق قولنا بعض اللا متنفس لا إنسان بالإطلاق العام وإلا فلا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ ويلزمه كل لا متنفس إنسان دائما. والإنسان أخص من اللا متنفس فيلزم حمل الأخص على كل أفراد الأعم وذلك محال. [ب] ومنها أن موضوع كل قضية فهو إما أخص من نقيض محموله أو مباين له مباينة كلية أو جزئية. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمكن ثبوت موضوعها لكل فرد من أفراد بعض محمولها؛ ويلزم من ذلك ثبوت نقيض موضوعها لبعض أفراد نقيض محمولها الذي هو المطلوب. [ت] ومنها أنه لا بد وأن توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن الموضوع والمحمول يصدق عليه أنه لا موضوع ولا محمول بالفعل فيصدق حينئذ بعض اللا محمول لا موضوع؛ وهو المطلوب. ومنهم من طعن في الوجه الأول منها وقال ما ذكرتم من اللزوم في حيز المنع فقولنا في المثال المذكور كل لا متنفس إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ وكيف وأنه أعم منه وكذلك أخصية الموضوع من نقيض المحمول وامتناع حمله على جميع أفراد نقيض المحمول؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع أيضًا لما أن الموضوع وهو الإنسان في المثال ينقسم إلى المتنفس واللا متنفس. ثمَّ المثال من جملة ما لا يعد من البراهين فإن من الأمثلة ما ليس كذلك كما إذا كان كل واحد من الطرفين أعني الموضوع والمحمول شاملاً بجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق كلها أو بعضها فيقال مثلا كل موجود إما قديم وإما حادث : فإن كل واحد منهما أي من الموضوع والمحمول شامل لجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق لبعضها وهو الحوادث الباقية بعد الحدوث لما أن المعنى من الحادث هو الموجود في زمان الحدوث. وبهذا يظهر الفساد في الثاني منها فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الموضوع في القضية أخص من نقيض المحمول أو مباينا له. وأما في الثالث فليس كل موضوع و محمول بحيث توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عنهما بل منهما ما يكون شاملا للموجودات والمعدومات نحو الممكن العام فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الممكن بالإمكان العام ضروري الوجود
As for the remaining propositions, such as temporal, existential, possible, and others, it is reported from Imam Zain al-Din al-Kasi that when they are positive, they convert by contraposition to a particular positive. He adduced for this [with] proofs: [1] Among them: that if they are not true when the original is true, then its contradictory is true, which is the universal negative perpetual with negated subject and predicate entailing the universal positive perpetual with preserved predicate. Then it necessitates the predication of the more specific on every individual of the more general. That is because if our statement 'Every human is a breather' is true with absolute generality, then our statement 'Some non-breather is not human' must be true with absolute generality; otherwise, 'No non-breather is not human perpetually' and it entails 'Every non-breather is a human perpetually'. And 'human' is more specific than 'non-breather', so it necessitates predication of the more specific on all individuals of the more general, which is impossible. [B] Among them: that the subject of every proposition is either more specific than the contradictory of its predicate or completely or partially disparate from it. Since this is the case, it is impossible for its subject to be established for every individual of some of its predicate; and from that follows the establishment of the contradictory of its subject for some individuals of the contradictory of its predicate, which is what is desired. [C] Among them: that there must exist existents or non-existents outside the subject and predicate of which it is true that it is neither subject nor predicate actually; so then 'Some non-predicate is non-subject' is true, which is what is desired. And among them are those who criticized the first proof and said: what you mentioned of implication is subject to denial; for our statement in the mentioned example 'Every non-breather is human' cannot be among the necessary consequences of our statement 'No non-breather is not human perpetually'; how so, while it is more general than it? Likewise, the specificity of the subject relative to the contradictory of the predicate and the impossibility of its predication on all individuals of the contradictory of the predicate; for it is also subject to denial because the subject, which is 'human' in the example, is divided into breather and non-breather. Then the example is among those that are not counted as proofs, for among examples are those that are not like that, as when each of the two sides, that is, subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from all or some of them. So it is said, for example: 'Every existent is either eternal or originated.' For each of them, i.e., subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from some of them, which are originated things remaining after origination, because the meaning of 'originated' is that which exists at the time of origination. By this the corruption in the second [proof] becomes apparent, for it is not necessary that the subject in the proposition be more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or disparate from it. As for the third, not every subject and predicate are such that there exist existents or non-existents outside them, rather among them are those that encompass existents and non-existents, like the general possible, for when we say 'Some possible by general possibility is necessary of existence'
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and partial critique of three proofs for the conversion by contraposition (عكس النقيض) of positive temporal, existential, and possible propositions into particular positives. The author attributes the proofs to Imam Zain al-Din al-Kasi. The first proof uses a reductio: if the converse by contraposition were false, then the contradictory of the original would lead to an impossibility, namely that the more specific (e.g., 'human') would be predicated of all individuals of the more general (e.g., 'non-breather'). The second proof argues that the subject is either more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or disparate from it, and from that the desired conversion follows. The third proof relies on the existence of individuals outside the subject and predicate. The author then reports a criticism of the first proof, which denies the claimed implication, noting that 'Every non-breather is human' is not necessarily entailed by 'No non-breather is not human perpetually' because the former is more general. The criticism also points out that the subject 'human' divides into breather and non-breather, undermining the proof. The critic adds that the example fails when both subject and predicate are all-encompassing, as in 'Every existent is either eternal or originated'. The criticism then claims that the second proof is also invalid because the subject may not be more specific or disparate from the contradictory of the predicate. Regarding the third proof, the critic notes that not every subject and predicate have individuals outside them; some, like 'general possible', encompass both existents and non-existents. The text cuts off mid-sentence, so the full criticism is incomplete. This lesson demonstrates the dialectical process of proposing and critiquing logical proofs.

Critique of the First Proof for Conversion by Contraposition
Critique of the first proof for conversion by contraposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the critic's objection to the first proof and evaluate its validity using the rules of logical implication.

Core Idea
A valid proof must hold for all cases; the critic shows the first proof fails because the subject term is not simple.

Having presented three proofs, the author reports a critique of the first proof. The critic argues that the claimed implication—from the falsity of the converse to an absurd predication—does not necessarily follow. The example of 'human' and 'breather' is shown to be flawed because 'human' divides into 'breather' and 'non-breather'. This lesson examines the critique and its implications for the validity of the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target of the critique
The critic targets the first proof, specifically the implication that if the converse is false then 'Every non-breather is human' necessarily follows from 'No non-breather is not human perpetually'.
The example is 'Every human is a breather'.
2
Understand the critic's main point
The critic denies the implication, arguing that 'Every non-breather is human' is more general than 'No non-breather is not human perpetually', so it is not a necessary consequence. Moreover, 'human' is not a simple kind—it divides into breather and non-breather, so the relation of specificity is not straightforward.
The critic uses the division of the subject to show the proof's assumption is false.
3
Apply the critic's reasoning to other examples
The critic notes that when subject and predicate are all-encompassing, e.g., 'Every existent is either eternal or originated', the proof fails because the subject and predicate cover all existents, leaving no room for the specificity argument.
This shows the proof is not universally valid.
4
Evaluate the critique's impact
The critique undermines the first proof. If the first proof fails, the second and third proofs are also questioned, as the critic briefly indicates their flaws. The passage is cut off, but the critique suggests the proofs are not conclusive.
The incomplete text prevents full evaluation of the critique of the third proof.

وأما في القضايا الباقية نحو الوقتيتين والوجوديتين والممكنتين وغير ذلك فعن الإمام زين الدين الكسي أنها إذا كانت موجبة تنعكس عكس النقيض إلى موجبة جزئية، واحتج عليه بوجوه : [1] منها أنها إذا لم تكن صادقة عند صدق الأصل فقد صدق نقيضها وهو السالبة الكلية الدائمة المعدولة الموضوع والمحمول المستلزمة للموجبة الكلية الدائمة المحصلة المحمول: وحينئذ يلزم حمل الأخص على كل فرد من أفراد الأعم؛ وذلك لأنه إذا صدق قولنا كل إنسان متنفس بالإطلاق العام وجب أن يصدق قولنا بعض اللا متنفس لا إنسان بالإطلاق العام وإلا فلا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ ويلزمه كل لا متنفس إنسان دائما. والإنسان أخص من اللا متنفس فيلزم حمل الأخص على كل أفراد الأعم وذلك محال. [ب] ومنها أن موضوع كل قضية فهو إما أخص من نقيض محموله أو مباين له مباينة كلية أو جزئية. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمكن ثبوت موضوعها لكل فرد من أفراد بعض محمولها؛ ويلزم من ذلك ثبوت نقيض موضوعها لبعض أفراد نقيض محمولها الذي هو المطلوب. [ت] ومنها أنه لا بد وأن توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن الموضوع والمحمول يصدق عليه أنه لا موضوع ولا محمول بالفعل فيصدق حينئذ بعض اللا محمول لا موضوع؛ وهو المطلوب. ومنهم من طعن في الوجه الأول منها وقال ما ذكرتم من اللزوم في حيز المنع فقولنا في المثال المذكور كل لا متنفس إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ وكيف وأنه أعم منه وكذلك أخصية الموضوع من نقيض المحمول وامتناع حمله على جميع أفراد نقيض المحمول؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع أيضًا لما أن الموضوع وهو الإنسان في المثال ينقسم إلى المتنفس واللا متنفس. ثمَّ المثال من جملة ما لا يعد من البراهين فإن من الأمثلة ما ليس كذلك كما إذا كان كل واحد من الطرفين أعني الموضوع والمحمول شاملاً بجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق كلها أو بعضها فيقال مثلا كل موجود إما قديم وإما حادث : فإن كل واحد منهما أي من الموضوع والمحمول شامل لجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق لبعضها وهو الحوادث الباقية بعد الحدوث لما أن المعنى من الحادث هو الموجود في زمان الحدوث. وبهذا يظهر الفساد في الثاني منها فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الموضوع في القضية أخص من نقيض المحمول أو مباينا له. وأما في الثالث فليس كل موضوع و محمول بحيث توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عنهما بل منهما ما يكون شاملا للموجودات والمعدومات نحو الممكن العام فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الممكن بالإمكان العام ضروري الوجود
As for the remaining propositions, such as temporal, existential, possible, and others, it is reported from Imam Zain al-Din al-Kasi that when they are positive, they convert by contraposition to a particular positive. He adduced for this [with] proofs: [1] Among them: that if they are not true when the original is true, then its contradictory is true, which is the universal negative perpetual with negated subject and predicate entailing the universal positive perpetual with preserved predicate. Then it necessitates the predication of the more specific on every individual of the more general. That is because if our statement 'Every human is a breather' is true with absolute generality, then our statement 'Some non-breather is not human' must be true with absolute generality; otherwise, 'No non-breather is not human perpetually' and it entails 'Every non-breather is a human perpetually'. And 'human' is more specific than 'non-breather', so it necessitates predication of the more specific on all individuals of the more general, which is impossible. [B] Among them: that the subject of every proposition is either more specific than the contradictory of its predicate or completely or partially disparate from it. Since this is the case, it is impossible for its subject to be established for every individual of some of its predicate; and from that follows the establishment of the contradictory of its subject for some individuals of the contradictory of its predicate, which is what is desired. [C] Among them: that there must exist existents or non-existents outside the subject and predicate of which it is true that it is neither subject nor predicate actually; so then 'Some non-predicate is non-subject' is true, which is what is desired. And among them are those who criticized the first proof and said: what you mentioned of implication is subject to denial; for our statement in the mentioned example 'Every non-breather is human' cannot be among the necessary consequences of our statement 'No non-breather is not human perpetually'; how so, while it is more general than it? Likewise, the specificity of the subject relative to the contradictory of the predicate and the impossibility of its predication on all individuals of the contradictory of the predicate; for it is also subject to denial because the subject, which is 'human' in the example, is divided into breather and non-breather. Then the example is among those that are not counted as proofs, for among examples are those that are not like that, as when each of the two sides, that is, subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from all or some of them. So it is said, for example: 'Every existent is either eternal or originated.' For each of them, i.e., subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from some of them, which are originated things remaining after origination, because the meaning of 'originated' is that which exists at the time of origination. By this the corruption in the second [proof] becomes apparent, for it is not necessary that the subject in the proposition be more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or disparate from it. As for the third, not every subject and predicate are such that there exist existents or non-existents outside them, rather among them are those that encompass existents and non-existents, like the general possible, for when we say 'Some possible by general possibility is necessary of existence'
Source pages p. 495

Commentary
This passage is a presentation and partial critique of three proofs for the conversion by contraposition (عكس النقيض) of positive temporal, existential, and possible propositions into particular positives. The author attributes the proofs to Imam Zain al-Din al-Kasi. The first proof uses a reductio: if the converse by contraposition were false, then the contradictory of the original would lead to an impossibility, namely that the more specific (e.g., 'human') would be predicated of all individuals of the more general (e.g., 'non-breather'). The second proof argues that the subject is either more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or disparate from it, and from that the desired conversion follows. The third proof relies on the existence of individuals outside the subject and predicate. The author then reports a criticism of the first proof, which denies the claimed implication, noting that 'Every non-breather is human' is not necessarily entailed by 'No non-breather is not human perpetually' because the former is more general. The criticism also points out that the subject 'human' divides into breather and non-breather, undermining the proof. The critic adds that the example fails when both subject and predicate are all-encompassing, as in 'Every existent is either eternal or originated'. The criticism then claims that the second proof is also invalid because the subject may not be more specific or disparate from the contradictory of the predicate. Regarding the third proof, the critic notes that not every subject and predicate have individuals outside them; some, like 'general possible', encompass both existents and non-existents. The text cuts off mid-sentence, so the full criticism is incomplete. This lesson demonstrates the dialectical process of proposing and critiquing logical proofs.

وجودية

B) Because 'human' can be divided into 'breather' and 'non-breather', so the specificity relation is not straightforward.
C) Because the original proposition is false.
D) Because 'non-breather' is more specific than 'human'.

Key Takeaway
The critique of the first proof shows that logical proofs must be universally applicable; a single counterexample can undermine a proof that relies on specific term relations.

Conditions for Contrapositive Validity and Their Critique
Author states conditions for contrapositive validity; critic raises counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two necessary conditions for the contrapositive (aks al-naqid) of a categorical proposition and explain why a critic found the first condition problematic.

Core Idea
Contrapositive validity requires the subject to be more specific than the contradictory of its predicate.

The contrapositive—converting 'All A are B' into 'All non-B are non-A'—is a powerful tool, but it is not always valid. Classical logicians specified conditions under which the contrapositive holds. In this passage, the author presents two such conditions: the subject must be more specific than the contradictory of its predicate, and there must be entities external to both terms. However, a critic challenges the first condition with a counterexample that exposes its limits. By analyzing this debate, you will learn to critically evaluate when the contrapositive can be applied.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two conditions for contrapositive validity as stated by the author.
Locate in the Arabic text where the author lists the conditions: 'أخص من نقيض محموله' (more specific than the contradictory of the predicate) and existence of external entities. Restate in your own words.
The first condition ensures logical necessity; the second ensures non-vacuous fulfillment.
2
Understand why the critic denies the first condition.
The critic says the example 'Every non-respiring is a human' does not follow from 'No non-respiring is without a human'. Why? Because the subject 'non-respiring' is more general than 'human' (its contradictory is 'non-human'), so the specificity condition fails.
The critic's point is that the condition is not necessary for the inference; the example shows a case where the condition fails yet the contrapositive might still hold? Actually the critic says it does not hold. So it is a counterexample to the condition being sufficient? The critic is attacking the necessity of the condition.
3
Recognize the critic's broader objection.
The critic extends the attack by considering propositions where both subject and predicate are universal, such as 'Every existent is either eternal or originated'. Here, the subject ('existent') is not more specific than the contradictory of the predicate ('non-eternal' is not a term), so again the condition fails.
This shows that the condition is not universally applicable.
4
Formulate the debate in your own mind.
Summarize the author's position and the critic's objection as a dialectical exchange. What is at stake? The validity conditions of a fundamental logical operation.

أخص من نقيض محموله أو مباين له مباينة كلية أو جزئية. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمكن ثبوت موضوعها لكل فرد من أفراد بعض محمولها؛ ويلزم من ذلك ثبوت نقيض موضوعها لبعض أفراد نقيض محمولها الذي هو المطلوب. ومنها أنه لا بد وأن توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن الموضوع والمحمول يصدق عليه أنه لا موضوع ولا محمول بالفعل فيصدق حينئذ بعض اللا محمول لا موضوع؛ وهو المطلوب. ومنهم من طعن في الوجه الأول منها وقال ما ذكرتم من اللزوم في حيز المنع فقولنا في المثال المذكور كل لا متنفس إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ وكيف وأنه أعم منه وكذلك أخصية الموضوع من نقيض المحمول وامتناع حمله على جميع أفراد نقيض المحمول؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع أيضًا لما أن الموضوع وهو الإنسان في المثال ينقسم إلى المتنفس واللا متنفس. ثمَّ المثال من جملة ما لا يعد من البراهين فإن من الأمثلة ما ليس كذلك كما إذا كان كل واحد من الطرفين أعني الموضوع والمحمول شاملاً بجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق كلها أو بعضها فيقال مثلا كل موجود إما قديم وإما حادث : فإن كل واحد منهما أي من الموضوع والمحمول شامل لجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق لبعضها وهو الحوادث الباقية بعد الحدوث لما أن المعنى من الحادث هو الموجود في زمان الحدوث. وبهذا يظهر الفساد في الثاني منها فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الموضوع في القضية أخص من نقيض المحمول أو مباينا له. وأما في الثالث فليس كل موضوع و محمول بحيث توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عنهما بل منهما ما يكون شاملا للموجودات والمعدومات نحو الممكن العام فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الممكن بالإمكان العام ضروري الوجود لم يصدق معه بعض ما ليس بضروري الوجود ليس بالإمكان العام؛ وقد كان المعنى من الممكن بالإمكان العام ما ليس بضروري مطلقا : فهذه خلاصة طعنهم في الحجج التي مرَّ ذكرها . وقد يقال في الطعن الأول وهو منع الملزوم إن ذلك من جملة ما تقدم ذكره وتقرر ؛ وفي الثاني أنه مدفوع بالعناية كما إذا قيل يعنى بكونه أعم أنه من جملة ما ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره؛ وفي الثالث وهو المثال المذكور أن الكلام فيما يمكن وجوده في الخارج، والمحمول فيما ذكرتم وهو أحدهما لا على التعيين فلا يمكن أن يوجد في الخارج أصلا، ولو أمكن وجوده لكان الكلام فيه كما في الغير؛ فلا فائدة في التعرُّض لذلك المثال؛ وفي الرابع أن الممكن بالإمكان العام عبارة عما لا يكون ممتنعا عند الجمهور من المتقدمين والمتأخرين، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون كما ذكرتم. وبالجملة فما ذهب إليه الإمام الكسي فهو حق باعتبار لزوم الشيء للشيء في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ والشيء لا يكون من لوازم الغير إلا وأن يكون عدم الغير من لوازمه. فأما باعتبار حمل الشيء على الشيء ففيه من الكلام؛ وقد مرّ من قبل أنه لا يلزم من حمل الشيء على الشيء أنه من لوازم ذلك الشيء على معنى أنه لا يمكن وجوده دون وجود ذلك، ولا عدم ذلك دون عدمه. وأما ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين فهذه القضايا إذا كانت خارجية مطلقة يلزمها من عكس النقيض إذا قيد الموضوع بما يناقض جهة القضية من السوالب . أما من أقسام سالبة الموضوع فخارجية مطلقة كلية دائمة كما في قولنا كل (ج) موجود في الخارج فهو «ب» في الخارج. فإنه إذا صدق هذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) بموجود في الخارج وهو (ب) بموجود في الخارج.
[The subject is] more specific than the contradictory of its predicate or completely or partially different from it. Since this is the case, it is not possible for its subject to be affirmed of every individual of some of its predicates; and from that follows the affirmation of the contradictory of its subject for some individuals of the contradictory of its predicate, which is what is desired. And among them is that there must exist existents or non-existents external to the subject and predicate, such that it is true that it is neither subject nor predicate in actuality, then at that time some of the not-predicate is not subject; and that is what is desired. And among them is one who criticized the first of these aspects and said: What you mentioned of necessity is in the realm of denial, for our statement in the mentioned example "Every non-respiring is a human" cannot be a necessary consequence of our statement "No non-respiring is without a human at all times"; how so, since it is more general than it, and likewise the specificity of the subject from the contradictory of the predicate and the impossibility of its predication on all individuals of the contradictory of the predicate; for it is also in the realm of denial, because the subject, which is "human" in the example, is divided into the respiring and the non-respiring. Then the example is among those that are not counted as demonstrations, for among examples are those that are not like that, as when each of the two sides, namely subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from all or some of them, so for example it is said "Every existent is either eternal or originated": then each of them, i.e., subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from some of them, namely the originated things remaining after origination, because the meaning of "originated" is that which exists at the time of origination. And by this the corruption of the second of them becomes apparent, for it is not necessary that the subject in the proposition be more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or differing from it. As for the third, not every subject and predicate is such that there exist existents or non-existents external to them, but rather some of them encompass existents and non-existents, such as the absolute possible, for if we say "Some absolute possible is necessary of existence" it is not true with it that "Some non-necessary of existence is not absolute possible"; and the meaning of "absolute possible" was that which is not necessary absolutely. So this is the summary of their criticism of the arguments that have been mentioned. And it may be said regarding the first criticism, which is the denial of the entailed, that that is among what has already been mentioned and established; and regarding the second, that it is repelled by attention (bi-al-'inaya), as if it is said: by its being more general it is meant that it is among that which is divided into it and other than it; and regarding the third, which is the mentioned example, that the discussion concerns what can exist in the external, and the predicate in what you mentioned, which is one of them not specifically determined, cannot exist in the external at all, and if it could exist, then the discussion about it would be as about the other; so there is no benefit in raising that example; and regarding the fourth, that the absolute possible is an expression for what is not impossible according to the majority of the earlier and later scholars, and if it is so, then it cannot be as you mentioned. In summary, what Imam al-Kashshi held is true with respect to the necessary entailment of a thing for a thing in every one of these forms; and a thing is not a necessary concomitant of another unless the non-existence of the other is a necessary concomitant of it. But with respect to the predication of one thing on another, there is discussion about it; and it has passed before that it does not follow from the predication of a thing on a thing that it is a necessary concomitant of that thing in the sense that it cannot exist without the existence of that, nor that its non-existence cannot be without the non-existence of that. And as for what a group of earlier scholars held: these propositions, if they are absolute external (kharijiyyah mutlaqah), entail from contrapositive when the subject is restricted by what contradicts the modality of the proposition from among negative [propositions]. Among the types of negative subject propositions is the absolute external universal perpetual, as in our statement "Every (j) existing in the external is (b) in the external." For if this is true, then our statement "No (j) is existing in the external while it is (b) existing in the external" is true.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding the conditions under which the contrapositive (aks al-naqid) of a categorical proposition is valid. The author first lays out two arguments for a necessary relation between subject and predicate: (1) the subject is more specific than the contradictory of the predicate, and (2) there must be entities external to subject and predicate. Then a critic attacks these arguments, giving counterexamples. The critic says the first inferred necessity is not valid; e.g., 'Every non-respiring is a human' does not necessarily follow from 'No non-respiring is without a human at all times' because the example is not a proper demonstration. Further, if both subject and predicate are universal (e.g., 'Every existent is either eternal or originated'), the subject is not necessarily more specific. The critic also rejects the third argument by noting that some terms like 'absolute possible' include both existents and non-existents, so the requirement of external entities fails. The author then responds: the first criticism is already established and not problematic; the second is repelled by reinterpreting 'more general' as divisibility; the third example is irrelevant because the predicate in question cannot exist externally; the fourth criticism misinterprets 'absolute possible'—according to the majority, it means 'not impossible', so the example stands. Finally, the author agrees with Imam al-Kashshi that necessary entailment is about the impossibility of existence without the other, not about predication. The passage ends with a discussion of 'absolute external' propositions and their contrapositive, using the variables ج and ب.

اللزوم
خارجية مطلقة

C) The subject must be more specific than the contradictory of its predicate.
D) The subject must be empty.

Key Takeaway
The validity of contrapositive inference depends on the logical relation between the subject and the contradictory of the predicate, not on the truth of the original proposition alone.

Defense of Contrapositive Conditions and Special Cases
Author rebuts criticisms and refines the conditions for contrapositive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's defense against the criticisms of the contrapositive conditions and identify the special case of absolute external propositions.

Core Idea
The author defends the conditions by reinterpreting 'more general' and showing that some counterexamples are irrelevant.

After the critic's objections, the author mounts a defense. He argues that the first criticism is already settled, redefines 'more general' in terms of divisibility, dismisses the problematic example as irrelevant to real existence, and corrects the misunderstanding of 'absolute possible.' Finally, he endorses Imam al-Kashshi's distinction between necessary entailment and predication, and introduces the special case of absolute external propositions where the contrapositive requires a specific restriction on the subject. This lesson walks through the author's rebuttals and their logical import.

Guided Path
1
Understand the author's response to the first criticism.
The author says the denial of the entailed is already established. What does that mean? It refers to the previous argument that the specificity condition is necessary.
The author dismisses the first criticism as not novel.
2
Analyze the author's reinterpretation of 'more general' in the second criticism.
The author says that 'more general' here means that the subject is divided into it and other things (i.e., it is a genus that includes the predicate). So the condition is about the logical relationship, not just extension.
This reframes the condition to handle cases where the subject is more general but still has a specific relation.
3
Examine why the example of 'every non-respiring is a human' is rejected.
The author claims that the predicate in that example does not refer to something that can exist in the external world, so the example is not relevant to real-world logical inference.
This is a critique of the critic's choice of example.
4
Look at the author's handling of 'absolute possible'.
The author argues that 'absolute possible' is defined as 'what is not impossible' by the majority, so the counterexample fails.
This shows the importance of precise definitions in logical disputes.
5
Understand Imam al-Kashshi's distinction and the special condition for absolute external propositions.
The author distinguishes between necessary entailment (luzum) and predication (haml). For absolute external universal propositions, the contrapositive is valid when the subject is restricted by the contradictory of the proposition's modality (i.e., from negative to affirmative?).
This is a nuanced refinement that shows the author's position to be more sophisticated.
