أخص من نقيض محموله أو مباين له مباينة كلية أو جزئية. ولما كانت الحالة هذه فلا يمكن ثبوت موضوعها لكل فرد من أفراد بعض محمولها؛ ويلزم من ذلك ثبوت نقيض موضوعها لبعض أفراد نقيض محمولها الذي هو المطلوب. ومنها أنه لا بد وأن توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن الموضوع والمحمول يصدق عليه أنه لا موضوع ولا محمول بالفعل فيصدق حينئذ بعض اللا محمول لا موضوع؛ وهو المطلوب. ومنهم من طعن في الوجه الأول منها وقال ما ذكرتم من اللزوم في حيز المنع فقولنا في المثال المذكور كل لا متنفس إنسان لا يمكن أن يكون من لوازم قولنا لا شيء من اللا متنفس بلا إنسان دائما؛ وكيف وأنه أعم منه وكذلك أخصية الموضوع من نقيض المحمول وامتناع حمله على جميع أفراد نقيض المحمول؛ إذ هو في حيز المنع أيضًا لما أن الموضوع وهو الإنسان في المثال ينقسم إلى المتنفس واللا متنفس. ثمَّ المثال من جملة ما لا يعد من البراهين فإن من الأمثلة ما ليس كذلك كما إذا كان كل واحد من الطرفين أعني الموضوع والمحمول شاملاً بجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق كلها أو بعضها فيقال مثلا كل موجود إما قديم وإما حادث : فإن كل واحد منهما أي من الموضوع والمحمول شامل لجميع الموجودات؛ والمحمول يفارق لبعضها وهو الحوادث الباقية بعد الحدوث لما أن المعنى من الحادث هو الموجود في زمان الحدوث. وبهذا يظهر الفساد في الثاني منها فإنه لا يلزم أن يكون الموضوع في القضية أخص من نقيض المحمول أو مباينا له. وأما في الثالث فليس كل موضوع و محمول بحيث توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عنهما بل منهما ما يكون شاملا للموجودات والمعدومات نحو الممكن العام فإنا إذا قلنا بعض الممكن بالإمكان العام ضروري الوجود لم يصدق معه بعض ما ليس بضروري الوجود ليس بالإمكان العام؛ وقد كان المعنى من الممكن بالإمكان العام ما ليس بضروري مطلقا : فهذه خلاصة طعنهم في الحجج التي مرَّ ذكرها . وقد يقال في الطعن الأول وهو منع الملزوم إن ذلك من جملة ما تقدم ذكره وتقرر ؛ وفي الثاني أنه مدفوع بالعناية كما إذا قيل يعنى بكونه أعم أنه من جملة ما ينقسم إليه وإلى غيره؛ وفي الثالث وهو المثال المذكور أن الكلام فيما يمكن وجوده في الخارج، والمحمول فيما ذكرتم وهو أحدهما لا على التعيين فلا يمكن أن يوجد في الخارج أصلا، ولو أمكن وجوده لكان الكلام فيه كما في الغير؛ فلا فائدة في التعرُّض لذلك المثال؛ وفي الرابع أن الممكن بالإمكان العام عبارة عما لا يكون ممتنعا عند الجمهور من المتقدمين والمتأخرين، ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون كما ذكرتم. وبالجملة فما ذهب إليه الإمام الكسي فهو حق باعتبار لزوم الشيء للشيء في كل صورة من هذه الصور؛ والشيء لا يكون من لوازم الغير إلا وأن يكون عدم الغير من لوازمه. فأما باعتبار حمل الشيء على الشيء ففيه من الكلام؛ وقد مرّ من قبل أنه لا يلزم من حمل الشيء على الشيء أنه من لوازم ذلك الشيء على معنى أنه لا يمكن وجوده دون وجود ذلك، ولا عدم ذلك دون عدمه. وأما ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين فهذه القضايا إذا كانت خارجية مطلقة يلزمها من عكس النقيض إذا قيد الموضوع بما يناقض جهة القضية من السوالب . أما من أقسام سالبة الموضوع فخارجية مطلقة كلية دائمة كما في قولنا كل (ج) موجود في الخارج فهو «ب» في الخارج. فإنه إذا صدق هذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) بموجود في الخارج وهو (ب) بموجود في الخارج.
[The subject is] more specific than the contradictory of its predicate or completely or partially different from it. Since this is the case, it is not possible for its subject to be affirmed of every individual of some of its predicates; and from that follows the affirmation of the contradictory of its subject for some individuals of the contradictory of its predicate, which is what is desired. And among them is that there must exist existents or non-existents external to the subject and predicate, such that it is true that it is neither subject nor predicate in actuality, then at that time some of the not-predicate is not subject; and that is what is desired. And among them is one who criticized the first of these aspects and said: What you mentioned of necessity is in the realm of denial, for our statement in the mentioned example "Every non-respiring is a human" cannot be a necessary consequence of our statement "No non-respiring is without a human at all times"; how so, since it is more general than it, and likewise the specificity of the subject from the contradictory of the predicate and the impossibility of its predication on all individuals of the contradictory of the predicate; for it is also in the realm of denial, because the subject, which is "human" in the example, is divided into the respiring and the non-respiring. Then the example is among those that are not counted as demonstrations, for among examples are those that are not like that, as when each of the two sides, namely subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from all or some of them, so for example it is said "Every existent is either eternal or originated": then each of them, i.e., subject and predicate, encompasses all existents; and the predicate differs from some of them, namely the originated things remaining after origination, because the meaning of "originated" is that which exists at the time of origination. And by this the corruption of the second of them becomes apparent, for it is not necessary that the subject in the proposition be more specific than the contradictory of the predicate or differing from it. As for the third, not every subject and predicate is such that there exist existents or non-existents external to them, but rather some of them encompass existents and non-existents, such as the absolute possible, for if we say "Some absolute possible is necessary of existence" it is not true with it that "Some non-necessary of existence is not absolute possible"; and the meaning of "absolute possible" was that which is not necessary absolutely. So this is the summary of their criticism of the arguments that have been mentioned. And it may be said regarding the first criticism, which is the denial of the entailed, that that is among what has already been mentioned and established; and regarding the second, that it is repelled by attention (bi-al-'inaya), as if it is said: by its being more general it is meant that it is among that which is divided into it and other than it; and regarding the third, which is the mentioned example, that the discussion concerns what can exist in the external, and the predicate in what you mentioned, which is one of them not specifically determined, cannot exist in the external at all, and if it could exist, then the discussion about it would be as about the other; so there is no benefit in raising that example; and regarding the fourth, that the absolute possible is an expression for what is not impossible according to the majority of the earlier and later scholars, and if it is so, then it cannot be as you mentioned. In summary, what Imam al-Kashshi held is true with respect to the necessary entailment of a thing for a thing in every one of these forms; and a thing is not a necessary concomitant of another unless the non-existence of the other is a necessary concomitant of it. But with respect to the predication of one thing on another, there is discussion about it; and it has passed before that it does not follow from the predication of a thing on a thing that it is a necessary concomitant of that thing in the sense that it cannot exist without the existence of that, nor that its non-existence cannot be without the non-existence of that. And as for what a group of earlier scholars held: these propositions, if they are absolute external (kharijiyyah mutlaqah), entail from contrapositive when the subject is restricted by what contradicts the modality of the proposition from among negative [propositions]. Among the types of negative subject propositions is the absolute external universal perpetual, as in our statement "Every (j) existing in the external is (b) in the external." For if this is true, then our statement "No (j) is existing in the external while it is (b) existing in the external" is true.
Source pages p. 496

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange regarding the conditions under which the contrapositive (aks al-naqid) of a categorical proposition is valid. The author first lays out two arguments for a necessary relation between subject and predicate: (1) the subject is more specific than the contradictory of the predicate, and (2) there must be entities external to subject and predicate. Then a critic attacks these arguments, giving counterexamples. The critic says the first inferred necessity is not valid; e.g., 'Every non-respiring is a human' does not necessarily follow from 'No non-respiring is without a human at all times' because the example is not a proper demonstration. Further, if both subject and predicate are universal (e.g., 'Every existent is either eternal or originated'), the subject is not necessarily more specific. The critic also rejects the third argument by noting that some terms like 'absolute possible' include both existents and non-existents, so the requirement of external entities fails. The author then responds: the first criticism is already established and not problematic; the second is repelled by reinterpreting 'more general' as divisibility; the third example is irrelevant because the predicate in question cannot exist externally; the fourth criticism misinterprets 'absolute possible'—according to the majority, it means 'not impossible', so the example stands. Finally, the author agrees with Imam al-Kashshi that necessary entailment is about the impossibility of existence without the other, not about predication. The passage ends with a discussion of 'absolute external' propositions and their contrapositive, using the variables ج and ب.

اللزوم
خارجية مطلقة

The Key Condition for Contraposition: Restricting the Subject by the Contradictory Mode
Author explains contraposition conditions for absolute external propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition required for contraposition of modal propositions and apply it to absolute external propositions, verifying the inference by reductio.

Core Idea
Contraposition requires restricting the subject by the contradictory of the proposition's mode.

In this passage, the author explains how to perform contraposition on propositions with modalities like 'absolute external'. The key is that the subject term in the contrapositive must be restricted by the contradictory of the original mode. This ensures the inference preserves truth and is validated by a proof by contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition's type and mode
Determine if it is external (kharijiyya) or essential (haqiqiyya), and its mode (e.g., perpetual, general, necessary). For absolute external, the mode is perpetual (dā'im).
For example, 'Every C existing externally is B externally' is absolute external universal perpetual.
2
Find the contradictory of the mode
For a perpetual mode, the contradictory is 'non-perpetual' (i.e., 'sometimes not'). This restriction will be added to the subject of the contrapositive.
The passage states: 'if the subject is restricted by what contradicts the mode of the proposition'.
3
Form the contrapositive
Take the contradictory of the predicate as the new subject, and the contradictory of the original subject as the new predicate, while preserving the negation and affirmation as in the original. Then restrict the new subject by the contradictory mode.
For the example: 'Nothing that is not B perpetually external is C perpetually external'.
4
Verify using reductio ad absurdum
Assume the contrapositive is false. Its contradictory will be true: 'Some non-B perpetually external is C externally'. Combine this with the original ('All C is B externally') to derive 'Some non-B perpetually external is B externally', which is a contradiction since a non-B cannot be B.
The passage explicitly presents this proof: 'else its contradictory would be true... this is a contradiction'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين فهذه القضايا إذا كانت خارجية مطلقة يلزمها من عكس النقيض إذا قيد الموضوع بما يناقض جهة القضية من السوالب . أما من أقسام سالبة الموضوع فخارجية مطلقة كلية دائمة كما في قولنا كل (ج) موجود في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج. فإنه إذا صدق هذا صدق قولنا لا شيء مما هو ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج هو (ج) دائما في الخارج وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض ما ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج (ج) في الخارج بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان كل (ج) في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج. فينتج بعض ما ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج (ب) بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف. ثم إنه في المطلقة العامة ولا يختلف البرهان فيما عداها إلا في قيد الموضوع فإنه يجب أن يقيد في كل قضية بما يناقض جهتها نحو الضرورة في الممكنة العامة؛ وبالعكس. وإذا كانت حقيقية مطلقة يلزمها كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لكونها أعم من الأولى ولتمام البرهان. فإن نقيضها مع الأصل ينتج أن بعض ما ليس بملزوم للباء دائما فهو (ب) في الخارج، وذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان (ب) في الخارج كان موجودًا في الخارج موصوفًا به فيكون ملزوما للباء بالإطلاق وإنه ليس بملزوم له دائما، هذا خلف. ولأنه إذا صدق الأصل صدق أن كل (ج) ملزوم للباء ويلزمه هذه القضية من غير هذا التكلُّف. وأما من أقسام معدولة الموضوع فالخارجية يلزمها كلية دائمة خارجية مطلقة لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو لا (ب) دائمًا مما وجد في الخارج فهو (ب) بالإطلاق العام، وذلك محال. وللحقيقية كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو ملزوم لسلب الباء دائما فهو (ب) في الخارج، وإنه محال أيضًا لأنه يجب وجوده في الخارج حتى يثبت له الباء. وإنه ينبغي عنه دائما، هذا خلف. ويمكن أن يلزمها من القضايا غير ما ذهبوا إليه في عكس النقيض باصطلاحهم غير أنها لا تكون من قبيل عكس النقيض أصلا عند الجمهور؛ وقد كان من اللوازم في عكس النقيض أن يجعل نقيض المحمول موضوعا ونقيض الموضوع محمولا مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله كما مر .
As for what a group of earlier scholars adhered to: these propositions, if they are absolute external, are necessitated by contraposition when the subject is restricted by what contradicts the mode of the proposition from among the negatives. As for the divisions of negative subject: an absolute external universal perpetual, as in our saying 'Every C existing externally is B externally.' For if this is true, then our saying is true: 'Nothing that is not B perpetual externally is C perpetual externally'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some of what is not B perpetual externally is C externally with general absoluteness'; and it was that every C externally is B externally. So it results that some of what is not B perpetual externally is B with general absoluteness—this is a contradiction. Then regarding the general absolute, the proof does not differ except in the restriction of the subject; for it is necessary to restrict in every proposition by what contradicts its mode, like necessity in the general possible, and conversely. And if it is an absolute essential, it is necessitated by a universal perpetual essential subject because it is more general than the former and for completion of the proof. For its contradictory with the original yields that some of what is not a necessary concomitant of B perpetually is B externally, and that is impossible; because if it is B externally, it exists externally described by it, so it is a necessary concomitant of B absolutely, yet it is not a necessary concomitant of it perpetually—this is a contradiction. And because if the original is true, then that every C is a necessary concomitant of B is true, and this proposition is necessitated without this exertion. As for from the divisions of negated subject: the external is necessitated by a universal perpetual absolute external because the original with the contradictory yields the truth of our saying 'Some of what is perpetually not B from what has existed externally is B with general absoluteness,' and that is impossible. And for the essential, a universal perpetual essential subject because the original with the contradictory yields the truth of our saying 'Some of what is perpetually a necessary concomitant of the negation of B is B externally,' and it is also impossible because it must exist externally in order for B to be established for it. And it is always implied by it—this is a contradiction. And it is possible that they are necessitated by propositions other than what they adhered to in contraposition according to their terminology, except that they are not of the type of contraposition at all according to the majority; and it was among the requirements of contraposition that the contradictory of the predicate be made subject and the contradictory of the subject be made predicate while preserving the negation and affirmation as it was, as has passed.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage explains the application of conversion by contraposition (aks al-naqid) to various types of propositions. The author refers to the view of some earlier scholars. The key requirement is that for a proposition to have a valid contrapositive, the subject must be restricted by the contradictory of the proposition's mode (jiha). The discussion begins with absolute external (kharijiyya mutlaqa) universal perpetual propositions. An example: 'Every C existing externally is B externally.' Its contrapositive, after restricting the subject, is 'No non-B perpetual external is C perpetual external.' The author proves this by showing that if the contrapositive were false, a contradiction would arise. The same proof applies to other propositions, only the restriction changes: for general possibility, the mode is necessity; the restriction is the contradictory of necessity. For absolute essential (haqiqiyya mutlaqa) propositions, the contrapositive is a universal perpetual essential proposition. The proof shows that the original and its contradictory yield an impossible conclusion. The same reasoning is applied to propositions with negated subjects (ma'dulat al-mawdu'). For external propositions with negated subjects, the contrapositive is a universal perpetual absolute external; for essential, a universal perpetual essential. Finally, the author notes that while some may derive contrapositives differently, the standard definition of contraposition requires swapping the contradictory of the predicate with the subject and the contradictory of the subject with the predicate, preserving the truth value. This passage is typical of advanced logical discussions on immediate inferences, central to Islamic logic manuals.

خارجية مطلقة
قيد الموضوع
جهة القضية

Key Takeaway
For valid contraposition of modal propositions, always restrict the subject by the contradictory of the original mode.

Contraposition for Essential and Negated-Subject Propositions
Author explains contraposition conditions for absolute essential and negated-subject propositions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct contrapositives for absolute essential (haqiqiyya) propositions and for propositions with negated subjects, applying the same subject restriction condition.

Core Idea
Essential propositions require a universal perpetual essential contrapositive; negated-subject propositions follow the same pattern.

The same condition of restricting the subject by the contradictory mode applies to essential (haqiqiyya) propositions and those with negated subjects (ma'dulat al-mawdu'). The author illustrates this with proofs showing that the original and its contradictory lead to an impossibility. Finally, he reiterates the standard definition of contraposition, distinguishing it from other possible inferences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition as essential or with negated subject
Essential propositions (haqiqiyya) predicate a property that is a necessary concomitant (lāzim) of the subject by essence. Negated-subject propositions have a subject that is negated (e.g., 'non-C' or 'what is not B').
The passage distinguishes 'min aqsām sālibat al-mawḍū' (negative subject) and 'ma'dūlat al-mawḍū' (negated subject).
2
Determine the mode and its contradictory
For essential, the mode is perpetual (dā'im) because the essential property holds always. The contradictory of perpetual is 'not perpetual' (i.e., 'sometimes not').
The passage says: 'if it is an absolute essential, it is necessitated by a universal perpetual essential subject'.
3
Form the contrapositive with subject restriction
For an essential proposition like 'Every C is a necessary concomitant of B perpetually', the contrapositive becomes 'Everything that is not a necessary concomitant of B perpetually is not C,' but the author expresses it as a universal perpetual essential subject.
The exact contrapositive: 'some of what is not a necessary concomitant of B perpetually is B externally' is shown impossible.
4
Verify the validity using proof by contradiction
Assume the contrapositive false. Its contradictory together with the original yields a contradiction. For essential: the contradictory of the contrapositive asserts that something is not a necessary concomitant of B yet is B externally, which is impossible.
The passage states: 'because if it is B externally, it exists externally described by it, so it is a necessary concomitant of B absolutely'.
5
Confirm the standard definition of contraposition
The author concludes by stating the majority view: contraposition requires making the contradictory of the predicate the subject, and the contradictory of the subject the predicate, while preserving the original's negation or affirmation.
This defines the operation clearly, distinguishing it from other inferences.

وأما ما ذهب إليه قوم من المتقدمين فهذه القضايا إذا كانت خارجية مطلقة يلزمها من عكس النقيض إذا قيد الموضوع بما يناقض جهة القضية من السوالب . أما من أقسام سالبة الموضوع فخارجية مطلقة كلية دائمة كما في قولنا كل (ج) موجود في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج. فإنه إذا صدق هذا صدق قولنا لا شيء مما هو ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج هو (ج) دائما في الخارج وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو بعض ما ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج (ج) في الخارج بالإطلاق العام؛ وقد كان كل (ج) في الخارج فهو (ب) في الخارج. فينتج بعض ما ليس (ب) دائما في الخارج (ب) بالإطلاق العام، هذا خلف. ثم إنه في المطلقة العامة ولا يختلف البرهان فيما عداها إلا في قيد الموضوع فإنه يجب أن يقيد في كل قضية بما يناقض جهتها نحو الضرورة في الممكنة العامة؛ وبالعكس. وإذا كانت حقيقية مطلقة يلزمها كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لكونها أعم من الأولى ولتمام البرهان. فإن نقيضها مع الأصل ينتج أن بعض ما ليس بملزوم للباء دائما فهو (ب) في الخارج، وذلك محال؛ لأنه إذا كان (ب) في الخارج كان موجودًا في الخارج موصوفًا به فيكون ملزوما للباء بالإطلاق وإنه ليس بملزوم له دائما، هذا خلف. ولأنه إذا صدق الأصل صدق أن كل (ج) ملزوم للباء ويلزمه هذه القضية من غير هذا التكلُّف. وأما من أقسام معدولة الموضوع فالخارجية يلزمها كلية دائمة خارجية مطلقة لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو لا (ب) دائمًا مما وجد في الخارج فهو (ب) بالإطلاق العام، وذلك محال. وللحقيقية كلية دائمة حقيقية الموضوع لما أن الأصل مع النقيض ينتج صدق قولنا بعض ما هو ملزوم لسلب الباء دائما فهو (ب) في الخارج، وإنه محال أيضًا لأنه يجب وجوده في الخارج حتى يثبت له الباء. وإنه ينبغي عنه دائما، هذا خلف. ويمكن أن يلزمها من القضايا غير ما ذهبوا إليه في عكس النقيض باصطلاحهم غير أنها لا تكون من قبيل عكس النقيض أصلا عند الجمهور؛ وقد كان من اللوازم في عكس النقيض أن يجعل نقيض المحمول موضوعا ونقيض الموضوع محمولا مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله كما مر .
As for what a group of earlier scholars adhered to: these propositions, if they are absolute external, are necessitated by contraposition when the subject is restricted by what contradicts the mode of the proposition from among the negatives. As for the divisions of negative subject: an absolute external universal perpetual, as in our saying 'Every C existing externally is B externally.' For if this is true, then our saying is true: 'Nothing that is not B perpetual externally is C perpetual externally'; otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Some of what is not B perpetual externally is C externally with general absoluteness'; and it was that every C externally is B externally. So it results that some of what is not B perpetual externally is B with general absoluteness—this is a contradiction. Then regarding the general absolute, the proof does not differ except in the restriction of the subject; for it is necessary to restrict in every proposition by what contradicts its mode, like necessity in the general possible, and conversely. And if it is an absolute essential, it is necessitated by a universal perpetual essential subject because it is more general than the former and for completion of the proof. For its contradictory with the original yields that some of what is not a necessary concomitant of B perpetually is B externally, and that is impossible; because if it is B externally, it exists externally described by it, so it is a necessary concomitant of B absolutely, yet it is not a necessary concomitant of it perpetually—this is a contradiction. And because if the original is true, then that every C is a necessary concomitant of B is true, and this proposition is necessitated without this exertion. As for from the divisions of negated subject: the external is necessitated by a universal perpetual absolute external because the original with the contradictory yields the truth of our saying 'Some of what is perpetually not B from what has existed externally is B with general absoluteness,' and that is impossible. And for the essential, a universal perpetual essential subject because the original with the contradictory yields the truth of our saying 'Some of what is perpetually a necessary concomitant of the negation of B is B externally,' and it is also impossible because it must exist externally in order for B to be established for it. And it is always implied by it—this is a contradiction. And it is possible that they are necessitated by propositions other than what they adhered to in contraposition according to their terminology, except that they are not of the type of contraposition at all according to the majority; and it was among the requirements of contraposition that the contradictory of the predicate be made subject and the contradictory of the subject be made predicate while preserving the negation and affirmation as it was, as has passed.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage explains the application of conversion by contraposition (aks al-naqid) to various types of propositions. The author refers to the view of some earlier scholars. The key requirement is that for a proposition to have a valid contrapositive, the subject must be restricted by the contradictory of the proposition's mode (jiha). The discussion begins with absolute external (kharijiyya mutlaqa) universal perpetual propositions. An example: 'Every C existing externally is B externally.' Its contrapositive, after restricting the subject, is 'No non-B perpetual external is C perpetual external.' The author proves this by showing that if the contrapositive were false, a contradiction would arise. The same proof applies to other propositions, only the restriction changes: for general possibility, the mode is necessity; the restriction is the contradictory of necessity. For absolute essential (haqiqiyya mutlaqa) propositions, the contrapositive is a universal perpetual essential proposition. The proof shows that the original and its contradictory yield an impossible conclusion. The same reasoning is applied to propositions with negated subjects (ma'dulat al-mawdu'). For external propositions with negated subjects, the contrapositive is a universal perpetual absolute external; for essential, a universal perpetual essential. Finally, the author notes that while some may derive contrapositives differently, the standard definition of contraposition requires swapping the contradictory of the predicate with the subject and the contradictory of the subject with the predicate, preserving the truth value. This passage is typical of advanced logical discussions on immediate inferences, central to Islamic logic manuals.

Contraposition for Particular Propositions
Author critiques views on contraposition for particular propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of contraposition (aks al-naqid) for particular propositions and identify exceptions cited by earlier logicians.

Core Idea
Contraposition of particular propositions fails because it is not uniform, despite some claimed exceptions.

In this passage, the author examines whether particular propositions can be contraposed. While universal propositions convert cleanly, particular ones cause problems. The majority of logicians deny validity, but some earlier scholars, like al-Kashshi, proposed special cases. Understanding why these exceptions fail will sharpen your grasp of immediate inference conditions.

Guided Path
1
Recall the rule of contraposition
Remind yourself: the contradictory of predicate becomes subject, contradictory of subject becomes predicate, quality unchanged. Works for universals.
2
Test on a particular proposition
Take 'Some writer is moving (while a writer)'. Try to contrapose: 'Some non-moving is non-writer (while non-moving)'. Does it follow?
3
Consider the majority's challenge
The author gives a counterexample: 'Some animal is not human' does not yield 'Some human is not animal'. Why does the entailment fail?
4
Examine al-Kashshi's refinement
He restricts to actual (muhassala) propositions and excludes negated (ma'dula) ones. Does his restriction save the rule?
5
Conclude with the author's verdict
The truth is that particular affirmatives do not contraposit as such, otherwise it would hold in all forms. The majority position stands.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويمكن أن يلزمها من القضايا غير ما ذهبوا إليه في عكس النقيض باصطلاحهم غير أنها لا تكون من قبيل عكس النقيض أصلا عند الجمهور؛ وقد كان من اللوازم في عكس النقيض أن يجعل نقيض المحمول موضوعا ونقيض الموضوع محمولا مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله كما مر . وما ذهبوا إليه هذا القوم ليس كذلك بل وإن كان لازما فذلك من لوازم عكس النقيض. اللهم إلا أن يصطلحوا فيسموا عكس النقيض على خلاف ما ذهب إليه الجمهور؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا يعتبر عند غيرهم من الحكماء، هذا إذا كانت الموجودات كلية. فأما إذا كانت جزئية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض أصلا عند الجمهور كما مرَّ من قبل، وعند قوم من المتقدمين إنها لا تنعكس إلا في الخاصتين منها فإنه إذا صدق قولنا بالضرورة بعض الكاتب متحرّك لا دائمًا بل ما دام كاتبًا وجب أن يصدق قولنا بالضرورة بعض ما ليس بمتحرك ليس بكاتب لا دائمًا بل ما دام ليس بمتحرك؛ وذلك لأن المتحرّك لازم للكاتب في صورة ما لا محالة فيكون نقيضه في تلك الصورة مستلزمًا لنقيض الكاتب. غير أنه ضعيف جدا فإنه لا يكون مطردًا يصدق في كل صورة ; وقد صدق قولنا بالضرورة بعض الحيوان لا إنسان لا دائما بل ما دام حيوانًا، ولا يصدق بالضرورة بعض الإنسان لا حيوان لا دائما بل ما دام إنسانًا. وعلى هذا في العرفية العامة. وأما قول الشيخ «إنه إذا صدق قولنا بعض «ج» «ب» وجب أن يصدق قولنا بعض ما ليس (ب) ليس «ج»، فذلك من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه. وعن الإمام زين الدين الكسي أن الموجبة الجزئية فيما عدا الدائمتين والمشروطتين والعرفيتين تنعكس موجبة جزئية لما مر من قبل أنه لا بد وأن توجد موجودات أو معدومات خارجة عن الموضوع والمحمول، غير أنه في حيز المنع لما مر . وعنه أنه قال أيضًا إنها إذا كانت محصلة تنعكس، وإذا كانت معدولة لا تنعكس لانتقاضه بحمل الحيوان على اللا إنسان. وبالجملة فالحق إنها لا تنعكس من حيث هي الموجبة الجزئية كما ذهب إليه الجمهور وإلا لكانت منعكسة في جميع الصور; وليس كذلك} .
And it can be entailed from propositions other than what they held concerning contraposition in their terminology, except that they are not of the type of contraposition at all according to the majority; and it was among the requirements of contraposition that the contradictory of the predicate be made subject and the contradictory of the subject be made predicate while preserving the negation and affirmation as they are, as was mentioned. And what these people held is not like that; rather, even if it is entailed, that is among the entailments of contraposition. Unless they stipulate and name contraposition contrary to what the majority held; and this is among what is not considered by other philosophers, when the terms are universal. As for particular terms, they do not contraposit at all according to the majority, as was mentioned before, and according to some earlier scholars, they do not contraposit except in the special two [forms]. For if our statement is necessarily true: 'Some writer is moving, not always but while he is a writer', then our statement must be necessarily true: 'Some non-moving is not a writer, not always but while he is not moving'; that is because moving is necessary for writer in a certain form undoubtedly, so its contradictory in that form necessitates the contradictory of writer. But it is very weak because it is not uniform, true in every form; and our statement is necessarily true: 'Some animal is not human, not always but while it is an animal', but it is not necessarily true: 'Some human is not animal, not always but while it is human'. And similarly in the general conventional. And as for the saying of al-Shaykh: 'If our statement "Some C is B" is true, then our statement "Some non-B is non-C" must be true', that is among what we have already discussed. And from Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashshi: the particular affirmative except the two permanent, the two conditional, and the two conventional, converts as a particular affirmative, for what was mentioned before, that there must necessarily exist existents or non-existents outside the subject and predicate, except that it is in the realm of prevention as was mentioned. And from him he also said: if they are actual (muhassala) they convert, and if they are negated (ma'dula) they do not convert because it is contradicted by predicating 'animal' of 'non-human'. In summary, the truth is that they do not convert as particular affirmatives as the majority held, otherwise they would convert in all forms; and it is not so.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the validity of contraposition (aks al-naqid) for particular propositions. The author first distinguishes the standard definition of contraposition according to the majority (jumhoor): swapping the contradictories of subject and predicate while preserving the quality. He notes that some group (perhaps the logicians he critiques) proposed a different conversion that is not true contraposition. Then he addresses the case of universal terms: contraposition works for them. For particular propositions, the majority holds that contraposition is invalid. However, some earlier scholars claimed it works in two special cases, illustrated by the example of 'Some writer is moving' with the qualification of duration. The argument is that if 'writer' necessarily implies 'moving' in some form, then the contradictory of 'moving' implies the contradictory of 'writer'. The author objects that this is not uniform, giving a counterexample: 'Some animal is not human' does not entail 'Some human is not animal' even with temporal qualification. He then mentions al-Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina) who claimed that 'Some C is B' entails 'Some non-B is non-C', but says this has been discussed earlier. Finally, he reports the view of Imam Zayn al-Din al-Kashshi, who held that the particular affirmative converts except for certain types (permanent, conditional, conventional), provided the terms are 'actual' (muhassala) not 'negated' (ma'dula). Al-Kashshi gives a counterexample to negated terms: 'animal' predicated of 'non-human' fails. The author concludes that the correct position is that particular affirmatives do not convert as such, aligning with the majority.

Contraposition is valid only for universal propositions; particular affirmatives do not generally contraposit, contrary to some minority views.

Contrapositive Conversion of Universal Negatives Across Modalities
Author proves contrapositive conversion for universal negatives across modalities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contrapositive of any universal negative categorical proposition and verify its validity across actual, possible, and conditional modalities.

Core Idea
Universal negatives always convert by contrapositive; the converse of 'No A is B' is 'Not every non-B is non-A'.

In this passage, the author establishes a foundational rule of immediate inference: universal negative propositions convert by contrapositive in every modality. This rule is essential for building valid arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence. We will walk through the proof and its application to actual, perpetual, and possible propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Take a universal negative: 'No A is B' (e.g., 'No intoxicant is halal'). Note its subject (A) and predicate (B).
The proposition must be universal in quantity and negative in quality.
2
Formulate the contrapositive
Swap subject and predicate while negating both: 'Not every non-B is non-A' (i.e., 'Some non-B is non-A' is false). In propositional form: ~(∀¬B ¬A) ≡ ∃¬B A.
Contrapositive is not the converse; it is a distinct immediate inference.
3
Verify by contradiction
Assume the contrapositive is false. Then its contradictory 'Every non-B is A' is true. But if every non-B is A, then every A is B (by contrapositive of that universal affirmative), contradicting the original 'No A is B'.
This proof uses the relationship between universal negative and universal affirmative via obversion.
4
Apply to different modalities
For actual negatives, the contrapositive is absolute general. For conditional (e.g., 'as long as it is A'), the contrapositive also holds with the same temporal or modal qualification. For possible negatives, the contrapositive is possible general.
The author shows the same logical structure works for perpetual, conditional general, conditional special, and possible propositions.
5
Relate to simple conversion of affirmatives
Affirmatives convert simply: 'Every A is B' gives 'Some B is A'. Negatives convert by contrapositive because truth in negatives combines a thing with the contradictory of another, while affirmatives combine two things positively.
Understanding this symmetry helps memorize and apply both rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما السوالب فإنها تنعكس عكس النقيض بأي جهة كانت وفي أي مادة كلية كانت أو جزئية فعلية كانت أو ممكنة على حسب الموجبات في العكس المستوي؛ إذ الفعلية منها كلية كانت أو جزئية تنعكس جزئية مطلقة عامة كما أي كل «أب» دائما: وقد كان بالضرورة لا شيء من «أب»، هذا خلف. ولأنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من أب ) صدق قولنا كل «أ» هو ليس «ب»؛ وإنه ينعكس عكسا جزئيا وهو قولنا بعض ليس «ب» هو «أ»؛ وهذا من جملة ما يلزمه فيقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «أب» ما دام «أ»، ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مرَّ . وأما المشروطة الخاصة فيقال بالضرورة لا شيء من «أب» لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»، ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر وعلى هذا في العرفية الخاصة. وكذلك في الوقتية والمنتشرة وغيرهما من القضايا الفعلية، فإن ما ينعكس في الموجبات بالعكس المستوي فذلك ينعكس في السالبات بعكس النقيض لما أن الصدق في ذلك العكس مما يستلزم الصدق في هذا؛ وبالعكس لما كان الصدق في الموجبات يجمع بين الشيء والشيء؛ والصدق في السالبات يجمع بين الشيء ونقيض ذلك الشيء. ألا ترى أنك إذا قلت فعلية كانت أو ممكنة.
As for negatives, they convert by contrapositive in whatever mode (modality) and in whatever matter, whether universal or particular, actual or possible, corresponding to affirmatives in simple conversion. For the actual ones, whether universal or particular, convert as a particular absolute general, as it is said 'Necessarily, no A is B', so 'Not every non-B is non-A absolutely general' otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Every non-B is A always'. So it yields a contrapositive, namely 'Every non-A is non-B always' i.e. 'Every A is B always'; but it was necessarily 'No A is B', this is a contradiction. And because if our statement 'No A is B' is true, our statement 'Every A is non-B' is true; and it converts by partial conversion, namely 'Some non-B is A'; and this is among those which entail the truth of the conversion because then it is impossible for the contradictory to be true, namely 'Not every non-B is non-A absolutely general' as mentioned. It can also be proven by other proofs, for example it is said: if our statement 'No A is B' is true, then its converse is true, namely 'Not every non-B is non-A'; otherwise the original would be true along with its contradictory; and that is impossible because the contradictory entails something whose opposite necessitates the original, namely 'Every A is B'. Likewise, it is said: if 'No A is B' is true, then among the other things, there is some that are not A and not B, so what is not A is not B in that case, and what is not B is not A as well, and this is among what is impossible to be true without the truth of the converse. And this applies to the perpetual. As for the conditional general, it is said: 'Necessarily, no A is B as long as it is A', so 'Not every non-B is non-A absolutely general' otherwise its contradictory would be true; that leads to impossibility as mentioned. As for the conditional special, it is said: 'Necessarily, no A is B not always but as long as it is A', so 'Not every non-B is non-A absolutely general' for what was mentioned; and this applies to the special conventional. And likewise for temporal, distributive, and other actual propositions. For what converts in affirmatives by simple conversion, that converts in negatives by contrapositive, because truth in that conversion entails truth in this one; and conversely, because truth in affirmatives combines a thing with a thing, while truth in negatives combines a thing with the contradictory of that thing. Do you not see that if you say 'Every C is B', you have combined this with that; and if you say 'No C is B', you have combined this with the contradictory of that? This if the negatives are actual. As for if they are possible, whether special or general, they convert by contrapositive as a particular possible general, as it is said 'No A is B with special possibility', so 'Not some non-B is non-A with general possibility' otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Every non-B is A necessarily'; and that leads to impossibility as before. This if the negatives are universal, whether actual or possible.
Source pages p. 500

Commentary
This passage describes the conversion by contrapositive (عكس النقيض) for universal negative categorical propositions. The author begins by stating the general rule: negative propositions convert by contrapositive in any modality and any matter (universal/particular, actual/possible), just as affirmatives convert by simple conversion. He then proves this for actual negatives: if it is true that 'No A is B', then the contrapositive 'Not every non-B is non-A' must be true. If not, its contradictory 'Every non-B is A always' would be true, implying 'Every A is B always', contradicting the original. He provides two proofs: the first via converting 'No A is B' to 'Every A is non-B' (by obversion), then partially converting to 'Some non-B is A' (which is part of the contrapositive). The second proof shows that if 'No A is B' is true, there exist things that are neither A nor B, so the contrapositive holds. The author then extends this to perpetual (دائمة) and conditional (مشروطة) propositions, adjusting the modality. For example, in a conditional general proposition 'No A is B as long as it is A', the contrapositive must be in the absolute general sense. He explains that the conversion of affirmatives (simple conversion) and negatives (contrapositive) are related because truth in affirmatives combines two terms, while truth in negatives separates them by contradiction. Finally, he addresses possible negatives: if 'No A is B' is possible (special possibility), the contrapositive is 'Not some non-B is non-A' with general possibility, otherwise a necessary universal affirmative would follow, causing impossibility. The lesson concludes by summarizing: these rules apply to all universal negatives, actual or possible.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
السوالب
الإطلاق العام
الإمكان الخاص

Contrapositive Conversion of Particular Negatives
Author explains contrapositive conversion for particular negative propositions, actual and possible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform contrapositive conversion on particular negative propositions, both actual and possible, and verify the validity of the conversion.

Core Idea
Particular negatives convert by contrapositive to particular negatives, with a shift in modality.

This passage from the logic text explains how particular negative propositions (e.g., 'Some A is not B') undergo contrapositive conversion. Unlike universal negatives, the result is not a universal but remains particular, with adjustments in modality depending on whether the original is actual (necessary or non-necessary) or possible. Understanding this rule is crucial for handling immediate inferences in Islamic logic, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of particular negative
Determine whether the proposition is actual (فعلية) or possible (ممكنة). Actual ones may be necessary or not.
The passage covers both types.
2
For actual particular negatives: apply contrapositive
The contrapositive of 'Some A is not B' (actual) is 'Not some non-B is non-A' with absolute generality. Verify using reductio: if false, contradictory leads to impossibility.
The modality shifts to absolute general.
3
For conditional general propositions
For 'Not every C is B as long as it is C', the contrapositive is 'Not every non-B is non-C absolutely general'. The reasoning is similar.
This applies to conditional propositions with temporal conditions.
4
For possible particular negatives
The contrapositive of 'Some A is not B with special possibility' is 'Not some non-B is non-A with general possibility'. Modality shifts from special to general possibility.
The result remains a particular negative.
5
Verify validity
Check that the truth value is preserved: if the original is true, the contrapositive must be true. The passage uses reductio to argue for the conversion.
This ensures the conversion is sound.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فأما إذا كانت جزئية فإنها إما أن تكون فعلية وهي تنعكس جزئية مطلقة عامة ضرورية كانت أو لا ضرورية فيقال بالضرورة بعض «أ» ليس «ب»، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» دائمًا؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف، وعلى هذا في الدائمة. وأما في المشروطة العامة فيقال ليس كل ج ب ما دام «ج»، ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مرَّ، وعلى هذا في المشروطة الخاصة وغيرها. وأما أن تكون ممكنة وإنها تنعكس جزئية ممكنة عامة كما يقال بعض ( أ ) ليس «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» بالإمكان العام.
As for when they are particular, they are either actual, and they convert as a particular absolute general, whether necessary or not necessary; it is said: 'Necessarily, some A is not B, so not some non-B is non-A absolutely general' otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Every non-B is A always'; and that leads to impossibility as known; and this applies to the perpetual. As for the conditional general, it is said: 'Not every C is B as long as it is C, so not every non-B is non-C absolutely general' otherwise its contradictory would be true; and that leads to impossibility as before; and this applies to the conditional special and others. As for when it is possible, it converts as a particular possible general, as it is said: 'Some A is not B with special possibility, so not some non-B is non-A with general possibility.'
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage focuses on the contrapositive conversion of particular negative propositions. The author divides them into actual (فعلية) and possible (ممكنة). For actual particular negatives, such as 'Some A is not B' (with necessity or without), the contrapositive is 'Not some non-B is non-A' in the absolute general sense (i.e., a particular negative with absolute modality). If the original is true, the contrapositive must be true; otherwise its contradictory—'Every non-B is A always'—would be true, which would imply the falsehood of the original. This applies to perpetual (دائمة) propositions as well. For conditional propositions, such as 'Not every C is B as long as it is C' (which is essentially a particular negative conditioned by 'as long as it is C'), the contrapositive is 'Not every non-B is non-C absolutely general'. The reasoning is the same: if the contrapositive were false, its contradictory would force an impossible conclusion. For possible particular negatives, e.g., 'Some A is not B with special possibility', the contrapositive is 'Not some non-B is non-A with general possibility'—that is, a particular negative possible proposition. The author emphasizes that these conversions follow the same pattern as universal negatives but yield particular results, consistent with the rules of immediate inference.

Key Terms
الجزئية الفعلية
المشروطة العامة
الإمكان العام

Key Takeaway
Particular negative propositions convert by contrapositive to particular negatives, with modality shifting from actual (with or without necessity) to absolute general, and from special possibility to general possibility.

Core Rule of 'Aks al-Naqid for Actual Negatives
Author explains the rationale and application of 'aks al-naqid for actual negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert any universal actual negative proposition using 'aks al-naqid and explain why simple conversion fails.

Core Idea
Negatives convert by contradictory, not by simple flip.

In the previous lesson, you learned that affirmative propositions convert by simple conversion. But negatives behave differently. This lesson introduces 'aks al-naqid, the conversion of the contradictory, which preserves truth for negatives. We will see the logical reasoning and then apply it to a kalam example.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the conjunction difference
Notice that an affirmative proposition 'Every J is B' conjoins subject and predicate directly. A negative 'No J is B' conjoins subject with the contradictory of the predicate. This difference necessitates a different conversion rule.
The author emphasizes this by saying truth in affirmatives brings together a thing and a thing, but truth in negatives brings together a thing and the contradictory of that thing.
2
Apply 'aks al-naqid to a universal actual negative
Take a universal actual negative like 'No A is B'. To convert, swap subject and predicate, and replace the original subject with its contradictory in the new predicate. The result: 'Not every B is A' (equivalent to 'Some B is not A').
Do not use simple conversion, as that would change truth value.
3
Verify using the author's reasoning
Assume the conversion were otherwise (e.g., 'No B is A'). Show that this would imply the contradictory of the original, leading to impossibility. The author uses this reductio to justify the rule.
This proof by contradiction is standard in Islamic logic.
4
Practice with an Islamic example
Consider the kalam proposition: 'No created thing is eternal' (universal actual negative). Convert it using 'aks al-naqid: 'Not every eternal thing is not created' (i.e., some eternal thing is not created). This is valid because God is eternal and not created.
This example shows the rule's applicability to theological discourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا دائما بل ما دام «أ»، ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس «أ» بالإطلاق العام لما مر وعلى هذا في العرفية الخاصة. وكذلك في الوقتية والمنتشرة وغيرهما من القضايا الفعلية، فإن ما ينعكس في الموجبات بالعكس المستوي فذلك العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» دائمًا؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف، وعلى هذا في الدائمة. وأما في المشروطة العامة فيقال ليس كل ج ب ما دام «ج»، ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مرَّ، وعلى هذا في المشروطة الخاصة وغيرها. وأما أن تكون ممكنة وإنها تنعكس جزئية ممكنة عامة كما يقال بعض ( أ ) ليس «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس ( أ ) بالإمكان العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال كذلك. واعلم / بأنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الموجود بممكن، أو بعض الموجود ليس بممكن فذلك لا يكون من الممكنات بل من القضايا الفعلية فتنعكس عكس النقيض هكذا: ليس كل ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام، أو ليس بعض ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام والمعنى بالممكن هو الممكن بالإمكان الخاص في هذا المثال؛ إذ الموجود أي موجود كان ليس إلا وهو ممكن بالإمكان العام. فأما بالإمكان الخاص فلا مجال لذلك وإلا لكان الموجود حال كونه موجودا جائز العدم وذلك محال لاستحالة الاجتماع بين النقيضين. ولا يقال لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان من المحال أن يكون الشيء ممكنا والشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون موجودًا، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل عدمه حينئذ، أو معدوما، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل وجوده كذلك. فإن الشيء لا يكون ممكنا إلا وأن يكون إمكانه له لذاته. ولو كان كذلك لكان ممكنا دائمًا سواء كان موجودًا أو معدوما لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته لا بالنظر إلى وجوده وعدمه. وهذا من جملة ما مرَّ ذكره من قبل. وما هو المشهور عند الجمهور في عكس السوالب. فهو الذي مضى ذكره في هذا الفصل؛ Not always but as long as 'A', not everything that is not B is not A with absolute generality, according to what has passed, and similarly in the particular conditional. Likewise in the temporal and distributed and other actual propositions. For what converts in affirmatives by simple conversion converts in negatives by conversion of the contradictory, because truth in that conversion necessitates truth in this; and conversely, because truth in affirmatives brings together a thing and a thing, and truth in negatives brings together a thing and the contradictory of that thing. Do you not see that when you say 'Every J is B' you have brought together this and that, and when you say 'Nothing from J is B' you have brought together this and the contradictory of that? This is when the negatives are actual. As for when they are possible, specific or general, they convert by conversion of the contradictory to a particular possible general, as it is said: 'Nothing from A is B with specific possibility' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Everything that is not B is not A necessarily', and that leads to impossibility as previously mentioned. This applies when the negatives are universal, actual or possible. As for when they are particular, they are either actual and convert to a particular absolute general, necessary or not necessary, so it is said: 'Necessarily some A is not B' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with absolute generality', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Everything that is not B is not A always', and that leads to impossibility as known. Similarly for the perpetual. As for the general conditional, it is said: 'Not every J is B as long as J' implies 'Not everything that is not B is not J with absolute generality', otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility as previously. Similarly for the specific conditional and others. As for when it is possible, it converts to a particular possible general, as it is said: 'Some A is not B with specific possibility' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility', otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility similarly. And know that if you say 'Nothing of the existent is possible' or 'Some of the existent is not possible', that is not among possibles but rather among actual propositions, so it converts by conversion of the contradictory thus: 'Not everything that is not possible is not existent with absolute generality' or 'Not some of what is not possible is not existent with absolute generality'. What is meant by 'possible' is possible with specific possibility in this example; for the existent, whatever existent it may be, is nothing but what is possible with general possibility. As for specific possibility, there is no scope for that, otherwise the existent while being existent would be contingent of non-existence, and that is impossible due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories. And it is not said that if it were as mentioned, then it would be impossible for a thing to be possible and the thing not be free from being existent – and it is among what is impossible of non-existence then – or non-existent – and it is among what is impossible of existence likewise. For a thing is only possible when its possibility belongs to it in itself. And if it were so, it would be possible always, whether existent or non-existent, but it is with respect to its essence, not with respect to its existence or non-existence. This is among what has been previously mentioned. And what is well-known among the majority regarding the conversion of negatives is that which has been mentioned in this section. So take heed of what you know, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules for converting negative propositions (al-sawalib) by 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory). The author begins by building on earlier discussion, stating that for universal negatives that are actual (fi'liyya) or conditional ('urfiyya) and others, they do not convert by simple conversion ('aks mustawi) like affirmatives do. Instead, they convert by 'aks al-naqid. The reason given is logical: an affirmative asserts a conjunction between subject and predicate, while a negative asserts a conjunction between subject and the contradictory of the predicate. Consequently, the conversion must reflect this. For example, 'Every J is B' (affirmative) can be simply converted to 'Some B is J', but 'No J is B' (negative) converts to 'Not every B is J' (which is equivalent to 'Some B is not J' but the author uses the form with 'not every'). The author then distinguishes between actual and possible propositions. For possible negatives (mumkina), whether general or specific, they convert via 'aks al-naqid to a particular possible general. An example is given: 'Nothing from A is B with specific possibility' converts to 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility'. This is justified by the absurdity of the contradictory, which would entail a necessary universal affirmative that leads to impossibility. Next, the author treats particular negatives. If actual, they convert to a particular absolute general (necessary or not). If possible, they convert to a particular possible general. The author also discusses perpetual (da'ima) and conditional (mashruta) propositions, applying similar reasoning. A special note is made about the proposition 'Nothing of the existent is possible' or 'Some existent is not possible'. These are actually actual propositions (since existence and possibility in the general sense are actual), so they convert by 'aks al-naqid accordingly. The author clarifies that 'possible' here means specific possibility; everything existent is possible with general possibility. Otherwise, the existent would be contingent of non-existence, which is impossible. The passage concludes by referencing the well-known view of the majority on this subject and urging the reader to reflect. All examples are from logical formalizations using variables, but the underlying principles apply to Islamic sciences, e.g., in kalam arguments about possibility and existence.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض
القضايا الفعلية
السوالب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Modal Conversion: Possible and Particular Negatives
Author details conversion rules for possible and particular negatives with modality distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between actual and possible negative propositions and apply the appropriate conversion rule for universal and particular cases.

Core Idea
Possibility and quantity modify the conversion rule for negatives.

The author extends the doctrine of 'aks al-naqid to cover possible (mumkina) negatives, both universal and particular. You will learn how modality affects the conversion result, and see a special case where a proposition about existence is actually actual, not possible. This lesson builds on the core rule and applies it to more nuanced propositions found in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modality of the negative
Determine if the negative is actual (fi'liyya) or possible (mumkina). Actual negatives are grounded in reality; possible negatives are about what is merely possible, with specific or general possibility.
The author distinguishes between actual and possible early in the passage.
2
Convert universal possible negatives
For a universal possible negative like 'No A is B with specific possibility', the conversion yields a particular possible general negative: 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility'. This is proved by contradiction.
Note the shift from specific to general possibility in the conversion.
3
Handle particular negatives
For particular actual negatives, convert to a particular absolute general (with or without necessity). For particular possible negatives, convert to a particular possible general. Always using 'aks al-naqid.
The passage gives examples: 'Necessarily some A is not B' converts to 'Not some of what is not B is not A with absolute generality'.
4
Recognize misleading modal propositions
Propositions about existence and possibility, like 'Nothing of the existent is possible', are actually actual, not possible, because existence entails general possibility. So they convert like other actuals.
The author clarifies that 'possible' in this context refers to specific possibility, not general possibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

عامة، كما يقال لا شيء من «أب» بالإمكان الخاص، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس ( أ ) بالإمكان العام وإلا لصدق تقيضه وهو قولنا كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» بالضرورة؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال كما مر من قبل، هذا إذا كانت السوالب كلية فعلية كانت أو ممكنة. فأما إذا كانت جزئية فإنها إما أن تكون فعلية وهي تنعكس جزئية مطلقة عامة ضرورية كانت أو لا ضرورية فيقال بالضرورة بعض «أ» ليس «ب»، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «أ» دائمًا؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال على ما عرف، وعلى هذا في الدائمة. وأما في المشروطة العامة فيقال ليس كل ج ب ما دام «ج»، ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج» بالإطلاق العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وذلك يفضي إلى المحال كما مرَّ، وعلى هذا في المشروطة الخاصة وغيرها. وأما أن تكون ممكنة وإنها تنعكس جزئية ممكنة عامة كما يقال بعض ( أ ) ليس «ب» بالإمكان الخاص، ليس بعض ما ليس «ب» ليس ( أ ) بالإمكان العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال كذلك. واعلم / بأنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الموجود بممكن، أو بعض الموجود ليس بممكن فذلك لا يكون من الممكنات بل من القضايا الفعلية فتنعكس عكس النقيض هكذا: ليس كل ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام، أو ليس بعض ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام والمعنى بالممكن هو الممكن بالإمكان الخاص في هذا المثال؛ إذ الموجود أي موجود كان ليس إلا وهو ممكن بالإمكان العام. فأما بالإمكان الخاص فلا مجال لذلك وإلا لكان الموجود حال كونه موجودا جائز العدم وذلك محال لاستحالة الاجتماع بين النقيضين. ولا يقال لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان من المحال أن يكون الشيء ممكنا والشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون موجودًا، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل عدمه حينئذ، أو معدوما، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل وجوده كذلك. فإن الشيء لا يكون ممكنا إلا وأن يكون إمكانه له لذاته. ولو كان كذلك لكان ممكنا دائمًا سواء كان موجودًا أو معدوما لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته لا بالنظر إلى وجوده وعدمه. وهذا من جملة ما مرَّ ذكره من قبل. وما هو المشهور عند الجمهور في عكس السوالب. فهو الذي مضى ذكره في هذا الفصل؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت، وبالله التوفيق.
Not always but as long as 'A', not everything that is not B is not A with absolute generality, according to what has passed, and similarly in the particular conditional. Likewise in the temporal and distributed and other actual propositions. For what converts in affirmatives by simple conversion converts in negatives by conversion of the contradictory, because truth in that conversion necessitates truth in this; and conversely, because truth in affirmatives brings together a thing and a thing, and truth in negatives brings together a thing and the contradictory of that thing. Do you not see that when you say 'Every J is B' you have brought together this and that, and when you say 'Nothing from J is B' you have brought together this and the contradictory of that? This is when the negatives are actual. As for when they are possible, specific or general, they convert by conversion of the contradictory to a particular possible general, as it is said: 'Nothing from A is B with specific possibility' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Everything that is not B is not A necessarily', and that leads to impossibility as previously mentioned. This applies when the negatives are universal, actual or possible. As for when they are particular, they are either actual and convert to a particular absolute general, necessary or not necessary, so it is said: 'Necessarily some A is not B' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with absolute generality', otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'Everything that is not B is not A always', and that leads to impossibility as known. Similarly for the perpetual. As for the general conditional, it is said: 'Not every J is B as long as J' implies 'Not everything that is not B is not J with absolute generality', otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility as previously. Similarly for the specific conditional and others. As for when it is possible, it converts to a particular possible general, as it is said: 'Some A is not B with specific possibility' implies 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility', otherwise its contradictory would be true, and that leads to impossibility similarly. And know that if you say 'Nothing of the existent is possible' or 'Some of the existent is not possible', that is not among possibles but rather among actual propositions, so it converts by conversion of the contradictory thus: 'Not everything that is not possible is not existent with absolute generality' or 'Not some of what is not possible is not existent with absolute generality'. What is meant by 'possible' is possible with specific possibility in this example; for the existent, whatever existent it may be, is nothing but what is possible with general possibility. As for specific possibility, there is no scope for that, otherwise the existent while being existent would be contingent of non-existence, and that is impossible due to the impossibility of the conjunction of two contradictories. And it is not said that if it were as mentioned, then it would be impossible for a thing to be possible and the thing not be free from being existent – and it is among what is impossible of non-existence then – or non-existent – and it is among what is impossible of existence likewise. For a thing is only possible when its possibility belongs to it in itself. And if it were so, it would be possible always, whether existent or non-existent, but it is with respect to its essence, not with respect to its existence or non-existence. This is among what has been previously mentioned. And what is well-known among the majority regarding the conversion of negatives is that which has been mentioned in this section. So take heed of what you know, and with God is success.
Source pages p. 501

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rules for converting negative propositions (al-sawalib) by 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory). The author begins by building on earlier discussion, stating that for universal negatives that are actual (fi'liyya) or conditional ('urfiyya) and others, they do not convert by simple conversion ('aks mustawi) like affirmatives do. Instead, they convert by 'aks al-naqid. The reason given is logical: an affirmative asserts a conjunction between subject and predicate, while a negative asserts a conjunction between subject and the contradictory of the predicate. Consequently, the conversion must reflect this. For example, 'Every J is B' (affirmative) can be simply converted to 'Some B is J', but 'No J is B' (negative) converts to 'Not every B is J' (which is equivalent to 'Some B is not J' but the author uses the form with 'not every'). The author then distinguishes between actual and possible propositions. For possible negatives (mumkina), whether general or specific, they convert via 'aks al-naqid to a particular possible general. An example is given: 'Nothing from A is B with specific possibility' converts to 'Not some of what is not B is not A with general possibility'. This is justified by the absurdity of the contradictory, which would entail a necessary universal affirmative that leads to impossibility. Next, the author treats particular negatives. If actual, they convert to a particular absolute general (necessary or not). If possible, they convert to a particular possible general. The author also discusses perpetual (da'ima) and conditional (mashruta) propositions, applying similar reasoning. A special note is made about the proposition 'Nothing of the existent is possible' or 'Some existent is not possible'. These are actually actual propositions (since existence and possibility in the general sense are actual), so they convert by 'aks al-naqid accordingly. The author clarifies that 'possible' here means specific possibility; everything existent is possible with general possibility. Otherwise, the existent would be contingent of non-existence, which is impossible. The passage concludes by referencing the well-known view of the majority on this subject and urging the reader to reflect. All examples are from logical formalizations using variables, but the underlying principles apply to Islamic sciences, e.g., in kalam arguments about possibility and existence.

Key Terms
الإمكان الخاص
الإمكان العام
القضايا الممكنة
القضايا الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Modal conversion requires careful attention to whether the proposition is actual or possible, and the quantity affects the result.

Different Views on Conversion of Negative Propositions
Author mentions differing opinions on whether a negative converts to a particular affirmative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the differing views on the conversion of negative propositions by 'aks al-naqid and compare the standard view with Avicenna's position.

Core Idea
Negative propositions convert differently according to different logicians.

This brief passage records a divergence of opinion among logicians regarding the conversion of negative categorical propositions. Some held that a negative converts to a particular affirmative, while Avicenna and his followers claimed it converts to both a particular affirmative and a particular negative. Understanding these differences is important for evaluating arguments that rely on immediate inference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question
The passage discusses how a negative proposition (e.g., 'No A is B') should be converted using 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory).
2
Extract the first view
Some people (qawm) held that a negative converts to a particular affirmative. For example, 'No A is B' would become 'Some B is A'? Actually, that seems non-standard; the author notes this contradicts the earlier scholars.
This view is unusual and is rejected by the salaf.
3
Extract the standard view (al-salaf)
The predecessors (al-salaf) held that a universal negative converts to a universal negative via 'aks al-naqid, i.e., 'No A is B' becomes 'No B is A'? Actually, 'aks al-naqid of 'No A is B' typically yields 'Some B is not A'. The author says the standard view has already been mentioned.
This is the conventional position in Aristotelian logic.
4
Extract Avicenna's view
Avicenna and his followers held that a negative converts to both a particular affirmative and a particular negative. This means from 'No A is B' we can infer both 'Some B is not A' (particular negative) and 'Some not-B is A'? The passage is concise.
The exact mechanics require further study, but the key is the allowance of a particular affirmative result.
5
Compare and contrast
Note the disagreement: the salaf restrict the conversion to a negative, while Avicenna expands it to include an affirmative. This affects how one can reason with negative propositions in Islamic sciences.

Source Text

فصل [ فيما يتعلق بالسوالب في الحمليات ] وأما [ما] ذهب إليه قوم وهو أن السالبة تنعكس عكس النقيض إلى موجبة جزئية، فذلك على خلاف ما ذهب إليه السلف وهو الذي مر ذكره. وعن الشيخ وأتباعه أنها تنعكس إلى موجبة جزئية، وإلى سالبة جزئية أيضًا.
Section [ concerning negatives in categorical propositions ] And as for [what] some people held, that the negative proposition converts by conversion of the contradictory to a particular affirmative, that is contrary to what the predecessors held, which is what has been mentioned. And it is reported from the Shaykh (Avicenna) and his followers that it converts to a particular affirmative and also to a particular negative.
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This is a brief section (fadl) that mentions a divergence of opinion regarding the conversion of negative propositions (al-sawalib fi al-hamliyyat). Some logicians (qawm) held that a negative proposition, when converted by 'aks al-naqid (conversion of the contradictory), yields a particular affirmative proposition. For example, 'No A is B' would become 'Some B is not A'? Actually, 'aks al-naqid of a universal negative typically gives a universal negative: 'No A is B' converts to 'Not every B is A' which is equivalent to 'Some B is not A', a particular negative, not affirmative. So these people's claim is unusual. The author states that this view is contrary to the position of the earlier scholars (al-salaf), which has already been discussed in the previous passage (i.e., the standard view that a universal negative converts to a universal negative via 'aks al-naqid). Then the author reports that Shaykh al-Ra'is (Avicenna) and his followers held that the negative proposition converts both to a particular affirmative and to a particular negative. This likely means that from 'No A is B' we can infer both 'Some B is not A' (particular negative) and 'Some not-B is A'? Actually, careful: In Avicenna's logic, the conversion of 'No A is B' yields 'Some B is not A' (particular negative) by simple conversion? No, that's not standard. The author may be summarizing a nuanced position. The passage is concise and leaves details implicit. This lesson simply records the differing views.

Key Terms
السلف (al-salaf): Earlier scholars/logicians who preceded the current discussion, presumably those whose view has been presented earlier in the text. [Note: In this context, it refers to predecessors in logic, not necessarily early Muslim generations.]
الشيخ (al-Shaykh): Avicenna (Ibn Sina), referred to by his honorific title 'al-Shaykh al-Ra'is'. [Note: Stable reference to Avicenna in post-classical Arabic logic.]
