Conversion of Modal Negative Propositions by Contradiction
Author explains conversion of modal negatives by contradiction with distinction between general and specific possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply conversion by contradiction (عكس النقيض) to modal negative propositions and distinguish between general and specific possibility.

Core Idea
Conversion by contradiction reveals the distinction between general possibility and specific possibility in modal logic.

This passage tackles a subtle aspect of immediate inference: converting negative modal propositions by contradiction. Modal logic becomes tricky when actuality mixes with possibility. The author distinguishes between general possibility (every existent is not impossible) and specific possibility (equally capable of existence and non-existence). This distinction is crucial for correctly converting propositions like 'No existent is possible'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of proposition
Determine whether the proposition is modal or actual. The author gives examples: 'No existent is possible' is an actual proposition, not modal, because it describes a state of affairs without explicit modality.
Actual propositions are قضايا فعلية.
2
Recognize the conversion rule
Conversion by contradiction (عكس النقيض) involves swapping subject and predicate and negating both. For a universal negative 'No S is P', the result is 'Not every non-P is non-S' (i.e., 'Some non-P is S').
This is equivalent to contraposition in Western logic.
3
Apply the rule to the proposition
Take 'No existent is possible' (لا شيء من الموجود بممكن). Convert: 'Not every non-possible is non-existent' = 'Some non-possible is existent'.
The conversion is valid under general possibility.
4
Consider the modal qualification
The conversion result must be interpreted under general possibility, not specific possibility. General possibility applies to all existents; specific possibility would imply an existent could be non-existent simultaneously, which is absurd.
الإمكان العام vs الإمكان الخاص.
5
Distinguish general vs. specific possibility
General possibility means 'not impossible' – every existent has it. Specific possibility means 'equally capable of existence and non-existence' – an existent cannot have it while existing, as that would entail a contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ليس «ب» ليس ( أ ) بالإمكان العام وإلا لصدق نقيضه؛ وإنه يفضي إلى المحال كذلك. واعلم / بأنك إذا قلت لا شيء من الموجود بممكن، أو بعض الموجود ليس بممكن فذلك لا يكون من الممكنات بل من القضايا الفعلية فتنعكس عكس النقيض هكذا: ليس كل ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام، أو ليس بعض ما ليس بممكن ليس بموجود بالإطلاق العام والمعنى بالممكن هو الممكن بالإمكان الخاص في هذا المثال؛ إذ الموجود أي موجود كان ليس إلا وهو ممكن بالإمكان العام. فأما بالإمكان الخاص فلا مجال لذلك وإلا لكان الموجود حال كونه موجودا جائز العدم وذلك محال لاستحالة الاجتماع بين النقيضين. ولا يقال لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان من المحال أن يكون الشيء ممكنا والشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون موجودًا، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل عدمه حينئذ، أو معدوما، وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل وجوده كذلك. فإن الشيء لا يكون ممكنا إلا وأن يكون إمكانه له لذاته. ولو كان كذلك لكان ممكنا دائمًا سواء كان موجودًا أو معدوما لكنه بالنظر إلى ذاته لا بالنظر إلى وجوده وعدمه. وهذا من جملة ما مرَّ ذكره من قبل. وما هو المشهور عند الجمهور في عكس السوالب. فهو الذي مضى ذكره في هذا الفصل؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت، وبالله التوفيق.
'What is not B is not A by general possibility, otherwise its contradictory would be true; and it leads to the absurd likewise. And know that when you say "No existent is possible" or "Some existent is not possible", that is not among possibilities but among actual propositions, so it converts by conversion of contradiction thus: "Not every thing that is not possible is not existent in absolute generality" or "Not some thing that is not possible is not existent in absolute generality". And by "possible" here is meant that which is possible by specific possibility; for every existent, whatever it is, is only and is possible by general possibility. As for specific possibility, there is no scope for that; otherwise the existent, while being existent, would be possibly non-existent—that is absurd due to the impossibility of the conjunction of contradictories. And it should not be said: if it were as mentioned, it would be impossible for a thing to be possible and for a thing to be either existent—and it would then be among those whose non-existence is impossible—or non-existent—and it would be among those whose existence is likewise impossible. For a thing is not possible unless its possibility is for it in itself. And if it were so, it would be always possible whether existent or non-existent, but [this is considered] with respect to its essence, not with respect to its existence and non-existence. This is among what has been mentioned before. And what is well-known among the majority concerning conversion of negatives—that has already been mentioned in this chapter. So take heed of what you have known, and with Allah is success.'
Source pages p. 502

Commentary
This passage is a technical discussion of the conversion of negative modal categorical propositions by contradiction (عكس النقيض). The author begins by stating a conditional: if something is not B, then it is not A in general possibility; otherwise, its contradictory would be true, leading to absurdity. He then applies this to actual propositions like 'No existent is possible' or 'Some existent is not possible', which are not modal but actual. These convert by contradiction into propositions like 'Not every non-possible is non-existent' (equivalent to 'Some non-possible is existent') or 'Not some non-possible is non-existent' (equivalent to 'Every non-possible is existent'). The key distinction is between general possibility (الإمكان العام)—which applies to every existent by virtue of not being impossible—and specific possibility (الإمكان الخاص)—which would make an existent possibly non-existent while existing, a contradiction. The author then refutes a potential objection: one might think that if a thing is possible, it would be either necessarily existent or necessarily non-existent. But the author clarifies that possibility is considered with respect to the essence of the thing, not its actual existence or non-existence; a thing is always possible in itself regardless of its state. This clarifies the modal logic of conversion. The author concludes by referencing the well-known view of the majority on conversion of negatives, which was already discussed.

Key Terms
الإمكان العام
الإمكان الخاص
عكس النقيض
قضايا فعلية

A) General possibility applies to non-existents only; specific possibility to existents only.
B) General possibility applies to all existents; specific possibility would make an existent possibly non-existent, which is contradictory.
C) General possibility is used only for affirmative propositions; specific possibility for negatives.
D) There is no distinction; both terms are synonymous.

Key Takeaway
Conversion by contradiction of modal negatives is valid only when the result is interpreted under general possibility, not specific possibility, to avoid absurdity.

Then determine if the conversion holds under general possibility or specific possibility. Use a kalam example: the changing world and its temporal origination.

Two Views on Converting Negatives by Contradiction
Author presents two opinions on converting negatives by contradiction: to particular affirmative only, or also to particular negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the conversion by contradiction (عكس النقيض) to universal and particular negatives to derive the corresponding propositions, and identify the two scholarly opinions on the matter.

Core Idea
Contraposition of a universal negative yields a particular affirmative; Ibn Sina adds a particular negative.

Immediate inference includes conversion by contradiction, also known as contraposition. This lesson explores a debate: does a universal negative (e.g., 'No C is B') convert to a particular affirmative only, or also to a particular negative? We will trace the arguments of the two schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Take the universal negative 'No C is B'. Write it in standard form.
2
State the first result
According to one view, the contrapositive is 'Some not-B is not C' (particular affirmative). Write it.
This is the result of taking the contradictory of the converted proposition.
3
Apply the proof by contradiction
Assume the result is false, so 'No not-B is not C' is true. Follow the steps to derive a contradiction with the original.
The exact steps are: convert 'No not-B is not C' to 'No C is not B', then to 'Every C is B', which contradicts 'No C is B'.
4
State the alternative view
Ibn Sina and followers hold that the contrapositive can also be 'Not every not-B is not C' (particular negative). Repeat the proof similarly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل [ فيما يتعلق بالسوالب في الحمليات ] وأما [ما] ذهب إليه قوم وهو أن السالبة تنعكس عكس النقيض إلى موجبة جزئية، فذلك على خلاف ما ذهب إليه السلف وهو الذي مر ذكره. وعن الشيخ وأتباعه أنها تنعكس إلى موجبة جزئية، وإلى سالبة جزئية أيضًا. أما الموجبة الجزئية فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها منه في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية وهي قولنا بعض ما ليس بج». وأما السالبة فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في عكس نقيض السالبة الجزئية وهي قولنا ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج». واحتجوا على الأول بأنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من «ج ب» وجب أن يصدق بعض ما ليس بج» وإلا لصدق لا شيء مما ليس بج»؛ وهذا مما ينعكس إلى قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) ؛ وإنه مما يستلزم قولنا كل ج ب»، وقد كان لا شيء من (ج) ب»، هذا خلف، وأنت تعرف أن العكس إما بجهة الإطلاق وإما بجهة الإمكان العام. وعلى الثاني أنه إذا صدق قولنا ليس كل ج ب وجب أن يصدق ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وإنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ج ب دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وكان ليس كل «ج ب»، هذا خلف. وقوم من المتقدمين [1] اعترضوا على قولهم في الأول أنه يلزم من صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) في الخارج صدق قولنا كل ج ب» في الخارج أن سلب الإيجاب الخارجي السالب المحمول لا يستلزم الإيجاب الخارجي المحمول لجواز أن لا يكون الموضوع / موجودًا أصلا كما تقدم. [١٤٥ظ] والسالبة المحصلة المحمول المقابلة لهذه الموجبة إذا فرضت أنها عكس نقيض السوالب لم يلزمها إلا بالاعتبارين المذكورين في الموجبات بطريق الاتفاق. ولو كان محمول هذه الموجبة نقيض موضوع الأصل، وفرضت أنها هي العكس لزمت الخارجية المطلقة السالبة الطرفين بطريق الاتفاق دون معدولتهما بل المعدولة أحدهما لجواز أن يكون الموضوع نفس الوجود أو لازما له مع امتناع المحمول كقولنا لا شيء من الموجود بخلاء، ولا يصدق بعض ما هو خلاء في الخارج لا موجود أو ليس بموجود في الخارج.
'Chapter [concerning negatives in categorical propositions]. As for what some people held, that the negative converts by conversion of contradiction into a particular affirmative—this is contrary to what the predecessors held, and it has been mentioned earlier. And from the Shaykh [Ibn Sina] and his followers, that it converts into a particular affirmative and also into a particular negative. As for the particular affirmative, it is what has been mentioned earlier from him concerning conversion of the contradictory of the universal negative, namely our saying "Some of what is not B is not C"? [OCR unclear] And as for the particular negative, it is what has been mentioned concerning conversion of the contradictory of the particular negative, namely our saying "Not every what is not B is not C". And they argued for the first [view] that if our saying "No C is B" is true, then "Some of what is not B is not C" must be true; otherwise "Nothing of what is not B is not C" would be true; and this converts to our saying "No C is not B"; and that entails our saying "Every C is B", but it was [given that] "No C is B"—this is a contradiction. And you know that the conversion is either by absolute necessity or by general possibility. And for the second [view] that if our saying "Not every C is B" is true, then "Not every what is not B is not C" must be true always or necessarily; and it converts by conversion of contradiction to our saying "Every C is B" always or necessarily; but it was [given that] "Not every C is B"—this is a contradiction. And some of the predecessors [1] objected to their claim concerning the first [view] that the truth of our saying "No C is not B" in the external world necessitates the truth of our saying "Every C is B" in the external world—[but] the negation of the external affirmation with a negative predicate does not entail the external affirmation with a [positive] predicate, because it is possible that the subject does not exist at all, as previously [explained]. And the fully determinate negative [proposition] with a [positive] predicate opposite to this affirmative, if it is assumed to be the conversion of the contradictory of negatives, it is only entailed by the two considerations mentioned in the affirmative [propositions] accidentally. And if the predicate of this affirmative were the contradictory of the subject of the original, and it were assumed to be the conversion, then the absolute external [proposition] with both terms negated would be entailed accidentally, not their modified [forms]; rather the modified one of them [is entailed] accidentally—because it is possible that the subject is existence itself or a necessary concomitant of it while the predicate is impossible, like our saying "No existent is a vacuum", yet it is not true that "Some vacuum is existent" or "not existent" in the external world.'
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage is a complex discussion on the conversion of negative categorical propositions by contradiction. The author first presents two opposing views. Some (likely a later group) hold that a universal negative (e.g., 'No C is B') converts by contradiction to a particular affirmative (i.e., 'Some not-B is not C'?), while the predecessors (السلف) held a different view, previously mentioned. The Shaykh (Ibn Sina) and his followers hold that it converts to both a particular affirmative and a particular negative. The particular affirmative is the conversion of the contradictory of the universal negative, and the particular negative is the conversion of the contradictory of the particular negative. Then the author provides proofs for each view using reduction to absurdity (خلف). For the first view: if 'No C is B' is true, then 'Some not-B is not C' must be true; otherwise 'Nothing not-B is not C' would be true, which converts to 'No C is not B' (by simple conversion?), and that entails 'Every C is B', contradicting the original. The conversion is either absolute or by general possibility. For the second view: if 'Not every C is B' (i.e., some C are not B) is true, then 'Not every not-B is not C' must be true always or necessarily; this converts by contradiction to 'Every C is B' always/necessarily, contradicting the original. Then some predecessors object to the first proof, arguing that from 'No C is not B' in the external world, it does not follow that 'Every C is B' because the subject might not exist at all, as previously explained. The final part discusses the conversion of the fully determinate negative (السالبة المحصلة) with a predicate opposite to an affirmative, and how it relates to the conversion of the contradictory of negatives. The author notes that if the predicate is the contradictory of the subject of the original, the conversion yields an absolute external negative, but this is only accidental, not necessary, due to possibility of empty subjects. This passage requires deep familiarity with the technicalities of conversion and the distinctions between internal and external propositions.

Key Terms
السالبة المحصلة
الخارجية المطلقة
المعدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the proof given for the first view, if 'No C is B' is true, what must be true according to the rule of contraposition?
A) Some not-B is not C
B) No not-B is not C
C) Every C is B
D) Some C is not B

Key Takeaway
Contraposition of a universal negative yields a particular affirmative; Ibn Sina's addition of a particular negative is contested.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the contrapositive of the universal negative 'No act of worship is invalid without intention' and state whether the result is a particular affirmative or a particular negative according to each view.
?
Given the particular negative 'Some contracts are not valid', apply the second view to derive its contrapositive and check if the proof by contradiction holds when the subject 'contracts' is known to exist.

The Objection from Existential Import in Contraposition
Objection to the first proof based on the possibility of an empty subject.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when contraposition fails due to the empty subject condition and explain the role of existential import.

Core Idea
Contraposition is only valid if the terms actually exist; otherwise the inference is unsound.

The contrapositive proofs presented in the previous lesson assume that the subject exists. Some predecessors objected that from 'No C is not B' one cannot infer 'Every C is B' if C might be empty. This lesson examines that objection and the distinction between absolute external propositions and modified ones.

Guided Path
1
Recall the objection
Some predecessors argued that the proof for the first view is flawed because it assumes that 'No C is not B' entails 'Every C is B' only if C exists. If C does not exist, 'No C is not B' can be true vacuously, but 'Every C is B' would be false.
2
Understand the distinction
The fully determinate negative (السالبة المحصلة) with a positive predicate does not automatically entail its affirmative counterpart when the subject is empty. The author brings in the absolute external proposition (الخارجية المطلقة) to handle this.
3
Apply to a concrete example
Consider 'No existent is a vacuum'. This is true because vacuums do not exist as existents. But the contrapositive 'Some vacuum is not existent' is false because vacuums do not exist at all. So contraposition fails.
4
Assess the conditions for valid contraposition
The conversion is only entailed accidentally, not necessarily, when the subject may be non-existent. So the original proof only works under the assumption of existential import.

Defining the Obversion-Converse of Universal Negative
Author defines the partial affirmative as obversion-converse of universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to perform the obversion-converse operation on a universal negative proposition (e.g., 'No C is B') to obtain the corresponding partial affirmative ('Some non-B is C').

Core Idea
Obversion-conversion turns a universal negative into a partial affirmative about the complement of the predicate.

In this lesson, we learn the first step of a powerful logical transformation: the obversion-converse (عكس نقيض). The author defines two results: for a universal negative, the output is a partial affirmative; for a partial negative, the output is a negative proposition. We focus on the first case and then apply it to Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original proposition
Start with a universal negative (E-type) like 'No C is B' (e.g., 'No intoxicant is permissible').
2
Obvert the proposition
Change the quality from negative to affirmative and replace the predicate with its contradictory: 'No C is B' becomes 'Every C is non-B' (obversion).
Obversion preserves truth.
3
Convert the obverted proposition
Convert the result by swapping the subject and predicate: 'Every C is non-B' becomes 'Some non-B is C' (conversion per accidens). This is the obversion-converse.
The result is a partial affirmative (I-type).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجبة الجزئية فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها منه في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية وهي قولنا بعض ما ليس بج». وأما السالبة فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في عكس نقيض السالبة الجزئية وهي قولنا ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج».
واحتجوا على الأول بأنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من «ج ب» وجب أن يصدق بعض ما ليس بج» وإلا لصدق لا شيء مما ليس بج»؛ وهذا مما ينعكس إلى قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) ؛ وإنه مما يستلزم قولنا كل ج ب»، وقد كان لا شيء من (ج) ب»، هذا خلف، وأنت تعرف أن العكس إما بجهة الإطلاق وإما بجهة الإمكان العام. وعلى الثاني أنه إذا صدق قولنا ليس كل ج ب وجب أن يصدق ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وإنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ج ب دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وكان ليس كل «ج ب»، هذا خلف.
وقوم من المتقدمين اعترضوا على قولهم في الأول أنه يلزم من صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) في الخارج صدق قولنا كل ج ب» في الخارج أن سلب الإيجاب الخارجي السالب المحمول لا يستلزم الإيجاب الخارجي المحمول لجواز أن لا يكون الموضوع موجودًا أصلا كما تقدم. والسالبة المحصلة المحمول المقابلة لهذه الموجبة إذا فرضت أنها عكس نقيض السوالب لم يلزمها إلا بالاعتبارين المذكورين في الموجبات بطريق الاتفاق. ولو كان محمول هذه الموجبة نقيض موضوع الأصل، وفرضت أنها هي العكس لزمت الخارجية المطلقة السالبة الطرفين بطريق الاتفاق دون معدولتهما بل المعدولة أحدهما لجواز أن يكون الموضوع نفس الوجود أو لازما له مع امتناع المحمول كقولنا لا شيء من الموجود بخلاء، ولا يصدق بعض ما هو خلاء في الخارج لا موجود أو ليس بموجود في الخارج.
The partial affirmative is that which was mentioned earlier regarding the obversion-converse of the universal negative, namely our saying 'Some of what is not B is C'. As for the negative, it is that which was mentioned regarding the obversion-converse of the partial negative, namely our saying 'Not every what is not B is not C'.
They argued for the first by saying: If our statement 'No C is B' is true, then 'Some of what is not B is C' must be true, otherwise 'Nothing of what is not B is C' would be true; and this converts to our statement 'No C is not B'; and this entails our statement 'Every C is B', while previously it was 'No C is B' — this is a contradiction. And you know that conversion is either by absolute modality or by general possibility. And for the second: If our statement 'Not every C is B' is true, then 'Not every what is not B is not C' must be true always or necessarily; and it converts by obversion-converse to our statement 'Every C is B' always or necessarily; while it was 'Not every C is B' — this is a contradiction.
A group of earlier scholars objected to their statement in the first [argument] that it follows from the truth of our statement 'No C is not B' in the external [sense] the truth of our statement 'Every C is B' in the external [sense] — that the negation of the external affirmative with a negated predicate does not entail the external affirmative with a positive predicate, because it is possible that the subject does not exist at all, as previously mentioned. The straightforward negative with a positive predicate corresponding to this affirmative, if assumed to be the obversion-converse of negatives, would not follow except by the two considerations mentioned in affirmatives by way of agreement. And if the predicate of this affirmative were the contradictory of the original subject, and it is assumed that it is the converse, then the absolute external negative of both terms would follow by way of agreement, not their modified terms, but rather the modification of one of them, because it is possible that the subject is existence itself or a necessary concomitant of it while the predicate is impossible, such as our saying 'Nothing existing is a vacuum', and 'Some of what is a vacuum does not exist in the external world' is not true — neither existing nor not existing in the external world.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage defines two propositions resulting from the obversion-converse (عكس نقيض) of universal negative and partial negative. The author first states that the partial affirmative ('Some non-B is C') is the obversion-converse of 'No C is B', and the negative ('Not every non-B is not C') is the obversion-converse of 'Not every C is B'. He then provides a proof by contradiction for each. For the first: if 'No C is B' is true but the obversion-converse were false, then 'Nothing of what is not B is C' would be true, which by simple conversion becomes 'No C is not B'. But that implies 'Every C is B', contradicting the original. The second proof is analogous. Then comes an objection from earlier logicians: they argue that the entailment from 'No C is not B' (external) to 'Every C is B' (external) fails because the subject may be empty. For example, 'Nothing existing is a vacuum' does not imply 'Every existing thing is a vacuum' or that 'Some vacuum exists'. The objection highlights the condition of existential import. The passage shows the care needed in conversion rules when dealing with external propositions.

Key Terms
عكس نقيض
خارجية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the obversion-converse of the universal negative 'No intoxicant is permissible'?
A) Some permissible is intoxicant
B) Some non-permissible is intoxicant
C) Every intoxicant is non-permissible
D) No non-permissible is intoxicant

Key Takeaway
The obversion-converse of a universal negative yields a partial affirmative about the complement of the predicate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the statement 'No forbidden thing (haram) is pure (tahir)' from Islamic law. Perform the obversion-converse to find the partial affirmative result.
?
Given 'No sin (ma'siya) is rewarded (thawab)', apply obversion-converse and state the result in Arabic.

Proof of Obversion-Conversion for Universal Negative
Author proves the obversion-converse of universal negative by reductio.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the proof by contradiction that validates the obversion-converse of a universal negative proposition.

Core Idea
The proof uses the fact that if the result were false, converting and obverting would lead to a contradiction with the original.

The author provides a rigorous proof for the obversion-converse of the universal negative using a reductio ad absurdum. This shows that logical transformations can be justified by fundamental principles. We will go through the steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Assume the original is true
Start with 'No C is B' (e.g., 'No human is stone'). Assume this is true.
2
Suppose the obversion-converse is false
If 'Some non-B is C' were false, then its contradictory 'Nothing of what is not B is C' would be true.
The contradictory of a partial affirmative is a universal negative.
3
Convert the contradictory
Convert 'Nothing of what is not B is C' to 'No C is not B' (simple conversion of a universal negative). This is equivalent to 'Every C is B' by obversion.
This step relies on the validity of simple conversion for universal negatives.
4
Derive a contradiction
We now have 'Every C is B' from the assumption, but the original says 'No C is B'. This is a contradiction. Therefore, the assumption that the obversion-converse is false must be false, so the obversion-converse is true.
The proof shows that the obversion-converse necessarily follows from the original.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموجبة الجزئية فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها منه في عكس نقيض السالبة الكلية وهي قولنا بعض ما ليس بج». وأما السالبة فهي التي مرَّ ذكرها في عكس نقيض السالبة الجزئية وهي قولنا ليس كل ما ليس «ب» ليس «ج».
واحتجوا على الأول بأنه إذا صدق قولنا لا شيء من «ج ب» وجب أن يصدق بعض ما ليس بج» وإلا لصدق لا شيء مما ليس بج»؛ وهذا مما ينعكس إلى قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) ؛ وإنه مما يستلزم قولنا كل ج ب»، وقد كان لا شيء من (ج) ب»، هذا خلف، وأنت تعرف أن العكس إما بجهة الإطلاق وإما بجهة الإمكان العام. وعلى الثاني أنه إذا صدق قولنا ليس كل ج ب وجب أن يصدق ليس كل ما ليس (ب) ليس (ج) دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وإنه ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا كل ج ب دائما أو بالضرورة؛ وكان ليس كل «ج ب»، هذا خلف.
وقوم من المتقدمين اعترضوا على قولهم في الأول أنه يلزم من صدق قولنا لا شيء من (ج) ليس (ب) في الخارج صدق قولنا كل ج ب» في الخارج أن سلب الإيجاب الخارجي السالب المحمول لا يستلزم الإيجاب الخارجي المحمول لجواز أن لا يكون الموضوع موجودًا أصلا كما تقدم. والسالبة المحصلة المحمول المقابلة لهذه الموجبة إذا فرضت أنها عكس نقيض السوالب لم يلزمها إلا بالاعتبارين المذكورين في الموجبات بطريق الاتفاق. ولو كان محمول هذه الموجبة نقيض موضوع الأصل، وفرضت أنها هي العكس لزمت الخارجية المطلقة السالبة الطرفين بطريق الاتفاق دون معدولتهما بل المعدولة أحدهما لجواز أن يكون الموضوع نفس الوجود أو لازما له مع امتناع المحمول كقولنا لا شيء من الموجود بخلاء، ولا يصدق بعض ما هو خلاء في الخارج لا موجود أو ليس بموجود في الخارج.
The partial affirmative is that which was mentioned earlier regarding the obversion-converse of the universal negative, namely our saying 'Some of what is not B is C'. As for the negative, it is that which was mentioned regarding the obversion-converse of the partial negative, namely our saying 'Not every what is not B is not C'.
They argued for the first by saying: If our statement 'No C is B' is true, then 'Some of what is not B is C' must be true, otherwise 'Nothing of what is not B is C' would be true; and this converts to our statement 'No C is not B'; and this entails our statement 'Every C is B', while previously it was 'No C is B' — this is a contradiction. And you know that conversion is either by absolute modality or by general possibility. And for the second: If our statement 'Not every C is B' is true, then 'Not every what is not B is not C' must be true always or necessarily; and it converts by obversion-converse to our statement 'Every C is B' always or necessarily; while it was 'Not every C is B' — this is a contradiction.
A group of earlier scholars objected to their statement in the first [argument] that it follows from the truth of our statement 'No C is not B' in the external [sense] the truth of our statement 'Every C is B' in the external [sense] — that the negation of the external affirmative with a negated predicate does not entail the external affirmative with a positive predicate, because it is possible that the subject does not exist at all, as previously mentioned. The straightforward negative with a positive predicate corresponding to this affirmative, if assumed to be the obversion-converse of negatives, would not follow except by the two considerations mentioned in affirmatives by way of agreement. And if the predicate of this affirmative were the contradictory of the original subject, and it is assumed that it is the converse, then the absolute external negative of both terms would follow by way of agreement, not their modified terms, but rather the modification of one of them, because it is possible that the subject is existence itself or a necessary concomitant of it while the predicate is impossible, such as our saying 'Nothing existing is a vacuum', and 'Some of what is a vacuum does not exist in the external world' is not true — neither existing nor not existing in the external world.
Source pages p. 503

Commentary
This passage defines two propositions resulting from the obversion-converse (عكس نقيض) of universal negative and partial negative. The author first states that the partial affirmative ('Some non-B is C') is the obversion-converse of 'No C is B', and the negative ('Not every non-B is not C') is the obversion-converse of 'Not every C is B'. He then provides a proof by contradiction for each. For the first: if 'No C is B' is true but the obversion-converse were false, then 'Nothing of what is not B is C' would be true, which by simple conversion becomes 'No C is not B'. But that implies 'Every C is B', contradicting the original. The second proof is analogous. Then comes an objection from earlier logicians: they argue that the entailment from 'No C is not B' (external) to 'Every C is B' (external) fails because the subject may be empty. For example, 'Nothing existing is a vacuum' does not imply 'Every existing thing is a vacuum' or that 'Some vacuum exists'. The objection highlights the condition of existential import. The passage shows the care needed in conversion rules when dealing with external propositions.

عكس نقيض
خارجية

B) 'Nothing of what is not B is C' is true
C) 'Some C is not B' is true
D) 'Every non-B is C' is true

Key Takeaway
The obversion-converse of a universal negative is proved by contradiction using conversion and obversion.

Objection to the Proof: The Problem of Empty Subjects
Earlier logicians object to the proof's validity for empty subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the obversion-converse of a universal negative fails due to an empty subject and explain the earlier logicians' objection.

Core Idea
The proof fails if the subject does not exist because the conversion step 'No C is not B' does not entail 'Every C is B' when C is empty.

Some earlier logicians raised a powerful objection to the proof: it assumes existential import. If the subject term (C) is empty, the conversion from 'Nothing of what is not B is C' to 'No C is not B' is valid, but the step from 'No C is not B' to 'Every C is B' requires that there is at least one C. Without that, we cannot conclude the contradiction. This lesson dissects that objection.

Guided Path
1
Recall the problematic step
In the proof, after conversion we get 'No C is not B'. The author then says this entails 'Every C is B'. But is that always true?
2
Consider an empty subject
If C is empty (e.g., 'phoenix' with no existing individuals), then 'No phoenix is not B' is vacuously true. But 'Every phoenix is B' is also vacuously true? Actually, in standard logic, universal affirmatives with empty subjects are often considered false or lacking truth value. In this context, the author uses 'external' (خارجية) propositions that require existence. So for external propositions, if C is empty, both 'No C is not B' and 'Every C is B' can be false because there is no C to instantiate them.
The nuance: 'Every C is B' would be false in external sense because no C exists to satisfy the condition, while 'No C is not B' might be considered true if there are no C that are not B (vacuously). The entailment breaks.
3
Example with vacuum
The author gives the example: 'Nothing existing is a vacuum' is true. The obversion-converse would be 'Some non-vacuum is existing', which is true. But if we try the reverse direction: from 'No existing thing is a vacuum', we get 'Some non-vacuum is existing', which is true because there are existing non-vacuums. However, the objection is about the proof: if we say 'No C is not B' does not entail 'Every C is B' when C is empty because the two statements differ in existential import.
4
Understand the conclusion
The objection shows that the proof only works when the subject is non-empty. This is a subtle point that later logicians addressed by restricting the domain or using 'genuine' (حقيقية) propositions that are unconditional.
This lesson highlights the importance of existential import in logical inferences.

Obversion-Converse Failure for Modified Terms
Author presents the second objection to the obversion-converse of partial negative and then resolves it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when the obversion-converse rule fails due to term modification and provide a counterexample from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Affirmative with positive terms does not convert in quality to a modified proposition.

The obversion-converse rule allows us to transform a universal negative statement like 'No non-B is J' into 'Every J is B'. But objectors have shown a flaw: the rule fails when the original proposition uses modified terms. In this lesson, we dissect their objection and see why the rule cannot be applied automatically.

Guided Path
1
Recall the obversion-converse rule
For a partial negative proposition like 'Some J is not B', the rule says we can obvert to 'Some J is non-B' and then convert to 'Some non-B is J'. Here, the objectors test a similar move: from 'Every non-B is not J' (universal negative with modified terms) they claim the rule would give 'Every J is B'. You must spot the difference: the starting proposition has negated subject and predicate.
2
Identify the objection's core
The objectors deny that the entailment holds. Their reason: an affirmative proposition with both terms positive (like 'Every J is B') cannot be derived from a proposition where both terms are modified (like 'Every non-B is not J') simply by changing quality. The rule requires the proposition to be of the same 'term status' (positive vs modified).
This is a technical point about 'محصلة الطرفين' versus 'معدولة الطرفين'.
3
Analyze the counterexample
The objectors give an example: 'Every what is not extended is not a vacuum' is true (since vacuum lacks extension). If the rule applied, we would infer 'Every vacuum is extended' – but this is false in the external world (vacuum might not exist or have extension). This shows the rule fails.
The example uses 'vacuum' (خلاء) and 'extended' (متحيز) – a standard metaphysical example.
4
Understand the author's response
The author does not repeat the resolution but says it has already been discussed earlier. This means the objection is not fatal; the rule can be qualified or defended by additional conditions. You must check the earlier resolution to see how the rule is salvaged.
The author treats this as a known objection with an established answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترضوا أيضًا على قولهم في الثاني وهو أنه إذا صدق كل ما ليس «ب» ليس ج دائما أو بالضرورة لزم كل ج) (ب) أن هذا اللزوم في حيز المنع؛ لأنا بينا أن الموجبة المحصلة الطرفين لا تنعكس كنفسها في الكيف إلى معدولة الطرفين. ولأنه لا يصدق وقد صدق قولنا كل ما ليس بمتحيز ليس بخلاء، أو كل ما هو لا متحيز هو لا خلاء؛ ولا يصدق كل خلاء متحيز في الخارج.
ثم الكلام في مثل هذه الاعتراضات قد مرّ من قبل فلا نعيده مرة أخرى
They also objected to their statement in the second [argument], namely that if 'Every what is not B is not J' is true always or necessarily, then 'Every J is B' would follow — they say that this entailment is subject to denial; because we have shown that the affirmative with both terms positive does not convert as itself in quality to a modified proposition of both terms. And because it is not true, while our statement 'Every what is not extended is not a vacuum' is true, or 'Every what is non-extended is non-vacuum'; but 'Every vacuum is extended' is not true in the external world.
Then the discussion regarding such objections has already passed, so we will not repeat it again.
Source pages p. 504

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the obversion-converse rule for the partial negative proposition. The objectors argue that the claimed entailment from 'Every non-B is not J' to 'Every J is B' is invalid. Their reasoning is that an affirmative proposition with both terms positive (like 'Every J is B') cannot be derived simply by changing the quality to a proposition with both terms modified (like 'Every non-B is not J'). They give an example: 'Every what is not extended is not a vacuum' is true, but 'Every vacuum is extended' is false in the external world (since 'vacuum' might not exist or have extension). This shows the rule fails. The author then notes that such objections have been discussed before and declines to repeat the resolution here. This is a typical dialectical move: acknowledging an objection but referring to earlier treatment.

D) Because the conversion must be by accidental modality

Key Takeaway
The obversion-converse rule fails when the original proposition uses modified terms; an affirmative with positive terms does not convert in quality to a modified proposition.

Conversion of External Negative Propositions
Author explains which negative external propositions convert to partial affirmatives and which do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a given negative external proposition converts to a partial affirmative by applying the conditions from this passage.

Core Idea
Only two specific external negatives convert to affirmatives; the rest do not.

This passage from a commentary on logic clarifies an important nuance in the conversion (عكس) of negative propositions. When a negative proposition is external (خارجية) – meaning its truth depends on actual existence of the subject – the standard conversion rules do not apply universally. Only two specific forms of external negatives yield a valid conversion to a partial affirmative. Understanding this exception is crucial for accurate logical inference in Islamic sciences, especially in Kalam where existential claims are common.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish external from genuine propositions
Identify whether the proposition's truth requires actual existence (خارجية) or not (حقيقية). This distinction is foundational to the conversion rule.
2
Locate the two special external negatives
The author states that only two specific external negatives convert. Look for the conditions: the predicate either agrees with the original subject or is its opposite, and the resulting affirmative is non-permanent.
The Arabic phrase 'إلا الخاصتين' marks the exception.
3
Test an example that does NOT convert
Consider 'لا شيء من الخلاء ببعد' (Nothing of vacuum is distant). According to the author, this external negative does not convert to a partial affirmative because none of the corresponding affirmatives are true.
The vacuum example illustrates the failure.
4
Understand the rationale
The conversion fails because the external negative is true while any affirmative would require existence of the subject, which is absent. The rule prevents invalid inferences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد الاعتراض ذهبوا إلى أن السوالب إذا كانت خارجية لا تنعكس إلى موجبة جزئية مرَّ ذكرها إلا الخاصتين فإنهما تنعكسان إلى الموجبة الجزئية اللا دائمة الموافقة المحمول لموضوع الأصل مطلقا لصدقها خارجية مطلقة لما مرَّ في العكس المستوي؛ والسالبة الموضوع أيضًا مطلقة لكونها أعم من المعدولة الموضوع، وإلى الموجبة الجزئية الوجودية اللا دائمة المقابلة المحمول الموضوع الأصل مطلقًا لصدقها خارجية مطلقة وإلا فلا شيء من لأ (ب) لا (ج) دائمًا أو كل لا (ب) لا (ج) دائما. ومع الثاني كل «ج» لا (ج)» دائمًا، هذا خلف. ويجب أن يصدق أيضًا ما هو الأعم من هذه الموجبة المعدولة الطرفين. وإن كانا حقيقيين فإنهما ينعكسان إلى ما لم يتوقف صدقها على وجود الذات من القضايا. وما عدا الخاصتين منها إذا كانت خارجية كلية كانت أو جزئية فلا ينعكس إلى موجبة جزئية مرَّ ذكرها سواء أخذتها سالبة الموضوع أو معدولة الموضوع إلا الخارجية الموضوع السالبة والحقيقية المطلقة المعدولة الموضوع بطريق الاتفاق. فإنه يصدق لا شيء من الخلاء يبعد في الخارج، ولا يصدق شيء من الموجبات أصلا لا سالبة الموضوع وهي بعض ما ليس ببعد أو ليس ملزوما له خلاء في الخارج، وبعض ما ليس ملزوما له ليس ملزوما للخلاء؛ إذ المعنى بالخلاء البعد المجرد ليكون كل
And after the objection, they went to the view that negative [propositions], if they are external, do not convert to a partial affirmative as mentioned, except two specific ones; for they both convert to the non-permanent partial affirmative whose predicate agrees with the subject of the original absolutely, because it is true as absolute external as previously mentioned regarding simple conversion; and the negative subject is also absolute because it is more general than the modified subject; and to the existential non-permanent partial affirmative whose predicate is the opposite of the subject of the original absolutely, because it is true as absolute external; otherwise, nothing of the non-B is non-J always, or every non-B is non-J always. And with the second, 'Every J is not J' always — this is a contradiction. And what is more general than this affirmative with both terms modified must also be true. And if they are genuine [propositions], then they convert to propositions whose truth does not depend on the existence of substance. Commentary
This passage discusses which negative external propositions can be converted to a particular affirmative. The author states that aside from two specific cases, external negatives do not convert. The two special cases convert to a non-permanent partial affirmative whose predicate is either the same as or opposite to the original subject. The author then explains the conditions using examples. For genuine (حقيقية) propositions that do not presuppose existence, the conversion yields propositions independent of existence. The final example about 'vacuum' shows that an external negative like 'Nothing of vacuum is distant' is true, but none of the corresponding affirmatives are true. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the main point is clear: conversion rules are restricted for external negatives.

Refuting a Conversion Rule with Indefinite Terms
Author refutes an objection about conversion of indefinite universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the validity of the conversion rule that 'All non-B is non-C' implies 'All C is B' using counterexamples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Conversion of propositions with indefinite terms does not always preserve truth—counterexamples in physics prove it.

In this passage, the author addresses an objection to the claim that a universal negative with indefinite terms can be converted to an affirmative. He shows that such conversion is not always valid by providing a concrete example from natural philosophy involving empty space and extension. This lesson will walk you through the logical structure of the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim under objection
The claim is: If 'Every not-B is not-C' is true (always or necessarily), then 'Every C is B' follows. Locate this in the Arabic: 'إذا صدق كل ما ليس «ب» ليس ج دائما أو بالضرورة لزم كل ج) (ب)'. Note the logical form.
The claim is about converting a universal negative with indefinite terms to a universal affirmative.
2
Understand the objection's grounds
The objector denies the implication because, as earlier shown, an affirmative with both terms definite (الموجبة المحصلة الطرفين) does not convert as itself in quality to one with both terms indefinite (معدولة الطرفين). The objector argues that the conversion fails due to the change in term quality.
The terms 'definite' (محصلة) mean positive terms; 'indefinite' (معدولة) mean negated or privative terms.
3
Examine the counterexample
The author provides a concrete counterexample: 'Every what is not extended is not empty' (كل ما ليس بمتحيز ليس بخلاء) is true. But the conversion 'Every empty is extended' (كل خلاء متحيز) is false in the external world. 'Empty' refers to void space, which is not extended, so the converse is false.
This shows that the premise is true but the conclusion is false, so the implication is invalid.
4
Assess the logical insight
The conversion rule fails because the original proposition has both terms negated (indefinite), while the conclusion has both terms positive (definite). The author reminds that such objections have been discussed before, indicating a well-known logical principle: conversion does not preserve truth when the quality or quantity of terms changes in this way.
This insight helps avoid invalid inferences in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترضوا أيضًا على قولهم في الثاني وهو أنه إذا صدق كل ما ليس «ب» ليس ج دائما أو بالضرورة لزم كل ج) (ب) أن هذا اللزوم في حيز المنع؛ لأنا بينا أن الموجبة المحصلة الطرفين لا تنعكس كنفسها في الكيف إلى معدولة الطرفين. ولأنه لا يصدق وقد صدق قولنا كل ما ليس بمتحيز ليس بخلاء، أو كل ما هو لا متحيز هو لا خلاء؛ ولا يصدق كل خلاء متحيز في الخارج. ثم الكلام في مثل هذه الاعتراضات قد مرّ من قبل فلا نعيده مرة أخرى.
They also objected to their statement in the second [case], namely that if 'Every what is not B is not C' is true always or necessarily, then 'Every C is B' follows. [They said] this implication is in the realm of denial; because we have shown that the affirmative with both terms definite does not convert as itself in quality to the one with both terms indefinite. And because it is not true, while our statement 'Every what is not extended is not empty' is true, or 'Every what is not extended is not empty', yet it is not true that 'Every empty is extended' in the external world. Then the discussion about such objections has passed before, so we will not repeat it again.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection raised against a claim about conversion. The claim is that if the universal negative 'All not-B is not-C' holds, then the affirmative 'All C is B' follows. The author refutes this by stating that the implication is not valid. He recalls that a standard affirmative with both terms definite (i.e., subject and predicate are positive terms) does not convert to an indefinite-term counterpart in the same quality. He provides a concrete counterexample: the statement 'Every what is not extended is not empty' (or 'Every non-extended is non-empty') is true, but the conversion 'Every empty is extended' is false in the external world. 'Empty' (خلاء) refers to void or empty space, which is not extended, so the conversion fails. The author then notes that such objections have been discussed before and will not be repeated, indicating a previous treatment.

Conversion of External Universal Negatives
Author explains conversion rules for external negatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an external universal negative proposition can undergo conversion and distinguish the two special types that convert to a particular affirmative.

Core Idea
Only two special types of external universal negatives convert to a particular affirmative; the rest do not.

In classical Islamic logic, the conversion (عكس) of negative propositions is not straightforward. This passage explains that external negatives (تنتفي عن الوجود الخارجي) behave differently. You need to know which ones convert and under what conditions, because incorrect conversion leads to false conclusions in Islamic arguments, especially in jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify external vs real propositions
First, determine whether a proposition is external (خارجية) or real (حقيقية). External propositions consider actual existence in the world, while real propositions consider essential attributes regardless of existence. This distinction affects conversion rules.
2
Recognize the two special types
Among external universal negatives, only two special types (الخاصتين) convert to a particular affirmative. Look for subject-negative (سالبة الموضوع) or indefinite-subject (معدولة الموضوع) forms with specific conditions.
The passage notes that subject-negative is more general than indefinite-subject.
3
Test the counterexample with 'empty'
Consider the proposition 'Nothing from empty (خلاء) is extended (بعد)'. This is external universal negative. Attempting to convert it to an affirmative fails because any resulting affirmative would be false. Understand why: 'empty' means absolute extension, so 'Some non-extension is empty' is contradictory.
This shows why external negatives beyond the two special types do not convert.
4
Apply to real propositions
Real propositions (حقيقية) convert differently: they can convert to propositions whose truth does not depend on existence. Compare this to external propositions to see the difference in logical behavior.
5
Check for necessity or perpetuity
Propositions that negate necessity or perpetuity follow similar patterns because they revert to their affirmative forms. Recognize that these conversions are not considered counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) by the majority.

Source Text

And after the objection, they went to [the claim] that if negatives are external, they do not convert to a particular affirmative mentioned before except the two special [types], for they convert to the non-permanent particular affirmative whose predicate agrees with the subject of the original absolutely, because she is true as an absolute external as passed in simple conversion; and the subject-negative [proposition] is also absolute because it is more general than the indefinite-subject [proposition], and to the existential non-permanent particular affirmative whose predicate opposes the subject of the original absolutely, because she is true as an absolute external, otherwise nothing from ... is always not C, or every not-B is not C always. And if they are real [propositions], they convert to propositions whose truth does not depend on the existence of the essence. For it is true that nothing from empty is extended in the external world, and no affirmative is true at all, neither subject-negative like 'Some what is not extended is not empty in the external world, and some what is not a concomitant of it is not a concomitant of empty'—for the meaning of empty is absolute extension so that every concomitant of it is a concomitant of extension; nor indefinite-subject like 'Some non-extension in the external world is empty in the external world and its concomitant, and some what is a concomitant of non-extension is empty in the external world.' And every proposition whose predicate is a necessary concomitant of its subject and one of them is impossible; then it is among similar cases. And if it is real, it converts with that to other propositions by way of implication; for actual propositions convert to the particular affirmative whose predicate agrees with the subject of the original, [and which is] external subject-negative; for then one must have a concept that is a concomitant of 'C' without 'B'. If it also exists externally, it is not absolutely, otherwise it would be a concomitant of it always, and if it does not exist, then 'B' does not exist externally either. And the absolute real, its contradictory with the original does not imply 'Nothing from the concomitant of C is a concomitant of it always' as passed in the external [propositions], except that this implication is impossible here but not there. And to the negative opposite to this affirmative, [which is] the external absolute subject-negative and external subject, otherwise 'Every what is not B in the external world is a concomitant of C' would be true; and it yields with the original 'Nothing from what is not B in the external world is a concomitant of it', which is a reduction to absurdity. And on this, they are converted in their terminology to other propositions as well. However, even though they are converted to them as they believe, none of them is counter-conversion according to the majority as passed before. In summary, propositions in which the negation of necessity or perpetuity is considered also require what their affirmatives require because they revert to them. So these are some of what these people have indicated in this chapter, and Allah knows the truth.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the conversion rules for negative propositions, specifically external (خارجية) and real (حقيقية) negatives. After an initial objection, the author explains that external universal negatives do not convert to a particular affirmative except for two special types. Those two specials convert to a non-permanent particular affirmative whose predicate matches the original subject absolutely. The author distinguishes between subject-negative (سالبة الموضوع) and indefinite-subject (معدولة الموضوع) forms, noting that the subject-negative is more general. He then gives a counterexample using 'empty' (خلاء) to show that external negatives yield no true affirmative, because if we say 'Nothing from empty is extended', we cannot convert to any affirmative like 'Some empty is extended' or 'Some non-extension is empty'. The author explains that the meaning of 'empty' is absolute extension, making the conversion invalid. He then discusses real propositions (حقيقية) which consider actual existence, and how they convert by implication to other types of propositions. He notes that their conversion yields propositions that are not considered counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) by the majority. Finally, he summarises that propositions involving negation of necessity or perpetuity follow similar patterns due to their reversion to affirmatives.

Counter-Conversion of Conditionals (ʿAks al-Naqīḍ)
Definition of counter-conversion for conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define counter-conversion for conditionals and apply it to produce the contrapositive of a given conditional proposition.

Core Idea
Counter-conversion swaps the opposites of antecedent and consequent, preserving truth and quality.

In this lesson, we explore the operation of counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) as applied to conditional propositions. The passage from our text defines it as replacing the opposite of the antecedent with the consequent, and vice versa, while keeping the affirmation/negation unchanged. This operation is a powerful immediate inference that allows us to derive logically equivalent conditionals, commonly used in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original conditional
Locate the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي) in the given conditional statement. For example, 'If it is wine, then it is haram' has antecedent 'it is wine' and consequent 'it is haram'.
2
Find the opposites
Determine the opposite (contradictory) of the antecedent and the opposite of the consequent. The opposite of 'it is wine' is 'it is not wine'; the opposite of 'it is haram' is 'it is not haram'.
3
Swap the opposites
Replace the antecedent's opposite with the consequent, and the consequent's opposite with the antecedent. So from 'If it is wine, then it is haram', we get 'If it is not haram, then it is not wine'.
This is the contrapositive.
4
Preserve quality
Ensure the new statement retains the same affirmation/negation as the original. If the original is affirmative, the contrapositive is affirmative; if negative, the contrapositive is negative.
5
Verify truth preservation
The contrapositive is logically equivalent to the original; they share the same truth value. This is a key feature of counter-conversion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الموجبات من الشرطيات ] وأما عكس النقيض في الشرطيات فإنها عند الجمهور عبارة عن تبديل مقابل المقدم تاليا، ومقابل التالي مقدما مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله؛ والصدق والكذب كذلك . ثم الحكم في الموجبات هنا نحو الحكم في السوالب هناك،
Chapter: On affirmatives among conditionals. As for counter-conversion in conditionals, according to the majority it is the replacement of the opposite of the antecedent as the consequent, and the opposite of the consequent as the antecedent, with the negation and affirmation remaining as they are; and truth and falsehood likewise. Then the ruling for affirmatives here is similar to the ruling for negatives there.
Source pages p. 505

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section header that introduces the topic of counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) for conditional propositions. The author states that according to the majority of scholars (الجمهور), counter-conversion in conditionals consists of replacing the opposite of the antecedent (مقابل المقدم) as the consequent, and the opposite of the consequent (مقابل التالي) as the antecedent, while preserving the original quality of negation or affirmation. This means that if the original conditional is affirmative, the counter-converse is also affirmative; if negative, the converse is negative. Additionally, the truth value is preserved. Finally, the author notes that the ruling for affirmative conditionals is similar to the ruling for negatives, which was presumably discussed earlier. This definition is a mnemonic anchor for a key logical operation.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض (ʿaks al-naqīḍ): Counter-conversion: an immediate inference where the contradictory of one term is swapped with the other, e.g., from 'If P then Q' to 'If not-Q then not-P'. In conditionals, it involves replacing antecedent's opposite with consequent and vice versa. [Note: Stable across the tradition; also known as contrapositive.]
شرطيات (sharṭiyyāt): Conditional propositions; typically compound propositions of the form 'If...then...' (muttaṣila) or 'Either...or...' (munfaṣila). [Note: No significant shift.]
مقدم (muqaddam): The antecedent of a conditional proposition; the part that precedes 'then' in a conditional. [Note: No significant shift.]
تالي (tālī): The consequent of a conditional proposition; the part that follows 'then'. [Note: No significant shift.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Conversion of Real Propositions to External Subject
Concludes discussion on conversion of real propositions to external subject propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish real propositions from external subject propositions and identify their respective conversion forms.

Core Idea
Real propositions convert by entailment, not by counter-conversion.

This passage wraps up a detailed discussion on how real propositions (ḥaqīqiyya) convert to external subject (khārijiyya) propositions. The author emphasizes that real propositions do not convert via the standard counter-conversion (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) but through a special form of entailment. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying immediate inferences in Islamic logical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is real (ḥaqīqiyya) or external (khārijiyya). Real propositions hold for any subject whether existing or not; external propositions consider only actually existing subjects.
The passage contrasts 'ḥaqīqiyya' with 'khārijiyya'.
2
Apply the correct conversion rule
For a real proposition, the conversion is not the standard 'aks al-naqīḍ (contrapositive) but a conversion by entailment (luzūm). The result is a particular affirmative with an external subject.
The author says actual propositions 'convert to the particular affirmative whose predicate agrees with the subject of the original, the negative of external subject'.
3
Check for entailment conditions
Verify that there exists a concept that entails the predicate but is not identical to the original subject. If such a concept exists externally, the conversion is absolute; otherwise, it is not.
The passage discusses the condition using variables 'b' and 'j'.
4
Avoid confusion with counter-conversion
Note that the conversion discussed is not 'aks al-naqīḍ according to the majority. The real proposition's conversion yields a particular external, not a universal or a contrapositive.
The author explicitly says 'none of them is the counter-conversion according to the majority'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

An entailed thing is necessarily entailed by the distant; and the infinite subject, which is not a distance in the external world, is a void in the external world, and an entailed thing, and some of what entails non-distance is a void in the external world. And every proposition whose predicate necessarily follows its subject and one of them is impossible, it is among the analogs of that. Commentary
This passage concludes a discussion on the conversion of real propositions (حقيقية) to external subject propositions. The author explains that real propositions convert by entailment to particular affirmatives with external subject, not via counter-conversion. He argues that for a real proposition like 'Every b is j', the conversion yields 'Some j is b' only if there exists a concept that entails j without being b. If that concept also exists externally, the conversion is absolute; otherwise, it is not. The author distinguishes real absolute propositions from external ones, noting that the absolute real does not entail its contradictory. He then shows that if the conversion were to a negative, a contradiction arises. Finally, he states that propositions with negation of necessity or perpetuity also have conversions similar to their affirmatives. This passage reinforces the technical distinction between types of conversion in Aristotelian logic as interpreted in the Islamic tradition.

Counter-Conversion in Conditionals: Definition and Proof for Necessary Conditionals
Defines counter-conversion and proves it for four conditional types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define counter-conversion for conditional propositions and prove its validity for necessary conditionals using a reductio argument.

Core Idea
Counter-conversion swaps the contradictory of each part while preserving quality.

This passage introduces the logical operation of counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) as applied to conditional propositions. Unlike categorical conversion, this operation transforms a conditional by swapping the contradictory of the antecedent with the consequent and vice versa, while keeping negation and affirmation unchanged. The author then demonstrates that only certain universal conditionals—necessary, perpetual, universally conditional, and customarily universal—yield a valid counter-conversion. We will focus on the definition and the proof for the necessary conditional.

Guided Path
1
Identify the operation
Find the definition of counter-conversion in the Arabic: 'تبديل مقابل المقدم تاليا، ومقابل التالي مقدما مع بقاء السلب والإيجاب بحاله'. Note that it involves swapping the contradictories of antecedent and consequent.
The quality (negation/affirmation) of the parts is preserved.
2
Extract the types of conditionals that are convertible
The author states that only universal conditionals are convertible, and among them only four types: necessary (ضرورية), perpetual (دائمة), universally conditional (مشروطة عامة), and customarily universal (عرفية عامة). List them.
These are the affirmative universal conditionals that admit counter-conversion.
3
Follow the proof for the necessary conditional
The proof uses reductio ad absurdum. Assume the original is true: 'necessarily whenever A is B, then C is D'. If the counter-conversion were false, its contradictory would be true: 'not necessarily whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B)'. This leads to a contradiction with the original, so the counter-conversion must be true.
The reasoning relies on the impossibility of the original and its contradictory being true simultaneously.
4
Generalize to other types
The perpetual conditional is similar except without the word 'necessity'. The universally conditional adds a condition 'as long as A is B', and its counter-conversion holds with a similar proof. The customarily universal follows the same pattern.
Each type preserves the modal or conditional qualification in the converse.

Section: On affirmatives among conditionals. As for counter-conversion in conditionals, according to the majority it is the replacement of the contradictory of the antecedent with the consequent, and the contradictory of the consequent with the antecedent, while preserving negation and affirmation; truth and falsehood are likewise. Then the rule for affirmatives here is similar to the rule for negatives there, and for negatives like affirmatives, as previously. Affirmatives, therefore, are not convertible unless they are universal, and the universal is not convertible as itself unless it is necessary, perpetual, universally conditional, or customarily universal. (1) For the necessary: it is said necessarily whenever A is B, then C is D; then necessarily whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B); otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'not whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B)'. So its counter-conversion is 'not whenever A is B, then C is D'; but it was necessarily that whenever A is B, then C is D, which is a contradiction. And because the original is true only if its converse is true, due to the impossibility of conjunction between the truth of the original and its contradictory. That is because the separation between antecedent and consequent either actually occurs or not; if it occurs, there is no room for the truth of the original at all; if it does not occur, there is no room for the truth of the contradictory, as previously. (2) Similarly for the perpetual: there is no difference except in the word 'necessity'. (3) For the universally conditional: it is said necessarily whenever A is B, then C is D as long as A is B; then necessarily whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B) as long as not (C is D); otherwise its contradictory would be true, namely 'not whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B) as long as not (C is D)' with absolute generality. So its counter-conversion holds, as previously, leading to impossibility. (4) Similarly for the customarily universal. Then an objector might say in this place: if it were as mentioned, then truth in the original would entail truth in the converse in every one of these forms, regardless of whether the consequent is among that which actually exists or among that which does not exist at all; but it is not so, because it is impossible that the original is true along with the truth of the converse if...
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage introduces a new section (فصل) on counter-conversion (عكس النقيض) in conditional propositions (شرطيات). The author begins by defining the operation: swapping the contradictory of the antecedent with the consequent, and the contradictory of the consequent with the antecedent, while preserving the quality (negation/affirmation). He then states that only universal conditionals can undergo this conversion, and among universals, only four types: necessary, perpetual, universally conditional, and customarily universal. For each, he provides a proof by contradiction: if the original is true, the counter-conversion must be true, otherwise a contradiction arises. The necessary conditional example: 'necessarily whenever A is B, then C is D' converts to 'necessarily whenever not (C is D), then not (A is B)'. The proof shows that if the converse were false, the original would also be false. The perpetual conditional is similar, lacking the word 'necessity'. The universally conditional adds a condition 'as long as A is B', and its counter-conversion holds mutatis mutandis. The customarily universal follows suit. The passage then begins an objection (ثم لقائل) questioning whether the entailment holds in all cases, but is cut off due to the page ending.

Conversion of Universal Negative Modal Propositions by Contraposition
Author states the conditions for converting universal negatives under necessity, perpetuity, and conditional modalities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a universal negative modal proposition can be converted by contraposition, and apply the conversion to necessary, perpetual, conditional, and customary modalities in Islamic logical arguments.

Core Idea
Universal negatives convert by contraposition only under necessity, perpetuity, conditional, or customary modalities.

In classical Islamic logic, conversion ('aks) often means transforming one proposition into another with the same truth value. But for negatives, the conversion is not simple; it requires contraposition (عكس النقيض). The author explains that only universal negatives with specific modal modifiers—necessary, perpetual, generally conditional, or generally customary—can be converted in this way. This lesson walks through the proof and conditions for each type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general condition for conversion
The author states that universal negatives (like affirmatives) convert only when they are universal and have one of four modalities: necessary, perpetual, generally conditional, or generally customary.
This rule applies to both affirmative and negative universal propositions.
2
Examine the proof for necessary universals
For a necessary universal negative (e.g., 'Necessarily, whenever A is B then C is D'), the contrapositive ('Necessarily, whenever C is not D then A is not B') is also necessary. The author proves this by contradiction: if the contrapositive were false, its contradictory would be true, leading to a contradiction with the original.
The proof relies on the impossibility of both the original and its contradictory being true.
3
Extend to perpetual universals
The perpetual (da'ima) case is identical to the necessary one, except the word 'necessity' is absent; the logical relation holds at all times but without essential necessity.
Perpetuity here means 'at all times' without the connotation of essential necessity.
4
Examine the proof for generally conditional universals
For a conditional universal (e.g., 'Necessarily, whenever A is B then C is D, as long as A is B'), the contrapositive is 'Necessarily, whenever C is not D then A is not B, as long as C is not D'. The proof mirrors the necessary case but with the added condition.
The condition 'as long as' restricts the scope of the implication.
5
Extend to generally customary universals
The generally customary ('uruiyya) case follows the same pattern as the conditional, but the condition is based on custom or general practice rather than logical necessity.
Customary universals are common in fiqh reasoning based on prevailing practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي السوالب كالموجبات أيضًا كما مر . فالموجبات إذن لا تكون منعكسة إلا وأن تكون كلية، والكلية لا تكون منعكسة كنفسها إلا وأن تكون ضرورية أو دائمة أو مشروطة عامة أو عرفية عامة. [١] أما الضرورية فيقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان أب فـ ج د فبالضرورة كلَّما لم يكن ج د لم يكن أب وإلا صدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس كلَّما لم يكن ج د لم يكن أب. فينعكس عكس النقيض وهو قولنا ليس كلَّما كان أب فـ ج د وقد كان بالضرورة كلَّما كان أب فـ ج د هذا خلف. ولأن الأصل لا يصدق إلا وأن يصدق عكسه لاستحالة الاجتماع بين صدقي الأصل والنقيض. وذلك لأن الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي لا يخلو من أن يكون متحققا في الجملة أو لا يكون، فإن كان متحققا فلا مجال لصدق الأصل أصلا، وإن لم يكن متحققا فلا مجال لصدق النقيض كما مر؛ [٢] وعلى هذا في الدائمة فإنه لا فرق بينهما إلا في لفظ الضرورة. [٣] وأما المشروطة العامة فيقال بالضرورة كلَّما كان أب فـ جد ما دام أب فبالضرورة كلما لم يكن ج د لم يكن أب ما دام لم يكن ج د وإلا لصدق نقيضه وهو قولنا ليس كلما لم يكن ج د لم يكن أب ما دام لم يكن ج د بالإطلاق العام. فينعكس عكس النقيض كما مر وإنه يفضي إلى المحال؛ [٤] وعلى هذا في العرفية العامة.
And with respect to the negatives, [the rule is] like the affirmatives as was mentioned. So the affirmatives then are not converted unless they are universal, and the universal is not converted as itself unless it is necessary or perpetual or generally conditional or generally customary. [1] As for the necessary: It is said with necessity: whenever A is B then C is D, then with necessity: whenever C is not D then A is not B; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: It is not the case that whenever C is not D then A is not B. Then it is converted by contraposition, which is our saying: It is not the case that whenever A is B then C is D. But it had been with necessity: whenever A is B then C is D. This is a contradiction. And because the original is true only if its converse is true, due to the impossibility of both the original and its contradictory being true. That is because the separation between the antecedent and consequent is either existent in some way or not; if it is existent, then there is no room for the truth of the original at all; if it is not existent, then there is no room for the truth of the contradictory as was mentioned. [2] And similarly for the perpetual: there is no difference between them except in the word 'necessity'. [3] As for the generally conditional: It is said with necessity: whenever A is B then C is D, as long as A is B, then with necessity: whenever C is not D, A is not B, as long as C is not D; otherwise its contradictory would be true, which is our saying: It is not the case that whenever C is not D then A is not B, as long as C is not D, with absolute generality. Then it is converted by contraposition as mentioned, and it leads to an impossibility. [4] And similarly for the generally customary.
Source pages p. 506

Commentary
This passage elaborates the conditions under which a universal negative proposition can be converted into another universal negative proposition (i.e., 'aks al-naqid or contraposition). The author states that the rules for negatives follow the same pattern as affirmatives previously discussed. For a universal affirmative to convert, it must be a universal proposition with a modality of necessity, perpetuity, general condition, or general custom. The same holds for universal negatives. The author then provides four examples: (1) Necessary: If 'whenever A is B, C is D' is necessarily true, then its contrapositive 'whenever C is not D, A is not B' is also necessarily true. The proof is by contradiction: if the contrapositive were false, its contradictory would be true, which leads to a contradiction with the original. (2) Perpetual: The rule is identical to the necessary, except the term 'necessity' is replaced by 'perpetuity'. (3) Generally conditional: The conditional 'whenever A is B, C is D' is qualified by 'as long as A is B'. Its contrapositive 'whenever C is not D, A is not B' is also conditional on 'as long as C is not D'. The proof is similar. (4) Generally customary: The same rule applies. The commentary emphasizes that the modality must be inherent in the proposition; without it, the conversion does not hold universally. For example, in fiqh: 'Every intoxicant (A) is forbidden (B)' is a universal affirmative without modality? Actually, it is absolute. But if it were 'Every intoxicant is necessarily forbidden', the contrapositive 'Whatever is not forbidden is not an intoxicant' would also be necessary. This illustrates the logical precision required in modal logic.

Key Terms
ضرورية
دائمة
مشروطة عامة
عرفية عامة
عكس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Universal negatives in Islamic logic convert by contraposition only when they carry a modal qualification of necessity, perpetuity, condition, or custom.

?
