Refuting Three Objections to Universal Conversion of Conditionals
Author refutes three objections about universal conversion of conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three distinct objections against the claim that the truth of a conditional proposition necessitates the truth of its converse universally, and explain how the author refutes each by distinguishing simple from composite necessitation and using third-figure syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
The author defends universal conversion by showing objections confuse simple and composite necessity and fail to produce a genuine counterexample.

In this passage, the author engages in a dialectical exchange, presenting three objections to the logical principle that if a conditional proposition is true, its converse is also true. Each objection is carefully dissected and refuted. By following the argument, you will learn how to handle sophisticated objections in logical reasoning using conceptual distinctions and syllogistic analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three objections
Read the opening part where the author lists objections labeled [ب] and [ت]. What is the core claim of each?
The first objection concerns the non-existence of the antecedent; the second and third use third-figure syllogisms.
2
Analyze the first objection and refutation
The first objection argues that non-existence of antecedent cannot be a concomitant of necessity. The author distinguishes between simple necessity and the composite 'necessity without consequent'. How does this distinction resolve the objection?
The non-existence of the antecedent is a concomitant of the composite, not of simple necessity alone.
3
Analyze the second objection and refutation
The second objection uses the third figure to show that truth of original entails truth of contradictory of converse. The author responds by noting that the middle term can be composite and provides a decisive proof that truth of original without truth of converse is impossible.
The proof considers whether truth of original alone is possible or not.
4
Analyze the third objection and refutation
The third objection is similar but with a different contradictory. The author shows it is covered by the same proof and adds an alternative argument.
The alternative argument uses the impossibility of antecedent without consequent.
5
Synthesize the overall strategy
The author uses a combination of conceptual distinction and syllogistic proof to refute all three objections. What general lesson does this teach about defending logical principles?
Careful analysis of the logical components is essential.

التالي من جملة ما لا يتحقق وجوده في نفس الأمر. وذلك لأن ارتفاع المقدم من لوازم اللزوم فيها فيكون ارتفاع اللزوم من لوازم تحققه كذلك. ولو كان كذلك فلا مجال للصدق في الأصل بلزومه وفي العكس وإلا لا يكون ارتفاع المقدم من لوازم لزوم التالي في مثل هذه الصورة؛ وذلك محال. [ب] أو يقول صدق الأصل لا يمكن أن يكون مستلزما لصدق العكس استلزاما كليا. وذلك لأن صدقه مع صدق النقيض أعنى نقيض العكس كلَّما كان متحققا كان صدقه متحققا بالضرورة. وكذلك كلّما كان متحققا كان صدق النقيض متحققا بالضرورة؛ وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون صدق الأصل مما يستلزم صدق النقيض في الجملة من الشكل الثالث. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون صدق الأصل مستلزما لصدق العكس استلزاما كليًّا؛ وإنه من اللوازم للانعكاس فيما ذكرتم. [ت] أو يقول إنه لا يكون مستلزما لذلك استلزاما كليا فإن صدقه مع صدق ما ينافي الصدق في العكس كلَّما كان متحققا كان صدقه متحققا. وكذلك كلّما كان متحققا كان صدق ما ينافي الصدق في العكس متحققا فيكون الصدق في الأصل مستلزما لصدق ما ينافي الصدق في العكس من الثالث كما مر ؛ وإنه مما ينا في كونه مستلزما على ما عرف.
لكنا نقول: في الأول لا نسلم أن ارتفاع المقدم من لوازم لزوم التالي في تلك الصورة بل من لوازم هذا المجموع وهو اللزوم بدون التالي. وقد علمت بأنه لا يلزم من كون الشيء ملزوما للشيء أن يكون جزؤه ملزوما. ولما كان ارتفاعه من لوازم المجموع كان ارتفاع المجموع من لوازم تحققه؛ وهذا هو المطلوب ليس إلا. وإن قيل هب» أنه كذلك لكن لا يلزم أن يكون ارتفاع المجموع عند تحققه بارتفاع ما دون اللزوم؛ وإنه مما ينافي اللزوم أيضًا. فنقول بل يلزم ضرورة انتفاء هذا المجموع في نفس الأمر وهو تحقق المقدم دون اللزوم لما مر . وفي الثاني نقول ما ذكرتم في حيز التعارض فإن من الممكن أن يكون الأوسط في الثالث مجموع صدق الأصل مع صدق ما يتحقق اللزوم لكنا لا نتعرض لذلك ؛ وقد كان من البراهين القاطعة على انتفاء هذا المجموع وهو صدق الأصل دون صدق العكس كما يقال صدق الأصل لا غير إما أن يكون ممكنا في نفس الأمر أو لا يكون دائما. وإنما كان ينبغي هذا المجموع وهو الصدق في الأصل دون الصدق في العكس أما إذا كان ممكنا فلانتفاء ١٤٧ ] صدق الأصل / ؛ إذ الإمكان مما ينافي اللزوم. وأما إذا لم يكن فلانتفاء الغير وهو لا غير وانتفاء المجموع مما يحقق استلزام صدق الأصل صدق العكس استلزاما كليا وهو المطلوب. وبهذا خرج الجواب عن الثالث أيضًا؛ إذ الثالث في حيز التعارض لما مرَّ ؛ وقد تعارض بهذا البرهان كذلك فإنه من جملة ما لا اختصاص له بالتالي؛ وهذا ظاهر . أو نقول إذا كان الأصل صادقًا كان وجود المقدم بدون التالي ممتنعا؛ إذ صدقه عبارة عن هذا الامتناع. ولما كان المقدم دون التالي ممتنعا كان نقيضه من لوازم نقيض التالي بالضرورة ضرورة امتناع نقيض التالي بدونه وإلا لما تحقق ذلك الامتناع ؛ وقد تحقق بالضرورة؛ إذ الكلام فيه. وإن قيل إنما يكون كذلك إذا كان ذلك النقيض أمرًا ممكنا فنقول هذا المنع ضايع فإنا إذا فرضنا ذلك النقيض كان فرضه فرض نقيض التالي لا محالة ممتنعًا كان ذلك النقيض في نفس الأمر أو لا يكون. ولما كان فرضه فرض نقيض التالي كان ذلك الفرض مستلزما لنقيض المقدم، وإن كان الأمر الذي يوصف بصفة كونه مقدَّما قطعي الثبوت على ذلك التقدير؛ وهذا من جملة ما لا خفاء فيه بعد الاطلاع على حقيقة كون الشيء مقدما لتاليه.
وأما البواقي من القضايا المذكورة [٥] فالمشروطة الخاصة تنعكس إلى عامتها عند الجمهور منهم. ومنهم من قال إنها تنعكس دائما في البعض لا
The consequent is among things whose existence is not realized in reality. That is because the non-existence of the antecedent is among the necessary concomitants of the necessity of the consequent in it, so the non-existence of the necessity is among the necessary concomitants of its realization likewise. And if it were so, there is no room for truth of the original with its necessity and in the converse; otherwise the non-existence of the antecedent would not be among the necessary concomitants of the necessity of the consequent in such a form; and that is impossible. [B] Or he says: The truth of the original cannot entail the truth of the converse universally. That is because its truth together with the truth of the contradictory, i.e. the contradictory of the converse, whenever it is realized, its truth is realized necessarily. And likewise, whenever it is realized, the truth of the contradictory is realized necessarily; then it follows that the truth of the original entails the truth of the contradictory in general in the third figure. And if it were so, then it is impossible for the truth of the original to entail the truth of the converse universally; and that is among the necessary concomitants of conversion according to what you have mentioned. [T] Or he says: It does not entail that universally, for its truth together with the truth of what contradicts truth in the converse, whenever it is realized, its truth is realized. And likewise, whenever it is realized, the truth of what contradicts truth in the converse is realized; so the truth in the original entails the truth of what contradicts truth in the converse in the third figure as passed; and that contradicts its being entailing according to what is known.
But we say: Regarding the first, we do not accept that the non-existence of the antecedent is among the necessary concomitants of the necessity of the consequent in that form, but rather among the necessary concomitants of this composite, which is necessity without the consequent. And you know that from something being necessary for another, it does not follow that its part is necessary. And since its non-existence is among the necessary concomitants of the composite, the non-existence of the composite is among the necessary concomitants of its realization; and this is the desired result and nothing else. And if it is said: 'Suppose it is so, but it does not follow that the non-existence of the composite upon its realization is by the non-existence of something less than necessity; and that contradicts necessity as well.' We say: Rather, it necessarily follows the non-existence of this composite in reality, i.e. the realization of the antecedent without necessity, as passed. And regarding the second, we say: What you mentioned is in the realm of contradiction, for it is possible that the middle term in the third figure is the composite of the truth of the original with the truth of what realizes necessity, but we do not engage with that; and it is among the decisive proofs for the non-existence of this composite, i.e. truth of the original without truth of the converse, just as it is said: The truth of the original alone either is possible in reality or is not always. And this composite should have been, i.e. truth in the original without truth in the converse, if possible, then for the non-existence [of truth of the original]? For possibility contradicts necessity. And if not, then for the non-existence of the other, i.e. alone; and the non-existence of the composite realizes the entailment of the truth of the original for the truth of the converse universally, which is the desired result. And by this, the answer to the third also emerges; for the third is in the realm of contradiction as passed; and it is contradicted by this proof as well, for it is among things that have no special connection to the consequent; and this is apparent. Or we say: If the original is true, then the existence of the antecedent without the consequent is impossible; for its truth is an expression of this impossibility. And since the antecedent without the consequent is impossible, its contradictory is among the necessary concomitants of the contradictory of the consequent necessarily, due to the necessity of the impossibility of the contradictory of the consequent without it; otherwise that impossibility would not be realized; and it has been realized necessarily, for the discussion is about it. And if it is said: It is so only if that contradictory is a possible thing. We say: This rejection is futile, for if we assume that contradictory, its assumption is the assumption of the contradictory of the consequent inevitably, whether that contradictory is possible in reality or not. And since its assumption is the assumption of the contradictory of the consequent, that assumption entails the contradictory of the antecedent, even if the thing described by the quality of being antecedent is of certain existence on that assumption; and this is among things about which there is no obscurity after examining the reality of something being antecedent to its consequent.
As for the remaining of the propositions mentioned [5], the special conditionals convert to their universal according to the majority of them. And some of them said they convert always in some...
Source pages p. 507

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of three objections against the claim that the truth of an original proposition cannot universally necessitate the truth of its converse. The author first presents three objections (labeled 'ب', 'ت'? Actually the text has [ب] and [ت] indicating different objectors). The first objection argues that if the consequent is a necessary consequence, the non-existence of the antecedent would be a necessary concomitant of that necessity, leading to absurdity. The second objection uses the third figure syllogism to show that if the original is true, its contradictory would also be true, so universal entailment fails. The third objection similarly uses the third figure to argue that the original's truth would entail the opposite of the converse.

Then the author responds: For the first, he distinguishes between necessity alone and the composite 'necessity without the consequent'. The non-existence of the antecedent is a concomitant of the composite, not of necessity alone. The non-existence of the composite follows from the realization of the antecedent without necessity, which is the desired result. He addresses a counter-response about non-existence of something less than necessity.

For the second objection, he says it leads to contradiction because the middle term could be composite, but the decisive proof is that the truth of the original alone either is possible or not; in both cases, the composite (truth of original without converse) is false, showing that the original's truth does entail the converse universally.

For the third, he says it is covered by the same proof, and adds an alternative argument: if the original is true, the antecedent without the consequent is impossible, which implies that the contradictory of the antecedent is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory of the consequent. He refutes an objection about possibility.

Finally, he briefly mentions that special conditional propositions (المشروطة الخاصة) convert to their universal according to most scholars, with some disagreement on whether they convert always in some cases.

Key Terms
المقدم
التالي
اللزوم
العكس
المشروطة الخاصة

Key Takeaway
The refutation of objections to universal conversion relies on clarifying the logical composite involved and using decisive proofs from the third figure.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take a conditional proposition from a kalam argument, such as 'If the world is created, then it has a cause.' Apply the first objection's reasoning to see if it holds, then use the author's distinction to refute it.
?

Inversion of the Special Conditional
Author explains inversion of special conditional and scholarly divergence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a special conditional and construct its contrapositive according to the majority view.

Core Idea
The special conditional inverts to the general conditional, but the inversion may only hold in some cases.

In logic, not all conditional statements invert the same way. The special conditional, which holds for the duration of its antecedent, follows a distinct rule of inversion. This lesson unpacks that rule and its scholarly disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the special conditional
Look for a conditional that explicitly restricts the antecedent to a duration, e.g., 'as long as A is B, then C is D'.
The temporal restriction is key.
2
Formulate the original
Write the conditional in the form: 'Necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D, not always but as long as A is B.'
This is a mashrūṭa khāṣṣa.
3
Apply contraposition
Swap and negate the antecedent and consequent to get: 'Necessarily, whenever D is not, then A is not, as long as C is not D.'
The quantifier 'as long as' carries over to the negative.
4
Check the result's modality
According to the majority, this inversion holds as a particular, not a universal — it is true in some cases but not always.
Some scholars deny it always inverts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما البواقي من القضايا المذكورة [٥] فالمشروطة الخاصة تنعكس إلى عامتها عند الجمهور منهم. ومنهم من قال إنها تنعكس دائما في البعض لا دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب» فـ «ج د» لا دائما بل ما دام «أب» فبالضرورة كلما لم يكن «د» لم يكن «أب» ما دام لم يكن «ج د» دائما في البعض لا دائما في البعض. [٦] وكذلك العرفية الخاصة وما عداهما من القضايا الباقية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية؛ فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب» كان «ج د» لا دائما بل وقت كذا. ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلما لم يكن ج د لم يكن «أب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا؛ فإن من التوالي ما لا يمكن رفعه دون رفع المقدم في غير ذلك الوقت أصلا. [۷] وعلى هذا في المنتشرة وغيرها التي هي الأعم منها؛ فالأعم على ما عرف.
As for the remaining propositions mentioned [5], the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa) inverts to its general according to the majority of them. And some of them said that it inverts always in some cases, not always in some cases, as is said: necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D, not always but as long as A is B, then necessarily, whenever D is not, A is not, as long as C is not D, always in some cases, not always in some cases. [6] And similarly the special conventional (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa) and what remains of the other propositions do not invert by contrapositive, due to the impossibility of inversion in the more specific of them, which is the temporal (al-waqtiyya). For it is true to say: necessarily, whenever A is B, C is D, not always but at such a time. But it is not true to say: necessarily, whenever C is not D, A is not B, not always but at such a time; for there are consequents that cannot be removed without removing the antecedent in any other time at all. [7] And similarly for the pervasive (al-muntashira) and others that are more general; the more general is as has been known.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage classifies the inversion (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) of conditional propositions. The author begins by discussing the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa), which, according to the majority, inverts to its general (i.e., the universal conditional). Some scholars hold that it inverts only in some cases. The example illustrates: 'necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D, not always but as long as A is B' — this special conditional inverts to 'necessarily, whenever D is not, A is not, as long as C is not D' (often a particular statement). Next, the author states that the special conventional (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa) and the remaining propositions do not invert by contrapositive. The reason is the impossibility of inversion in the more specific type, the temporal conditional (al-waqtiyya). An example is given: 'necessarily, whenever A is B, C is D, not always but at such a time' — the contrapositive would be 'necessarily, whenever C is not D, A is not B, not always but at such a time', but this is false because there are consequents that cannot be repudiated without repudiating the antecedent outside that specific time. Finally, the author extends this ruling to the pervasive (al-muntashira) and other more general conditionals, where the more general is as previously known.

Key Terms
مشروطة خاصة (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa): A special conditional: a conditional that holds as long as the antecedent holds, e.g., 'as long as A is B, then C is D'.
عكس النقيض (ʿaks al-naqīḍ): Contrapositive inversion: swapping and negating antecedent and consequent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of logicians discussed in the lesson, how does the special conditional (mashrūṭa khāṣṣa) invert?
A) It inverts to a universal conditional.
B) It inverts to a particular conditional.
C) It does not invert.
D) It inverts to a temporal conditional.

Key Takeaway
The special conditional, when inverted, yields a universal conditional according to most logicians, but this inversion is not universally accepted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the fiqh conditional: 'Whenever a woman is menstruating, she does not perform prayer; not always but as long as she is menstruating.' Construct its contrapositive and state whether it is valid according to this lesson's rule.

Why Temporal and Conventional Conditionals Do Not Invert
Author explains why temporal and conventional conditionals do not invert, with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a temporal conditional and explain why its contrapositive fails.

Core Idea
Temporal conditionals do not invert because the time restriction makes the contrapositive false.

Not all conditionals survive the inversion process. The temporal conditional, which holds only at a specific time, is a prime example of a conditional whose contrapositive is invalid. This lesson examines why.

Guided Path
1
Recognize a temporal conditional
Look for a time-specific restriction like 'at such a time' or 'during Ramadan', e.g., 'When the sun is at zenith, shadows are minimal; not always but at noon.'
This differs from a special conditional which has a continuous duration.
2
Formulate the original temporal conditional
Write: 'Necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D, not always but at time T.'
T is a specific moment.
3
Attempt contraposition
Try to write: 'Necessarily, whenever D is not, then A is not, not always but at time T.'
This is the contrapositive form.
4
Test with a counterexample
Consider a scenario where D is not at time T, but A is B at another time. Does that force A not to be B at time T? Not necessarily, because the original only guarantees the connection at time T. The consequent might be false at other times without affecting the antecedent at time T.
Thus the contrapositive is false.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٦] وكذلك العرفية الخاصة وما عداهما من القضايا الباقية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية؛ فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب» كان «ج د» لا دائما بل وقت كذا. ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلما لم يكن ج د لم يكن «أب» لا دائما بل وقت كذا؛ فإن من التوالي ما لا يمكن رفعه دون رفع المقدم في غير ذلك الوقت أصلا. [7] And similarly for the pervasive (al-muntashira) and others that are more general; the more general is as has been known.

Commentary
This passage classifies the inversion (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) of conditional propositions. The author begins by discussing the special conditional (al-mashrūṭa al-khāṣṣa), which, according to the majority, inverts to its general (i.e., the universal conditional). Some scholars hold that it inverts only in some cases. The example illustrates: 'necessarily, whenever A is B, then C is D, not always but as long as A is B' — this special conditional inverts to 'necessarily, whenever D is not, A is not, as long as C is not D' (often a particular statement). Next, the author states that the special conventional (al-ʿurfiyya al-khāṣṣa) and the remaining propositions do not invert by contrapositive. The reason is the impossibility of inversion in the more specific type, the temporal conditional (al-waqtiyya). An example is given: 'necessarily, whenever A is B, C is D, not always but at such a time' — the contrapositive would be 'necessarily, whenever C is not D, A is not B, not always but at such a time', but this is false because there are consequents that cannot be repudiated without repudiating the antecedent outside that specific time. Finally, the author extends this ruling to the pervasive (al-muntashira) and other more general conditionals, where the more general is as previously known.

Key Terms
وقتية (waqtiyya): A temporal conditional: a conditional that is true only at a specific time, e.g., 'at such a time'.
عرفية خاصة (ʿurfiyya khāṣṣa): A special conventional conditional: a conditional that is true according to convention or customary usage, i.e., it holds in most cases but not necessarily always.
منتشرة (muntashira): A pervasive conditional: a conditional that is true for a certain spread or distribution (e.g., 'most of the time').

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Temporal and conventional conditionals resist contraposition because their truth is tied to a particular time or custom, which the contrapositive fails to preserve.

Particular Affirmative Conditionals Do Not Invert
Argument that particular affirmative conditionals do not invert with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a particular affirmative conditional, attempt its contrapositive, and explain why the inversion fails using the author's example and response.

Core Idea
Not every conditional is contrapositive-friendly: particular affirmatives can fail the inversion test.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle point in the logic of conditionals: while universal conditionals (especially luzūmiyya) typically allow contraposition, particular affirmative conditionals do not. He provides a concrete example and then defends his position against a plausible objection. You will follow his reasoning step by step and learn to apply the same logical test.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim
The author asserts: 'As for the particular affirmative, it does not invert.' This means that if you have a conditional like 'Sometimes if A then B', you cannot validly infer 'Sometimes if not B then not A'.
Inversion here refers to contraposition (transposition) of a conditional proposition.
2
Examine the example
The example: 'Sometimes, if animal exists, then non-human exists.' This is a particular affirmative conditional: it asserts that in some cases, when animal exists, something that is not human also exists. Note that 'non-human' is a broad category including animals, plants, etc.
The example is chosen because man is a counterexample to the contrapositive.
3
Test the contrapositive
The proposed contrapositive: 'Sometimes, if non-human does not exist, then animal does not exist.' But the author points out that this is false: if non-human does not exist, that means everything that exists is human. Since man is an animal, animal still exists. So the contrapositive fails.
The failure hinges on the fact that man is a subset of animal; the original conditional is true in some cases (e.g., when a horse exists), but the contrapositive would require that every time the consequent (non-human) is absent, the antecedent (animal) is also absent, which is false in the case of man.
4
Consider the objection
An objector argues: In a conditional of implication (luzūmiyya), if the antecedent cannot hold without the consequent, then the removal of the consequent must entail the removal of the antecedent. Therefore the contrapositive should hold.
This objection is based on the standard property of strict implication: (A → B) is equivalent to (¬B → ¬A).
5
Understand the author's response
The author concedes the logical point but notes that the original particular conditional is not asserted as a strict implication in all cases; it only claims there is some state where A and B are inseparable. The contrapositive would only hold in that same state, but the original proposition 'Sometimes if A then B' is not restricted to that state. Therefore, from the truth of the particular affirmative, the truth of the particular contrapositive does not follow generally.
In other words, the particular quantifier 'sometimes' prevents the contrapositive from being universally valid; it may be true in some specific state but not as a general particular proposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس لما مر من قبل في السالبة الجزئية فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان الحيوان موجودا كان اللا إنسان موجودا. ولا يقال قد يكون إذا لم يكن اللا إنسان موجودًا لم يكن الحيوان موجودا. فإن من المحال أن يكون الإنسان موجودا دون الحيوان وإنه حيوان. ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه كيف لا يقال وكما أن وضع المقدم لا يمكن دون وضع التالي في اللزومية فكذلك رفع التالي لا يمكن دون رفع المقدم ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يصدق كل واحد من القولين؛ ولأن الكلام في اللزومية ومثل هذه الشرطية لا تكون لزومية إلا وأن لا يمكن الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي في حالة ما وفي تلك الحالة كما يصدق الأول من القولين يصدق الثاني منهما، وإن كان الإنسان في تلك الحالة محالًا، واللا حيوان عند تحققه كذلك. لكنا نقول: هذه كلها من المسلمات لكن لا يلزم من صدق الأول منهما صدق الثاني إلا في تلك الحالة والأول لا يكون مقيدا بقيد أن يكون في تلك الحالة فلا يلزم من صدقه صدق الثاني إذن. والحق فيه أنه يلزم
As for the particular affirmative, it does not invert, because of what has previously passed regarding the particular negative. So it is said, for example: Sometimes, if animal exists, then non-human exists. But it is not said: Sometimes, if non-human does not exist, then animal does not exist. For it is impossible that man exists without animal, and he is an animal. Then an objector may say: How is it not said? And just as the positing of the antecedent cannot be without the positing of the consequent in a conditional, likewise the lifting of the consequent cannot be without the lifting of the antecedent. And if it were so, it would be among the necessities that each of the two statements is true. And because the discussion is about the conditional, and such a conditional is not conditional unless separation between antecedent and consequent is impossible in some state, and in that state, just as the first of the two statements is true, the second of them is true, even if man is in that state impossible, and non-animal when it exists is likewise. But we say: These are all accepted premises, but it does not follow from the truth of the first of them the truth of the second except in that state, and the first is not restricted by the restriction of being in that state, so it does not follow from its truth the truth of the second then. And the truth of the matter is that it does follow.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage addresses the particular affirmative conditional (al-mūjiba al-juzʾiyya) and argues that it does not invert. The example given: 'Sometimes, if animal exists, then non-human exists' — this is a particular affirmative conditional. The contrapositive would be 'Sometimes, if non-human does not exist, then animal does not exist', but this is false because man is a type of animal; man exists only if animal exists, so the contrapositive fails. An objector then raises a point: in a conditional (luzūmiyya), if the antecedent cannot be true without the consequent, then lifting the consequent should require lifting the antecedent. The author's response is that the first statement (the original) is not qualified by a specific state, while the second (the contrapositive) only holds in that state where separation is impossible. Since the original is true in a general sense (sometimes), its truth does not entail the truth of the contrapositive in a corresponding general sense. The passage concludes that the truth is that it does follow (perhaps meaning that in that specific state it follows, but not universally).

لزومية
وضع المقدم
رفع التالي

Key Takeaway
Particular affirmative conditionals do not support contrapositive inference because the particular quantifier allows the original to be true in cases where the contrapositive would be false.

Contraposition of Universal Affirmative Conditionals
Author explains which universal affirmative conditionals have valid contraposition and which do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which universal affirmative conditional propositions have valid contraposition and which do not, and explain the reason based on temporal restriction.

Core Idea
Not all conditionals contraposit: temporal restrictions break the inference.

The author discusses contraposition (aks al-naqid) for universal affirmative conditionals. He explains that while some conditionals allow contraposition, others like temporal conditionals do not. Understanding this limitation is crucial to avoid fallacious inferences in logical arguments, especially those used in Islamic sciences like kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand contraposition of conditionals
Contraposition for a conditional 'if P then Q' yields 'if not Q then not P'. It is valid only if the implication is necessary and unrestricted. For universal affirmative conditionals, it is usually valid unless restricted.
This is the standard immediate inference.
2
Identify the two types mentioned in the passage
Locate the phrases 'دائما في البعض' (always in some) and 'الوقتية' (temporal) in the Arabic. The first refers to a limited universality, the second to a time-bound conditional.
The text uses variables 'أب' and 'ج د' for propositions.
3
Determine why temporal conditionals fail contraposition
For a temporal conditional like 'whenever A then B, but only at time T', the contrapositive 'whenever not B then not A' may not hold because B might be false outside T without A being false. The author says the consequent cannot be removed without the antecedent at other times.
This is the key logical point.
4
Generalize to other conditionals
The passage concludes that the more general conditionals (such as distributed ones) also do not contraposit. The principle is that any restriction (temporal, customary) that limits the universality of the implication invalidates contraposition.
The author says 'والأعم على ما عرف' (the more general is as known).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دائما في البعض كما يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب» فــ «ج د» لا دائما بل ما دام «أب فبالضرورة كلما لم يكن ( د ) لم يكن «أب» ما دام لم يكن «ج د» دائما في البعض لا دائما في البعض» . [٦] وكذلك العرفية الخاصة وما عداهما من القضايا الباقية فإنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض لامتناع العكس في الأخص منها وهي الوقتية؛ فإنه يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلما كان «أب» كان «ج د» لا دائما بل وقت كذا. ولا يصدق أن يقال بالضرورة كلَّما لم يكن ج د لم يكن «أب» لا دائمًا بل وقت كذا؛ فإن من التوالي ما لا يمكن رفعه دون رفع المقدم في غير ذلك الوقت أصلا. [۷] وعلى هذا في المنتشرة وغيرها التي هي الأعم منها؛ فالأعم على ما عرف.
Always in some, as it is said by necessity whenever 'ab' then 'jd' not always but as long as 'ab' then by necessity whenever not 'd', 'ab' is not the case as long as 'jd' is not the case, always in some, not always in some. [6] And likewise the special 'urfiya and the remaining propositions besides these two, they do not contraposit because contraposition is impossible in the more specific, namely the temporal; for it is true to say by necessity whenever 'ab' then 'jd' not always but in such a time. And it is not true to say by necessity whenever 'jd' is not the case, 'ab' is not the case, not always but in such a time; for some consequents cannot be removed without removing the antecedent at other times at all. [7] And likewise for the distributed and others which are more general; the more general is as known.
Source pages p. 509

Commentary
This passage discusses which universal affirmative conditional propositions can undergo contraposition (aks al-naqid). The author identifies two types: the first is a universal affirmative conditional that holds 'always in some' (a limited universality). The second is the special 'urfiya and temporal conditions, which do not allow contraposition because the implication is restricted to a specific time. For example, a statement like 'by necessity whenever A then B, but only at time T' does not imply its contrapositive 'by necessity whenever not-B then not-A' because the consequent may be impossible to remove at other times. The author then generalizes that all more general propositions (such as distributed ones) follow the same pattern: the more general, as known, does not permit contraposition.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض (aks al-naqid)
الوقتية (al-waqtiyya)
العرفية الخاصة (al-'urfiya al-khassa)

Key Takeaway
The validity of contraposition for universal affirmative conditionals depends on the absence of temporal restrictions; if the conditional is time-bound, its contrapositive does not necessarily hold.

Contrapositive of Particular Affirmative Conditionals
Author defends a rule about contrapositive of particular conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a particular affirmative conditional has a valid contrapositive and identify the role of implicative (luzumiyya) conditionals in such inferences.

Core Idea
Particular affirmatives do not contraposit unless the implication is unrestricted.

In this passage, the author tackles a subtle point: do particular affirmative conditionals have valid contrapositives? He explains why they generally do not, using an example and responding to a potential objection. The discussion hinges on whether the conditional is truly implicative (luzumiyya) and whether the truth of the original is restricted to a specific state.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim
The author states that particular affirmative conditionals do not convert (i.e., have a valid contrapositive). Locate this claim in the first sentence.
The term 'convert' here means contrapositive (عكس النقيض).
2
Examine the counterexample
The author gives an example: 'sometimes if animal exists, non-human exists' is true, but its contrapositive 'sometimes if non-human does not exist, animal does not exist' is false. Why is the contrapositive false? Because human exists whenever animal exists (human is a subset of animal).
The example relies on the fact that human is an animal, so non-human absence would require human to exist without animal, which is impossible.
3
Analyze the objector's reasoning
An objector argues that if the conditional is implicative (luzumiyya), the antecedent cannot hold without the consequent, so by contraposition, the consequent's negation should entail the antecedent's negation. Trace this reasoning in the passage.
The objector assumes that any correct implication allows contraposition.
4
Evaluate the author's response
The author concedes the objector's points but notes that the original conditional is not universally true; it holds only in some cases. The contrapositive would also hold only in those same cases, but since the original is not restricted to that state, its truth does not guarantee the contrapositive's truth. The key is that for the contrapositive to be valid, the implication must be unconditional.
The author concludes that if the conditional is genuinely implicative (unrestricted), the contrapositive follows; otherwise it does not.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الموجبة الجزئية فإنها لا تنعكس لما مر من قبل في السالبة الجزئية فيقال مثلا قد يكون إذا كان الحيوان موجودا كان اللا إنسان موجودا. ولا يقال قد يكون إذا لم يكن اللا إنسان موجودًا لم يكن الحيوان موجودا. فإن من المحال أن يكون الإنسان موجودا دون الحيوان وإنه حيوان. ثم لقائل أن يقول فيه كيف لا يقال وكما أن وضع المقدم لا يمكن دون وضع التالي في اللزومية فكذلك رفع التالي لا يمكن دون رفع المقدم ولو كان كذلك لكان من اللوازم أن يصدق كل واحد من القولين؛ ولأن الكلام في اللزومية ومثل هذه الشرطية لا تكون لزومية إلا وأن لا يمكن الافتراق بين المقدم والتالي في حالة ما وفي تلك الحالة كما يصدق الأول من القولين يصدق الثاني منهما، وإن كان الإنسان في تلك الحالة محالًا، واللا حيوان عند تحققه كذلك. لكنا نقول: هذه كلها من المسلمات لكن لا يلزم من صدق الأول منهما صدق الثاني إلا في تلك الحالة والأول لا يكون مقيدا بقيد أن يكون في تلك الحالة فلا يلزم من صدقه صدق الثاني إذن. والحق فيه أنه يلزم إذا كانت الشرطية لزومية وإلا لا تكون لزومية؛ فإن من المحال أن يمتنع هذا دون ذلك ولا يمتنع أيضًا.
As for the particular affirmative, it does not convert due to what was previously mentioned regarding the particular negative. For example, it is said: sometimes if animal exists, non-human exists. But it is not said: sometimes if non-human does not exist, animal does not exist. For it is impossible that human exists without animal, and he is animal. Then an objector may say: why is it not said? Just as the antecedent cannot be posited without positing the consequent in implicative conditionals, so too the consequent cannot be removed without removing the antecedent. If that were the case, it would necessitate that each of the two statements is true; and because the discussion is about implicative conditionals, and such a conditional is not implicative unless the antecedent and consequent cannot separate in some state, and in that state, just as the first of the two statements is true, so too the second of them is true, even if human in that state is impossible, and non-animal when it is realized likewise. But we say: all these are accepted, but the truth of the first does not entail the truth of the second except in that state, and the first is not restricted by the condition that it be in that state, so its truth does not entail the truth of the second. And the truth is that it entails if the conditional is implicative, otherwise it is not implicative; for it is impossible for this to be prevented without that, and also not prevented.

Commentary
This passage deals with the conversion (specifically contraposition) of particular affirmative conditional propositions. The author first states that such conditionals do not convert, using an example: 'Sometimes if animal exists, non-human exists' is true, but its contrapositive 'Sometimes if non-human does not exist, animal does not exist' is false because human always exists with animal. The author responds that even if the conditional is implicative in a specific state, the original statement is not restricted to that state, so the contrapositive does not follow generally. The key insight is that for a true contrapositive, the implication must be unrestricted.

Contraposition of Implicative Conditionals
Author states the universal rule for contraposition of implicative conditionals and distinguishes coincidental conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the contraposition rule to implicative conditionals and distinguish them from coincidental conditionals by checking whether the negation of the consequent entails negation of the antecedent.

Core Idea
An implicative conditional implies its contrapositive; a coincidental one does not.

In this passage, the author lays down the fundamental rule that defines an implicative conditional: it entails a universal conditional that supports contraposition. This rule is essential for understanding immediate inferences in conditional logic. The author also warns against conflating implicative conditionals with coincidental ones, which lack this property, because in coincidental conditionals the antecedent and consequent are not necessarily connected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
In a conditional statement 'if A then B', the antecedent is A (al-malzum) and the consequent is B (al-lazim). In the source, the author uses these terms explicitly.
2
State the two directions of implication
For an implicative conditional, two statements hold: (1) whenever the antecedent exists, the consequent exists; (2) whenever the consequent does not exist, the antecedent does not exist. This is the contrapositive.
The first direction is the original conditional; the second is its contrapositive.
3
Test for coincidental conditionals
Apply the same test to a coincidental conditional (ittifaqiyya). If the conditional is coincidental (e.g., 'If it rains, then the sky is blue' is not a necessary connection), the negation of the consequent does not entail negation of the antecedent.
The author explicitly says that for ittifaqiyya, contraposition fails.
4
Apply to Islamic science examples
Take a real example from usul al-fiqh: 'If an analogy is valid, then the ruling applies.' Its contrapositive: 'If the ruling does not apply, then the analogy is not valid.' Check whether this is true; if the connection is necessary, it holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم بأن الشرطية وإن كانت جزئية فيما نحن فيه فقد كانت مستلزمة الشرطية كلية من جانب المقدم تارة، ومن جانب التالي أخرى فيقال كلما كان الملزوم موجوداً كان اللازم موجودًا، وكلّما لم يكن اللازم موجوداً لم يكن الملزوم موجودًا وإلا لا يكون الملزوم ملزوما في نفس الأمر، ولا اللازم لازما كذلك؛ هذا هو الكلام في اللزومية. وأما في الاتفاقية فالحق فيها أنها لا تنعكس عكس النقيض كلية كانت أو جزئية فإنه لا يلزم من انتفاء التالي فيها انتفاء المقدم على ما عرف.
Know that the conditional, even if particular in our discussion, necessarily implies a universal conditional from the side of the antecedent sometimes, and from the side of the consequent other times. So it is said: whenever the antecedent exists, the consequent exists, and whenever the consequent does not exist, the antecedent does not exist, otherwise the antecedent is not really an antecedent, nor the consequent a consequent. This is the discussion of implicatives. As for coincidentals, the truth is that they do not contraposit, whether universal or particular, for the negation of the consequent does not entail the negation of the antecedent, as is known.
Source pages p. 510

Commentary
This passage clarifies that any implicative conditional (luzumiyya) implies a universal conditional that supports contraposition: if the antecedent exists, the consequent exists, and if the consequent does not exist, the antecedent does not exist. This is the defining property of an implicative conditional. In contrast, coincidental conditionals (ittifaqiyya) do not have a valid contrapositive, because the antecedent and consequent are not necessarily connected; the absence of the consequent does not guarantee the absence of the antecedent.

Key Terms
الملزوم (al-malzum)
اللازم (al-lazim)
الاتفاقية (al-ittifaqiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The antecedent may be present or absent.
C) The conditional becomes false.
D) Nothing can be concluded.

Key Takeaway
The defining property of an implicative conditional is that its contrapositive is valid; coincidental conditionals lack this property.
